Impossibility of Infinite Predication and the Role of Analysis
Author argues against infinite predication and explains the role of analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why predicates cannot be infinite and how analysis (tahlil) prevents infinite regress in demonstrations.

Core Idea
Analysis forces finite predicates, making demonstration possible; without it, no definition or proof exists.

If predicates were infinite, no term could be defined and no demonstration could reach a conclusion. The author argues that analysis (tahlil) breaks down compounds into simples, showing that predicates in a thing are finite. This secures the foundation of logical demonstration within Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem of infinite middle terms
If between two terms there were an infinite number of middle terms, you could never pass from one to the other, making demonstration impossible.
2
Recognize the role of analysis (tahlil)
Analysis reduces a compound to its simple components until a self-evident truth is reached, proving that predicates are finite.
The author says 'analysis necessitates that predicates in a thing are not infinite.'
3
Distinguish from denying demonstration
This argument assumes the existence of demonstration and definition; it does not refute someone who denies them altogether. That denial must be addressed separately.
The logician takes demonstration and definition as postulates.
4
Evaluate the scope of the argument
The author clarifies that analysis only proves finitude of predicates, not that demonstrations cannot increase infinitely overall. That question is deferred.
This is a nuanced point: the increase of demonstrations might go to infinity, but not the predicates within a single thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا انقسمت المقدّمة بالحد الأوسط وكانت موجبة كلية فلا يمكن أن يقع الحد الأوسط خارجا عن الطرفين بل يكون متوسطا بينهما لا محالة، وأما في السالب فقد يقع خارجًا، وقد يقع غير خارج. وأما البيان بالشكل الثالث فذلك لا بالفعل بل بالقوة. وكل حد يقع في الشكل الأول فإنه يقع بين الحدين لكنه في الشكل الثاني يخرج لا من جانب الأصغر، وبالجملة من جانب الكلي. وفي الشكل الثالث يخرج لا من جانب الأكبر، وفي الرابع يخرج من جانبي الأكبر والأصغر. وكذلك إذا أردت أن تصحح السالبة من قياس مقدمة من الأول، أو أردت أن تركب قياسا لإنتاج السالبة في الشكل الثاني فإنه لم يخرج من جانب الأصغر، وفي الثالث لم يخرج من جانب السالب. ويجب أن تعلم أن جملته تنحصر في أنه إذا كان حدان امتنع السلوك من أحدهما إلى الآخر وإن كانت وسائط بلا نهاية. وإن كانت محمولات بالحقيقة غير متناهية لم يكن حد ولا برهان. ولا يلزم شيء من هذا من يُنكر البرهان والحد إلا أن يبين عليه من وجه آخر أن هذا حدا وبرهانا؛ وليس ذلك على المنطقي بل هو موضوع له. وإن المعتمد ما ذكرناه من أمر التحليل لأن التحليل يوجب أن لا تكون المحمولات في الشيء متناهية. ومن جميع هذا لا يبين أن التزايد في البراهين لا يذهب إلى غير النهاية بل التحليل فقط. وأما أن التزايد كيف يذهب إلى غير النهاية فستعرف من بعد.
And when the premise is divided by the middle term and is universal affirmative, it is impossible for the middle term to fall outside the two extremes, but it must be intermediate between them without exception. As for the negative, it may fall outside and may not fall outside. As for the proof in the third figure, that is not actual but potential. Every term that falls in the first figure falls between the two terms, but in the second figure it goes out from the side of the minor, and in summary from the side of the universal. In the third figure it goes out from the side of the major, and in the fourth it goes out from both sides of the major and minor. Likewise, if you want to correct the negative from a syllogism having a premise from the first figure, or if you want to construct a syllogism to produce a negative in the second figure, then it does not go out from the side of the minor, and in the third it does not go out from the side of the negative. You must know that its entirety is confined to the fact that if there are two terms, passage from one to the other is impossible even if there are infinite middle terms. And if there were actually infinite predicates, there would be no term and no demonstration. None of this follows from one who denies demonstration and definition, unless it is shown from another aspect that this is a definition and demonstration; that is not the responsibility of the logician but is a postulate for him. The relied upon is what we have mentioned regarding the matter of analysis, because analysis necessitates that predicates in a thing are not infinite. From all of this it is not proven that the increase in demonstrations does not go to infinity, but only analysis. As for how the increase goes to infinity, you will know later.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage delves into the technicalities of syllogistic figures and the impossibility of infinite predication. The author first states that in a universal affirmative premise, the middle term must lie between the subject and predicate (as in the first figure). However, in a negative premise, the middle term may be outside or inside. The demonstration in the third figure is only potential, not actual. The author then systematically describes the position of the middle term in each of the four figures: in the first figure it lies between the extremes; in the second it goes outside on the side of the minor term (the subject of the conclusion), i.e., it is not between the extremes but has the major term under it; in the third figure it goes outside on the side of the major term (predicate of conclusion); in the fourth figure it goes outside both sides. This is crucial for understanding which figures can produce valid conclusions. Next, the author explains how to correct a negative syllogism: when you want to produce a negative conclusion, in the first figure the middle term does not go out from the side of the minor, in the third it does not go out from the side of the negative (i.e., the major? The text says 'from the side of the negative' which likely means the predicate of the negative premise). Then the author makes a profound epistemological point: if two extremes are given, one cannot pass from one to the other through an infinite number of middle terms—there must be a limit. If predicates were infinite, there would be no definite subject and no demonstration. This argument does not affect someone who denies demonstration and definition altogether; such a person would need separate proof. It is not the logician's job to prove the existence of demonstration and definition; they are taken as given. The method of analysis (tahlil) shows that predicates in a thing are finite. However, the author clarifies that this does not prove that demonstrations cannot increase infinitely; only analysis demonstrates finitude. The question of how the increase tends to infinity is deferred to later discussion.

Key Terms

البرهان
الحد

Checkpoint
What does analysis (tahlil) show about predicates?
A) They are infinite
B) They are finite
C) They are neither finite nor infinite
D) They are potential

Key Takeaway
Analysis ensures predicates are finite, making definition and demonstration possible within a logical system.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Explain how analysis would stop an infinite regress in a chain of definitions: 'human → animal → living body → body → substance → ...' and show that it must end at a simple, self-evident concept.
?
Why does the author say 'that is not the responsibility of the logician'? Relate this to the role of a logician versus a metaphysician in Islamic scholarship.

Classification of Premises and Their Role in Demonstrations
Classification of premises and their role in demonstration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify premises as principles of affirmative or negative demonstrations and identify simple premises.

Core Idea
Premises are the building blocks of demonstration; their quality determines the type of demonstration they serve.

The author opens by distinguishing two kinds of demonstrations—those that produce affirmative conclusions and those that produce negative ones. He then introduces the concept of a simple premise (a premise without a middle term), which can be either affirmative or negative. This foundation is essential for understanding how syllogisms are constructed and evaluated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of demonstrations
Notice that the author explicitly says some premises are principles for affirmative demonstrations, others for negative demonstrations.
This is a basic dichotomy in syllogistic logic.
2
Recognize the quality of a premise
A premise can be either affirmative or negative; this is its quality.
3
Understand simple premises
A simple premise has no middle term. The author compares it to basic musical intervals, but the key point is that it is an indivisible starting point.
The musical analogy (ratio 36:35) is not essential for logic but illustrates the idea of a minimal unit.
4
Connect premises to demonstrations
Affirmative premises serve as principles for affirmative demonstrations; negative premises for negative demonstrations.
This links back to the overall purpose: constructing valid burhan.

بعضها مبادئ البراهين المنتجة للموجبات، وبعضها مبادئ البراهين المنتجة للسالبات فإن المقدمة التي هي غير ذات وسط قد تكون موجبة، وقد تكون سالبة؛ وهذه بسائط المقدمات وأوائلها كما أن أول النقل متناولا أول الأبعاد اللحنية وهو ربع الطنيني الذي ينسب أحدهما إلى الآخر نسبة ست وثلاثين إلى خمس وثلاثين. وذلك لأن هذه تنقسم إلى أبعاد آخر، وتنقسم إليها سائر الأبعاد التي لها الأسماء مثل الذي بالكل، والذي بالخمسة، والذي بالأربعة والطنيني. وإذا انقسمت المقدّمة بالحد الأوسط وكانت موجبة كلية فلا يمكن أن يقع الحد الأوسط خارجا عن الطرفين بل يكون متوسطا بينهما لا محالة، وأما في السالب فقد يقع خارجًا، وقد يقع غير خارج. وأما البيان بالشكل الثالث فذلك لا بالفعل بل بالقوة. وكل حد يقع في الشكل الأول فإنه يقع بين الحدين لكنه في الشكل الثاني يخرج لا من جانب الأصغر، وبالجملة من جانب الكلي. وفي الشكل الثالث يخرج لا من جانب الأكبر، وفي الرابع يخرج من جانبي الأكبر والأصغر. وكذلك إذا أردت أن تصحح السالبة من قياس مقدمة من الأول، أو أردت أن تركب قياسا لإنتاج السالبة في الشكل الثاني فإنه لم يخرج من جانب الأصغر، وفي الثالث لم يخرج من جانب السالب. ويجب أن تعلم أن جملته تنحصر في أنه إذا كان حدان امتنع السلوك من أحدهما إلى الآخر وإن كانت وسائط بلا نهاية. وإن كانت محمولات بالحقيقة غير متناهية لم يكن حد ولا برهان. ولا يلزم شيء من هذا من يُنكر البرهان والحد إلا أن يبين عليه من وجه آخر أن هذا حدا وبرهانا؛ وليس ذلك على المنطقي بل هو موضوع له. وإن المعتمد ما ذكرناه من أمر التحليل لأن التحليل يوجب أن لا تكون المحمولات في الشيء / متناهية. ومن جميع هذا لا يبين أن التزايد في البراهين لا يذهب إلى غير النهاية بل التحليل فقط. وأما أن التزايد كيف يذهب إلى غير النهاية فستعرف من بعد.
Some of them are principles of demonstrations that produce affirmatives, and some are principles of demonstrations that produce negatives. For the premise that has no middle term may be affirmative or negative; these are the simple premises and their beginnings, just as the first movable thing (?) takes the first harmonic intervals, which is the quarter (?) of the double, one of which is related to the other by the ratio 36:35. This is because these divide into other intervals, and the other intervals that have names, such as the whole, the fifth, the fourth, and the double, divide into them. When the premise is divided by the middle term and is affirmative universal, it is impossible for the middle term to fall outside the two extremes; rather it must be between them inevitably. As for the negative, it may fall outside or not outside. As for the demonstration in the third figure, that is not actual but potential. Every term that occurs in the first figure occurs between the two terms, but in the second figure it goes out not from the side of the minor, and generally from the side of the universal. In the third figure it goes out not from the side of the major, and in the fourth it goes out from both sides of the major and the minor. Likewise, if you want to verify the negative from a syllogism with a premise from the first, or if you want to compose a syllogism to produce a negative in the second figure, it does not go out from the side of the minor, and in the third it does not go out from the side of the negative. You must know that the whole is restricted to the fact that if there are two terms, it is impossible to pass from one to the other even if there are infinite intermediate terms. If there are infinite real predicates, there is neither definition nor demonstration. None of this follows for one who denies demonstration and definition unless it is shown in another way that this is definition and demonstration; that is not the logician's responsibility but is posited for him. The relied-upon is what we mentioned concerning analysis, because analysis necessitates that predicates in a thing are not infinite. From all this it is not shown that increase in demonstrations does not go to infinity; rather analysis alone. As for how increase goes to infinity, you will know later.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a technical exposition on the nature of premises and the middle term in syllogisms. The author begins by classifying premises: some serve as principles for affirmative demonstrations, others for negative demonstrations. Simple premises (those without a middle term) can be either affirmative or negative, analogous to basic musical intervals such as the ratio 36:35 which is the smallest interval (quarter-tone). He then discusses the position of the middle term: in an affirmative universal premise, the middle term must lie between the two extremes (subject and predicate). In a negative premise, it may be outside or inside. The author notes that the third figure only potentially demonstrates, not actually. For each figure: in the first figure the middle term lies between the extremes; in the second it goes out from the minor term; in the third from the major; in the fourth from both. When converting negatives from the first figure or constructing negatives in the second, the middle term does not go out from the minor; in the third it does not go out from the negative side. Finally, the author asserts that there cannot be infinite middle terms between two terms; otherwise there would be no definition or demonstration. The logician does not need to prove that a given term is a definition or demonstration; that is a matter of subject matter. Analysis ensures that predicates in a thing are finite, so the regress of demonstrations in analysis stops. The increase in demonstrations (composing multiple syllogisms) may go to infinity, but this is discussed later.

البرهان
المقدمة البسيطة

D) Direct and indirect demonstrations

Key Takeaway
Premises are the raw material of demonstration, and their quality (affirmative or negative) determines the type of demonstration they serve.

Position of the Middle Term in the Four Figures
Position of the middle term in the four figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the position of the middle term in the first, second, third, and fourth figures of categorical syllogisms.

Core Idea
The middle term's position relative to the extremes defines the figure, and each figure has distinct structural rules.

The author provides a concise description of where the middle term lies in each of the four figures. This is a core topic in syllogistic logic, as the figure determines the arrangement of terms and affects the validity of the syllogism. Understanding these positions is essential for constructing and analyzing arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three terms of a syllogism
A categorical syllogism has three terms: major (predicate of the conclusion), minor (subject of the conclusion), and middle (connects the two).
2
Identify the middle term in the first figure
In the first figure, the middle term is the subject of the major premise and the predicate of the minor premise; thus it lies between the extremes.
The author says: 'Every term that occurs in the first figure occurs between the two terms.'
3
Identify the middle term in the second figure
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate of both premises, so it is outside from the minor side.
The text: 'in the second figure it goes out not from the side of the minor, and generally from the side of the universal.'
4
Identify the middle term in the third figure
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises, so it is outside from the major side.
The text: 'In the third figure it goes out not from the side of the major.'
5
Identify the middle term in the fourth figure
In the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate of the major and subject of the minor, so it goes out from both sides.
The text: 'in the fourth it goes out from both sides of the major and the minor.'

Source Text

Some of them are principles of demonstrations that produce affirmatives, and some are principles of demonstrations that produce negatives. For the premise that has no middle term may be affirmative or negative; these are the simple premises and their beginnings, just as the first movable thing (?) takes the first harmonic intervals, which is the quarter (?) of the double, one of which is related to the other by the ratio 36:35. This is because these divide into other intervals, and the other intervals that have names, such as the whole, the fifth, the fourth, and the double, divide into them. When the premise is divided by the middle term and is affirmative universal, it is impossible for the middle term to fall outside the two extremes; rather it must be between them inevitably. As for the negative, it may fall outside or not outside. As for the demonstration in the third figure, that is not actual but potential. Every term that occurs in the first figure occurs between the two terms, but in the second figure it goes out not from the side of the minor, and generally from the side of the universal. In the third figure it goes out not from the side of the major, and in the fourth it goes out from both sides of the major and the minor. Likewise, if you want to verify the negative from a syllogism with a premise from the first, or if you want to compose a syllogism to produce a negative in the second figure, it does not go out from the side of the minor, and in the third it does not go out from the side of the negative. Commentary
This passage is a technical exposition on the nature of premises and the middle term in syllogisms. The author begins by classifying premises: some serve as principles for affirmative demonstrations, others for negative demonstrations. Simple premises (those without a middle term) can be either affirmative or negative, analogous to basic musical intervals such as the ratio 36:35 which is the smallest interval (quarter-tone). In a negative premise, it may be outside or inside. The author notes that the third figure only potentially demonstrates, not actually. The logician does not need to prove that a given term is a definition or demonstration; that is a matter of subject matter. Infinite Middle Terms and the Necessity of Finite Analysis
The impossibility of infinite middle terms and the role of analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why an infinite chain of middle terms would make definition and demonstration impossible, and how analysis ensures finiteness of predicates.

Core Idea
Demonstration requires finite steps; infinite middle terms would negate the possibility of knowledge.

The author concludes the passage with a crucial point: if there were an infinite number of middle terms between two extremes, it would be impossible to connect them, and thus no definition or demonstration could exist. He introduces analysis as the tool that ensures predicates in a thing are finite. This argument addresses foundational issues in epistemology and logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim about two terms
The author asserts: if there are two terms, it is impossible to go from one to the other if there are infinite intermediate terms.
This is because each step would require another step ad infinitum, so no connection could ever be completed.
2
Connect to definition and demonstration
If predicates in a thing were infinite, then no definition (which breaks down a thing into finite predicates) and no demonstration (which proceeds through finite steps) would be possible.
Definition and demonstration require a finite chain of predicates.
3
Recognize the logician's task
The author notes that the logician does not need to prove that something is a definition or demonstration; that is a matter of subject matter. The logician only deals with the form.
This is a meta-logical point: logic assumes some content as given.
4
Identify the role of analysis (tahlil)
Analysis ensures that predicates in a thing are not infinite. It breaks down complex concepts into simpler ones until basic terms are reached.
The author says: 'analysis necessitates that predicates in a thing are not infinite.'
5
Distinguish analysis from composition
The increase in demonstrations (composing multiple syllogisms) may go to infinity, but analysis does not. The author promises to discuss the infinite increase later.

بعضها مبادئ البراهين المنتجة للموجبات، وبعضها مبادئ البراهين المنتجة للسالبات فإن المقدمة التي هي غير ذات وسط قد تكون موجبة، وقد تكون سالبة؛ وهذه بسائط المقدمات وأوائلها كما أن أول النقل متناولا أول الأبعاد اللحنية وهو ربع الطنيني الذي ينسب أحدهما إلى الآخر نسبة ست وثلاثين إلى خمس وثلاثين. وذلك لأن هذه تنقسم إلى أبعاد آخر، وتنقسم إليها سائر الأبعاد التي لها الأسماء مثل الذي بالكل، والذي بالخمسة، والذي بالأربعة والطنيني. وإذا انقسمت المقدّمة بالحد الأوسط وكانت موجبة كلية فلا يمكن أن يقع الحد الأوسط خارجا عن الطرفين بل يكون متوسطا بينهما لا محالة، وأما في السالب فقد يقع خارجًا، وقد يقع غير خارج. وأما البيان بالشكل الثالث فذلك لا بالفعل بل بالقوة. وكل حد يقع في الشكل الأول فإنه يقع بين الحدين لكنه في الشكل الثاني يخرج لا من جانب الأصغر، وبالجملة من جانب الكلي. وفي الشكل الثالث يخرج لا من جانب الأكبر، وفي الرابع يخرج من جانبي الأكبر والأصغر. وكذلك إذا أردت أن تصحح السالبة من قياس مقدمة من الأول، أو أردت أن تركب قياسا لإنتاج السالبة في الشكل الثاني فإنه لم يخرج من جانب الأصغر، وفي الثالث لم يخرج من جانب السالب. ويجب أن تعلم أن جملته تنحصر في أنه إذا كان حدان امتنع السلوك من أحدهما إلى الآخر وإن كانت وسائط بلا نهاية. وإن كانت محمولات بالحقيقة غير متناهية لم يكن حد ولا برهان. ولا يلزم شيء من هذا من يُنكر البرهان والحد إلا أن يبين عليه من وجه آخر أن هذا حدا وبرهانا؛ وليس ذلك على المنطقي بل هو موضوع له. وإن المعتمد ما ذكرناه من أمر التحليل لأن التحليل يوجب أن لا تكون المحمولات في الشيء / متناهية. ومن جميع هذا لا يبين أن التزايد في البراهين لا يذهب إلى غير النهاية بل التحليل فقط. وأما أن التزايد كيف يذهب إلى غير النهاية فستعرف من بعد.
Some of them are principles of demonstrations that produce affirmatives, and some are principles of demonstrations that produce negatives. For the premise that has no middle term may be affirmative or negative; these are the simple premises and their beginnings, just as the first movable thing (?) takes the first harmonic intervals, which is the quarter (?) of the double, one of which is related to the other by the ratio 36:35. This is because these divide into other intervals, and the other intervals that have names, such as the whole, the fifth, the fourth, and the double, divide into them. When the premise is divided by the middle term and is affirmative universal, it is impossible for the middle term to fall outside the two extremes; rather it must be between them inevitably. As for the negative, it may fall outside or not outside. As for the demonstration in the third figure, that is not actual but potential. Every term that occurs in the first figure occurs between the two terms, but in the second figure it goes out not from the side of the minor, and generally from the side of the universal. In the third figure it goes out not from the side of the major, and in the fourth it goes out from both sides of the major and the minor. Likewise, if you want to verify the negative from a syllogism with a premise from the first, or if you want to compose a syllogism to produce a negative in the second figure, it does not go out from the side of the minor, and in the third it does not go out from the side of the negative. You must know that the whole is restricted to the fact that if there are two terms, it is impossible to pass from one to the other even if there are infinite intermediate terms. If there are infinite real predicates, there is neither definition nor demonstration. None of this follows for one who denies demonstration and definition unless it is shown in another way that this is definition and demonstration; that is not the logician's responsibility but is posited for him. The relied-upon is what we mentioned concerning analysis, because analysis necessitates that predicates in a thing are not infinite. From all this it is not shown that increase in demonstrations does not go to infinity; rather analysis alone. As for how increase goes to infinity, you will know later.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a technical exposition on the nature of premises and the middle term in syllogisms. The author begins by classifying premises: some serve as principles for affirmative demonstrations, others for negative demonstrations. Simple premises (those without a middle term) can be either affirmative or negative, analogous to basic musical intervals such as the ratio 36:35 which is the smallest interval (quarter-tone). He then discusses the position of the middle term: in an affirmative universal premise, the middle term must lie between the two extremes (subject and predicate). In a negative premise, it may be outside or inside. The author notes that the third figure only potentially demonstrates, not actually. For each figure: in the first figure the middle term lies between the extremes; in the second it goes out from the minor term; in the third from the major; in the fourth from both. When converting negatives from the first figure or constructing negatives in the second, the middle term does not go out from the minor; in the third it does not go out from the negative side. Finally, the author asserts that there cannot be infinite middle terms between two terms; otherwise there would be no definition or demonstration. The logician does not need to prove that a given term is a definition or demonstration; that is a matter of subject matter. Analysis ensures that predicates in a thing are finite, so the regress of demonstrations in analysis stops. The increase in demonstrations (composing multiple syllogisms) may go to infinity, but this is discussed later.

Key Terms
التحليل
المحمولات غير المتناهية
الحد
البرهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does analysis (tahlil) necessitate?
A) That predicates in a thing are infinite
B) That predicates in a thing are not infinite
C) That demonstrations always increase to infinity
D) That the middle term is always inside the extremes

Key Takeaway
Demonstration requires finite steps; analysis ensures that predicates in a thing are finite, preventing an infinite regress that would make knowledge impossible.

Different Proofs, One Science
Author argues that different demonstrations do not make different sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between demonstrations with hierarchically related middle terms and those without, and explain why different demonstrations do not require different sciences.

Core Idea
Different middle terms do not require different sciences; the subject matter stays the same.

This passage addresses a potential confusion: if there are multiple demonstrations for the same conclusion, does that mean multiple sciences? The author clarifies that demonstrations, even with different middle terms, can belong to the same science. He distinguishes two types: where the middle terms are in a hierarchical relation, and where they are not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of alternative demonstrations
The author describes two cases: (a) two middle terms where one is predicated of the other (subordinate); (b) two middle terms that are not subordinate. Find these in the Arabic.
2
Extract the middle terms for each type
For type (a): first demonstration uses 'animal' as middle term; second uses 'perfect'. Note that 'animal' is not predicated of 'perfect', but the author says one is under the other? Actually the example: 'every human is animal, every animal is nourished' and 'every human is perfect, every perfect is moderate'. The middle terms are 'animal' and 'perfect'. The author says one is under the other? Re-read: 'one of its two middle terms is subordinate to the other'. So which? The text says 'the first: one of its two middle terms is subordinate to the other'. It seems the first set has middle terms 'animal' and 'perfect', but 'animal' is not under 'perfect'. Possibly the subordination refers to the relation within each demonstration? Actually the translation is ambiguous. Perhaps the author means that the first type has middle terms where one is predicated of the other? Let's check the Arabic: 'مثل قولنا كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان مغتذ ; وقولنا كل إنسان تام وكل تام معتدل' The two demonstrations are separate: one with middle term 'animal', another with middle term 'perfect'. They are not linked. The author then says 'فالأول أحد حديه الأوسطين تحت الآخر' meaning 'the first: one of its two middle terms is under the other'. That refers to within the first demonstration? No, 'the first' refers to the first type (a). So in type (a), the two middle terms (from two different demonstrations) are such that one is subordinate to the other. How? Possibly because 'animal' and 'perfect' are related? 'Perfect' is a property that can be predicated of 'animal'? Actually 'perfect' is not subordinate to 'animal' in a genus-species sense. Perhaps the author has a different understanding. We'll need to be cautious. The guided path should focus on the logical relation as described.
The relation of subordination here means that one middle term can be predicated of the other, e.g., 'perfect' can be said of 'animal'? Unclear. But the author's point is that whether the middle terms are hierarchically related or not, the demonstrations belong to the same science.
3
Analyze the second type with non-subordinate middle terms
Example: 'every pleasure-subject is moving, every moving is changing' and 'every pleasure-subject is at rest, every at rest is changing'. The middle terms 'moving' and 'at rest' are opposites, not subordinate. Yet both prove 'changing' for pleasure-subjects.
Also the laughing/bashful example shows convertible but non-subordinate middle terms.
4
Conclude why different middle terms do not necessitate different sciences
The unity of a science is determined by its subject matter (e.g., human, change), not by the variety of proofs. Multiple demonstrations enrich the science.
The author encourages deriving further examples from the remaining syllogistic figures.

وليس اختلاف البراهين توجب اختلافًا في هذا الباب فقد يكون على شيء واحد برهانان مختلفان لا من حدين أوسطين يحمل أحدهما على الآخر فقط مثل قولنا كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان مغتذ ؛ وقولنا كل إنسان تام وكل تام معتدل ومن حدين أوسطين لا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر مثل قولنا كل قابل اللذة متحرك، وكل متحرك متغير مع قولنا كل قابل اللذة ساكن، وكل ساكن متغير فالأول أحد حديه الأوسطين تحت الآخر. وأما الثاني فمما يختلفان ليس أحدهما تحت الآخر. وكذلك قولنا كل إنسان ضاحك، وكل ضاحك متعجب مع قولنا كل إنسان مستحي، وكل مستحي متعجب، فإنهما وإن كانا من جملة ما ينعكس أحدهما على الآخر فلا يكون أحدهما تحت الآخر: فقد ظهر أن اختلاف الحدود الوسطى لا يوجب اختلافًا في المباحث من جهة اختلاف علومها، ويمكن أن يطلب مؤخذ نظر هذا من الأشكال الباقية.
And the difference of demonstrations does not necessitate difference in this chapter. For there may be for one thing two different demonstrations: (a) from two middle terms one of which is predicated of the other only, like our saying: every human is an animal, and every animal is nourished; and our saying: every human is perfect, and every perfect is moderate. And (b) from two middle terms not one of which is predicated of the other, like our saying: every pleasure-subject is moving, and every moving is changing, together with our saying: every pleasure-subject is at rest, and every at rest is changing. So the first: one of its two middle terms is subordinate to the other. As for the second, they differ in that one is not subordinate to the other. Likewise, our saying: every human is laughing, and every laughing is wondering, together with our saying: every human is bashful, and every bashful is wondering. For these two, although they are among what one is convertible with the other, still one is not subordinate to the other. Thus it becomes clear that difference of middle terms does not necessitate a difference in the investigations from the perspective of their sciences' difference. And one can seek the basis of this view from the remaining figures.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage expands on the previous point: differing proofs do not make differing sciences. The author distinguishes two types of alternative demonstrations. First, where the two middle terms are in a hierarchical relation (one predicated of the other). Example: 'Every human is an animal, every animal is nourished' and 'Every human is perfect, every perfect is moderate' – here the middle terms 'animal' and 'perfect' are not directly related, but the first pair is one demonstration, the second another; they are distinct proofs but for the same conclusion? Actually the example is not fully explicit, but the author shows that different middle terms can still be within one science. Second, where the two middle terms are not hierarchical, like 'pleasure-subject' and 'moving' vs. 'pleasure-subject' and 'at rest' – these are contradictory, but both lead to 'changing', so they are alternative demonstrations. The author also gives an example with 'human is laughing' and 'human is bashful' – both lead to 'wondering' but middle terms are not subordinate. The conclusion is that differing middle terms do not change the science. He encourages the reader to find further examples from the remaining syllogistic figures.

The Diversity of Syllogistic Principles
Author refutes uniformity of syllogistic principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential and accidental production in syllogisms and explain why true syllogisms require true premises.

Core Idea
Syllogistic principles are not uniform: each science has its own specific starting points.

This passage challenges the assumption that all syllogisms share the same kind of principles. The author argues that there is a fundamental difference between syllogisms that produce true conclusions essentially and those that produce false conclusions. Moreover, the principles of each science are tied to its subject matter and essential accidents, making them unique to that science. Understanding this diversity is crucial for evaluating arguments across different fields.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target assumption
Locate the claim that the author is refuting: that all syllogistic principles are alike. This is stated at the beginning: 'مبادئ المقاييس كلها متفقة'.
2
Distinguish essential vs accidental production
Notice the distinction between producing a conclusion essentially (بالذات) and accidentally (بالعرض). A syllogism produces essentially when the conclusion necessarily follows from true premises; accidentally when it happens to be true but the premises are false.
This is crucial for understanding why true syllogisms must have true premises.
3
Apply the distinction to true and false syllogisms
Follow the argument: false syllogisms must have false premises. True syllogisms can accidentally have false premises, but then they are not genuine syllogisms because production is accidental. Therefore, genuine true syllogisms require true premises. These principles differ from those of false syllogisms.
The author concludes: 'كان من اللوازم أن تكون المنتجة للصادقات مقدمات صادقة'.
4
Examine the diversity across sciences
The author then argues that true syllogisms in different sciences have different principles. The example: geometry uses 'every magnitude is either rational or composite' while arithmetic uses 'every number is either prime or composite'. These are different because their subject matters differ (point vs unit). Thus, syllogistic principles are not uniform across sciences.
This reinforces the main point that principles vary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قيل إنه لا يصح أن يظن أن مبادئ المقاييس كلها متفقة: أما أولا فإن المقاييس منها منتجة للكاذبة؛ ويجب أن تكون مقدماتها كاذبة، ومنها منتجة للصادقة؛ وحينئذ يمكن أن تكون مقدماتها كاذبة فذلك إنتاج يتّبع منها لا بالذات بل بالعرض. ويُشبه أن لا تكون هي من جهة إنتاجها للصادقة عن الكاذبة قياسات؛ إذ القياس إنما هو قياس من جهة ما ينتج بالذات لا من جهة ما ينتج بالعرض. وإذا كان كذلك كان من اللوازم أن تكون المنتجة للصادقات مقدمات صادقة؛ وإنها غير مبادئ القياسات الكاذبة. وأيضًا فإن القياسات الكاذبة ليست متفقة في النتائج؛ فإن الأضداد قد تكذب معا. وأيضًا فإن المقاييس الصادقة يجب أن تكون واحدة بأعيانها؛ وذلك لأن المبادئ الخاصة بالأجناس الموضوعة لكل علم فتكون من موضوعاتها ومن عوارضها الذاتية مثل قولنا في الهندسة كل مقدار إما منطق وإما جم، وكل عدد إما أولي وإما مركب. ومن المعلوم أن هذه مختلفة لا مطابقة فيها لأن الهندسية كلها بعد النقطة، والعددية كلها بعد الوحدة : فلا يمكن أن يكون بينهما مطابقة. ولو كانت غير ضرورية فذلك إما أن يكون أحد المبدأين أعم من الآخر كما مر من قبل فيكون أحد الجنسين تحت الآخر أو فوقه؛ ومثل هذه الشركة في بعض في المبادئ. وهناك قد يكون الأمر على ما بينا قبل ذلك إذا كانت أجناس العلوم
Then it was said that it is not correct to suppose that the principles of syllogisms are all alike: firstly, among syllogisms are those that produce false conclusions; their premises must be false, and among them are those that produce true conclusions; but it is possible that their premises are false, so that production follows from them not essentially but accidentally. And it is probable that they are not syllogisms with respect to producing truth from false premises, for a syllogism is only a syllogism from the aspect of what it produces essentially, not from the aspect of what it produces accidentally. If so, it is necessary that those producing true conclusions have true premises; and they are not the principles of false syllogisms. Also, false syllogisms are not uniform in their conclusions; for opposites can both be false. Also, true syllogisms must be one in themselves; that is because the principles specific to the genera posited for each science are from their subjects and their essential accidents, such as our saying in geometry: every magnitude is either rational or composite, and every number is either prime or composite. And it is known that these are different with no correspondence, because geometrical things are all after the point, and numerical things are all after the unit; so there can be no correspondence between them. And if they were not necessary, then either one of the two principles is more general than the other, as has passed before, so one genus is under or above the other; and such is the commonality in some principles. And there the matter may be as we explained before, when the genera of the sciences [are such].
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage critiques the assumption that all syllogistic principles are uniform. The author presents several arguments. First, there is a distinction between syllogisms that produce false conclusions (which must have false premises) and those that produce true conclusions (which can accidentally have false premises, but that would not be a genuine syllogism because production would be accidental, not essential). Genuine syllogisms produce essentially; so true syllogisms must have true premises, and these premises are distinct from the principles of false syllogisms. Second, false syllogisms are not uniform in their conclusions because opposites can both be false. Third, true syllogisms must be identical in themselves; that is, the principles specific to each science (like geometry and arithmetic) come from the subjects and essential accidents of that science, and they are different with no correspondence—geometrical things are based on the point, arithmetical things on the unit. If principles are not necessary, there can be overlap in generality, but this is a different situation. The passage concludes by referring to a previous discussion about the genera of sciences.

Key Terms
مبادئ المقاييس
بالذات
بالعرض
عوارض ذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Its conclusion must be necessary.

Key Takeaway
True syllogisms require true premises, and the principles of each science are unique to its subject, so syllogistic principles are not all alike.

Distinguishing Specific and Common Principles of Demonstration
Author discusses the nature of demonstrative principles and their sharing across sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between specific and common principles of demonstration and explain why specific principles cannot be shared across sciences with different subject genera.

Core Idea
Specific principles derive from the subject genus; common principles apply universally.

In this passage, the author refines the claim that all syllogistic principles are the same. He distinguishes essential from accidental production, and then argues that specific principles are unique to each science's subject matter, while common principles like non-contradiction are shared. This distinction is crucial for maintaining the integrity of arguments in different Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify essential vs accidental syllogistic production
The passage states that a syllogism is defined by producing its conclusion essentially, not accidentally. If a syllogism produces truth from false premises, that is accidental. So true conclusions require true premises. Notice how this sets the stage for discussing principles.
2
Distinguish specific from common principles
The author divides principles into two types: specific (مبادئ خاصة) tied to the subject genus (e.g., geometry's principles about magnitude) and common (مبادئ عامة) like the law of non-contradiction. Understand that specific principles are grounded in the essence of the subject.
3
Analyze the geometry-arithmetic example
The author gives the example of geometry (deals with magnitude, after the point) and arithmetic (deals with number, after the unit). Their specific principles (e.g., 'every magnitude is rational or irrational') cannot be shared because their subject genera are different. Verify this by comparing their bases: point vs. unit.
This illustrates the impossibility of sharing specific principles across different subject genera.
4
Determine when sharing specific principles is possible
The passage explains that sharing specific principles is possible only when one science is subordinate to another (e.g., optics under geometry). If the genera are not under each other, sharing is impossible. Check the condition: the subject genus of one must be a species or part of the other's genus.
This is a key condition for participation in principles.
5
Apply the distinction to Islamic sciences
Now apply this to a real Islamic science: For example, the specific principle 'The world is temporally originated' belongs to Kalam, while 'Every intoxicant is prohibited' belongs to Fiqh. They cannot be interchanged. Common principles like 'A thing cannot be both true and false' apply in both. Practice identifying principles from other sciences like Tafsir or Hadith.
This reinforces the practical use of the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ومنها منتجة للصادقة؛ وحينئذ يمكن أن تكون مقدماتها كاذبة فذلك إنتاج يتّبع منها لا بالذات بل بالعرض. ويُشبه أن لا تكون هي من جهة إنتاجها للصادقة عن الكاذبة قياسات؛ إذ القياس إنما هو قياس من جهة ما ينتج بالذات لا من جهة ما ينتج بالعرض. وإذا كان كذلك كان من اللوازم أن تكون المنتجة للصادقات مقدمات صادقة؛ وإنها غير مبادئ القياسات الكاذبة. وأيضًا فإن القياسات الكاذبة ليست متفقة في النتائج؛ فإن الأضداد قد تكــذب مـعــا. وأيضًا فإن المقاييس الصادقة يجب أن تكون واحدة بأعيانها؛ وذلك لأن المبادئ الخاصة بالأجناس الموضوعة لكل علم فتكون من موضوعاتها ومن عوارضها الذاتية مثل قولنا في الهندسة كل مقدار إما منطق وإما جم، وكل عدد إما أولي وإما مركب. ومن المعلوم أن هذه مختلفة لا مطابقة فيها لأن الهندسية كلها بعد النقطة، والعددية كلها بعد الوحدة : فلا يمكن أن يكون بينهما مطابقة. ولو كانت غير ضرورية فذلك إما أن يكون أحد المبدأين أعم من الآخر كما مر من قبل فيكون أحد الجنسين تحت الآخر أو فوقه؛ ومثل هذه الشركة في بعض في المبادئ. وهناك قد يكون الأمر على ما بينا قبل ذلك إذا كانت أجناس العلوم المتشاركة واقعا بعضها تحت بعض. وأما الأجناس التي ليس بعضها تحت بعض فلا يمكن ذلك فيها؛ وأعني بالأجناس الموضوعات. وأما أن يكون مبدءًا داخلا في الوسط للآخر مثل الخطوط المتوارية التي بين المتواريين فيكون حينئذ إما متشاركين في الجنس؛ فيكون أحدهما مبدءًا والآخر نتيجة، أو لا متشاركين في الجنس أعني الموضوع بل في جنسه؛ فيكون أيضًا أحد العلمين تحت الآخر، فيكون الشركة في المبدأ على نحو ما قلناه من قبل. وأما العلوم المختلفة التي ليس بعضها تحت بعض فلا يمكن اشتراكها في المبدأ الخاص البتة. وأما المبادئ العامة مثل قولنا كل شيء إما أن يكون وإما أن لا يكون فقد يشترك فيها؛ فإن هذه المبادئ صالحة في بيان أحوال جميع الموجودات المختلفة التي بعضها كم وبعضها كيف وغير ذلك. وبالجملة لا يكون في العلوم المختلفة اشتراك بالفعل بل بالقوة، والنتائج المطلوبة في العلوم وإن كانت تزيد / على المقدمات على النحو المعلوم في تركيب القياس فليست زيادة مفرطة خارجة عن نسبة محفوظة؛ ولا حاجة إلى الإطناب في هذا الباب. وقد بان فيما سلف أن العلوم تشترك في المبادئ، والمبادئ منها خاصة ومنها عامة فعسى الحق هو أن المبادئ مناسبة في الجنس أي في الموضوع ولكن هذا لا يمكن، وأن العلوم التي لا يناسب في الموضوع فإن مبادئها الخاصة بأجناسها لا تناسب في الموضوع. والذي يجب أن يُعتقد فيه أنه الحق هو أن المبادئ تقال على النوعين: إما مبادئ فيها البرهان أي المقدمات الأولى في العلوم ، وإما مبادئ ففيها البرهان وهي أجناس العلوم أي موضوعاتها، وما يتعلق بها مما يوضع معها، أو يساويها كالواحد بوجه ما للموجود. فالقسم الأول يجوز أن تكون فيها مبادئ عامة
As for the discussion about the manner of sensation and the difference of opinions therein, that is separate from what we are in. Then it was said that it is not correct to think that the principles of all syllogisms are in agreement: First, because some syllogisms produce false [conclusions]; their premises must be false, and some produce true [conclusions]; then it is possible that their premises are false, so that production follows from them not essentially but accidentally. And it seems that they are not syllogisms in terms of producing truth from falsehood; for the syllogism is [only] a syllogism in terms of what it produces essentially, not in terms of what it produces accidentally. And if this is so, it follows that the producers of truths have true premises; and they are not the principles of false syllogisms. Also, false syllogisms do not agree in their conclusions; for opposites may both be false. Also, true syllogisms must be one in themselves; because the specific principles of the assigned genera for each science are from its subject matters and from its essential accidents, like our saying in geometry: every magnitude is either rational or irrational, and every number is either prime or composite. And it is known that these are different with no correspondence, because geometrical [magnitudes] are all after the point, and arithmetical [numbers] are all after the unit; so it is impossible for there to be correspondence between them. And if they were not necessary, then either one of the two principles is more general than the other, as mentioned before, so one of the two genera is under or above the other; and such is participation in some of the principles. And there the matter may be as we explained before when the genera of the sciences that participate actually lie under one another. As for the genera that do not lie under one another, that is impossible in them; and by genera I mean the subject matters. And as for a principle being included in the middle of another, like the parallel lines that are between parallels, then either they are participators in genus; so one is a principle and the other a result, or they do not participate in the genus—I mean the subject—but in its genus; so also one of the two sciences is under the other, and participation in the principle is in the manner we mentioned before. As for the different sciences that do not lie under one another, it is absolutely impossible for them to share the specific principle. As for the common principles, like our saying 'Everything either is or is not,' they may share in them; for these principles are suitable for explaining the states of all diverse existents, some of which are quantity, some quality, and so on. In general, there is no actual participation in different sciences, but potential, and the desired conclusions in the sciences, although they increase over the premises in the known manner in the composition of the syllogism, the increase is not excessive beyond a preserved proportion; and there is no need for verbosity in this topic. And it has become clear in what preceded that the sciences share in principles, and principles are of two types: specific and common. Perhaps the truth is that principles are appropriate in genus—that is, in the subject—but this is not possible, and that sciences that are not appropriate in subject have their specific principles that are not appropriate in subject. What must be believed as true is that principles are said of two types: either principles in which demonstration [occurs]—i.e., the first premises in the sciences—or principles for which demonstration [occurs]—i.e., the genera of the sciences, namely their subject matters, and what pertains to them that is laid down with them, or equals them, like the one in a certain respect to the existent. So the first type may have common principles.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the nature of demonstrative principles and their sharing across sciences. The author begins by dismissing a discussion on sensation as irrelevant. He then addresses the mistaken notion that all syllogistic principles are the same. He distinguishes syllogisms that produce truth essentially from those that produce truth accidentally, noting that a syllogism is defined by essential production. Therefore, true conclusions require true premises. He then argues that true syllogisms are unique to each science because specific principles derive from the subject genus and its essential accidents. For example, geometry deals with magnitude (rational/irrational), arithmetic with number (prime/composite); these are fundamentally different due to their bases (point vs. unit). If principles were not necessary, some might be more general, leading to subordination of sciences. The author clarifies that only sciences with genera under one another can share specific principles; otherwise, sharing is impossible. However, common principles like the law of non-contradiction can be shared across all sciences, as they apply to all existents. He concludes that sharing is potential, not actual. Finally, he divides principles into two types: those that are the premises in demonstration, and those that are the subject genera themselves (including their properties). The first type may include common principles.

Key Terms
مبادئ (mabādi'): Principles of demonstration, either the first premises in a science or the subject genera and their essential accidents. [Note: In later logic, mabādi' often refers specifically to primary premises (awwaliyyāt); here it is broader.]
البرهان (al-burhān): Demonstration, a syllogism that produces certain knowledge from true premises. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
الجنس (al-jins): Genus, here meaning the subject matter of a science (e.g., magnitude for geometry). [Note: In logic, jins is also a universal; here it is used for the subject genus of a science.]
الذاتي (al-dhātī): Essential, as opposed to accidental; a property that belongs to the essence of a thing. [Note: Stable but always requires careful distinction in usage.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because they have different subject matters: magnitude vs. number, based on point vs. unit.
B) Because one is descriptive and the other is prescriptive.
C) Because they use different logical rules.
D) Because they were invented by different people.

Key Takeaway
Specific demonstrative principles are unique to each science's subject genus, while common principles like non-contradiction are shared across all sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Middle Term is Not Always a Definition
Author critiques the claim that every demonstration's middle term is a definition or essence cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that every middle term in a demonstration is a definition by identifying efficient causes and external properties that serve as middle terms.

Core Idea
Not every middle term is a definition; efficient causes and external properties often serve as the 'why'.

In this passage, the author tackles a widespread misconception among earlier logicians: that every middle term in a demonstrative syllogism is a definition or part of a definition. By examining the different types of inquiries (hal, ma, lim) and giving concrete examples from natural causes, he shows that many middle terms are efficient causes or external properties, which are not definitions. This lesson will walk you through his argument and its implications for the relationship between demonstration and definition.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish hal, ma, lim inquiries
Notice that the author distinguishes three types of questions: 'whether' (hal), 'what' (ma), and 'why' (lim). The search for 'why' is actually a search for the middle term potentially. This sets up the confusion that some equate 'why' with 'what'.
The term 'potentially' means that the middle term is not yet explicit in the question but it is what would provide the answer.
2
Identify the false inference
The author critiques those who think that every search for 'why' is also a search for 'what' potentially, and then jump to the conclusion that all middle terms are definitions. This is a logical error (false conversion).
3
Examine counterexamples from the passage
The author gives examples: an intermediate genus causing a higher genus, properties causing other properties, and efficient causes like fire touching wood. None of these are definitions; they are either external or causal in a non-essential way.
Efficient cause: cause of existence, not quiddity.
4
Distinguish definition from description
Definitions capture quiddity (essence) and are composed of genus and differentia. Descriptions (rusum) include efficient causes and external properties. The author concludes that efficient causes belong to descriptions, not definitions.
This clarifies the boundary between demonstration and definition.

Source Text

بالقوة هل هناك حد أوسط مثل (۱) من سأل هل الشيء كذا فإنه إنما يطلب هل شيء يوجب العلم، أو لم قلتَ إن الشيء كذا فإنه مطلب ما عليه القياس؛ وإنه قياس وهو الحد الأوسط، أو ما عليه القياس في أنه برهان وهو الحد وهو الحد الأوسط الذي هو عليه الأمر في نفسه. فيكون البحث عن لم؛ بحثًا عما هو الحد الأوسط بالقوة فيكون طلب «لِمَ» هنا إنما هو طلب «لم» بالقياس إلى النتيجة؛ ويكون بالفعل، وطلب بالقياس إلى الحد الأوسط؛ ويكون بالقوة. وأما طلب «ما» الحد الأوسط فذلك ظاهر لا بد منه أن يكون مجهولا. فقول المعلم الأوَّل الموجود بالجزء يعني به الموجود شيء ما، والموجود بالكل يعني به الموجود على الإطلاق» (۲) والأول إما شيئًا جوهريا للموضوع أو عرضًا ذاتيًا أو عرضًا خارجيًا . ثمَّ يقول أعني بالموجود على الإطلاق الشيء المطلوب هل نفسه موجود مثل قولنا هل المثلث موجود: فهذا إنما يبحث عن وجود نفس الموضع . وأما هل المثلث كذا فإنه إنما يبحث عن وجود عارض ما أو لاحق؛ وهذا هو الموجود شيئًا ما ، فظهر من هذا أن المطالب بالقوة يرجع إلى هل الشيء، وإلى ما الشيء. وأن مطلب اللم بحث ما عن ما الشيء بوجه لأنه بالقوة بمعنى ما الأوسط ولكن من الناس من ظن أن هذا ينعكس؛ وأنه ليس في البراهين شيء وهو بحث اللهم إلا وهو بحث الما بالقوة، ولا بحث الما إلا وهو بحث اللم. وتعدَّى هذا إلى أن ظن أن الأوسط في البراهين هي الحدود وكل ذلك أمر باطل فإنه ليس كل بحث عما هو عن الأوسط. وأيضًا ليس البحث عما هو الأوسط هو البحث عن ما فيه أحد الجزأين من الآخر حتى يكون الجواب حدا؛ ولا كل ما هو علــة موجبة فهو حد أو جنس أو فصل أو مادة أو صورة. فإن العلل الموجبة لأمور لا في أنفسها ولا بوجه ما نفس الموجب لا صورة ولا مادة وكثيرًا ما نجد من الأوساط في البراهين ما ليس مادة ولا صورة حدا بل نجد شيئاً موجباً لشيء في شيء فالجنس المتوسط يوجب وجود الجنس الأعلى في النوع الآخر بل وفي كل ما يحمل عليه الجنس المتوسط؛ وليس هو حدا للأكبر ولا صورة ولا مادة. وكثيرًا من الخواص هي علة لكثير من الخواص وهي خارجة عنها ليست بجنس لها ولا فصل ولا حد. وكثيرًا من الأوساط البرهانية ليست حدودًا ولا عللا داخلة في جوهر [۳۱۰] الشيء بل عللا فاعلية وموجبة وحدا (۱) حكم مماسة النار فإنها قد يجعل / حدا أوسط في إثبات إحراق الخشبة وإن كان قد يمكن أن تجعل هذه العلة الموجبة فصولا من جهة على أنها أجزاء الفصول لا تحمل الفصول المعمولة منها كما أن القدوم لا يقال إنه حديد بل من حديد، ولا يقال إن الحمى عفونة بل (۳) من عفونة، وليست أجزاء فصول الذات هي أخص الفصول بل أجزاء فصول خاصة فقط. والعلل الفاعلية علل للوجود، وأجزاء الحد علل للماهية على ما عرف. ولهذا لا تدخل علل الوجود في الحدود بل في الرسوم القائمة مقام الحدود، فإذن ليس كل حد أوسط حدا أو جزء حد. ولو كان البرهان هو الذي أوسطه للحد فلا يوجد برهان على شيء إلا على ما وجود حد الأكبر للأصغر فيه ظاهر، ووجود نفس الحد الأكبر خفي. ثم البرهان بمعزل عن الحد وإن كان قد يتوهم فيما سلف من مشاركة طلب اللم وهو طلب البرهان، وطلب ما وهو طلب الحد أن البرهان والحدّ قد يقومان على شيء واحد من جهة واحدة؛ وليس كذلك
Potentially, is there a middle term? For example: (1) He who asks 'Is the thing such?' is only asking whether there is something that necessitates knowledge, or 'Why did you say that the thing is such?' is a demand for what the syllogism is based on; and it is a syllogism, and it is the middle term, or what the syllogism is based on in that it is a demonstration, and it is the definition, and it is the middle term which is the matter itself in itself. So the search for 'why' is a search for what is potentially the middle term; thus the request for 'why' here is only a request for 'why' relative to the conclusion, and it is actual, and a request relative to the middle term, and it is potential. As for the request for 'what' the middle term is, that is apparent, necessarily unknown. So the statement of the First Teacher, 'the existent by part' means 'the existent something', and 'the existent by whole' means 'the existent absolutely' (2). The first is either a substantial thing for the subject, or an essential accident, or an external accident. Then he says: 'I mean by existent absolutely the thing requested whether its very self exists, like our saying: Does the triangle exist?' This only investigates the existence of the very subject. As for 'Is the triangle such?' it only investigates the existence of some accident or consequent; and this is the existent something. So it appears from this that the potentially sought things reduce to 'whether the thing' and 'what the thing'. And that the request for 'why' is a search for 'what' the thing in a certain way, because it is potential in the sense of 'what' the middle term. But some people thought that this is convertible; that there is no demonstration which is a search for 'why' except it is a search for 'what' potentially, and no search for 'what' except it is a search for 'why'. And this extended to the thought that the middle terms in demonstrations are definitions, and all that is false, because not every search for 'what' is about the middle term. Also, the search for 'what' the middle term is not the search for 'in what way one of the two parts is from the other' such that the answer is a definition; nor is every affirmative cause a definition or genus or differentia or matter or form. For affirmative causes of things that are not in themselves nor in any way the same as the thing affirmed are neither form nor matter, and we often find among the middle terms in demonstrations what is not matter or form or definition; rather we find something affirming something in something: the intermediate genus necessitates the existence of the higher genus in the other species, and also in everything on which the intermediate genus is predicated; and it is not a definition for the major term, nor form nor matter. Many properties are causes of many properties, and are external to them, not a genus for them nor differentia nor definition. Many demonstrative middle terms are not definitions nor internal causes in the essence of the thing, but efficient and affirmative causes, and a cause like the touching of fire—it may be taken as a middle term in proving the burning of wood, although it may be possible to make this affirmative cause differentiae in a certain respect, provided that they are parts of differentiae; the differentiae made from them are not predicated of them, just as a chisel is not said to be iron but rather from iron, and fever is not said to be putrefaction but rather from putrefaction. The parts of essential differentiae are not the most specific differentiae but only parts of specific differentiae. Efficient causes are causes of existence, and the parts of the definition are causes of quiddity, as is known. Therefore, causes of existence do not enter into definitions, but rather into descriptions (rusum) that stand in place of definitions. Hence, not every middle term is a definition or part of a definition. If the demonstration were such that its middle term is for the definition, then no demonstration would exist for anything except where the existence of the definition of the major in the minor is apparent, and the existence of the definition itself is hidden. Then demonstration is separate from definition, although it might have been previously imagined, due to the shared seeking of 'why'—which is the seeking of demonstration—and seeking 'what'—which is the seeking of definition—that demonstration and definition may both pertain to the same thing from one aspect. But it is not so.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the claim that every middle term in a demonstration is a definition or part of a definition. The author begins by distinguishing types of inquiries: asking 'whether' (hal) and 'what' (ma) and 'why' (lim). The search for 'why' is essentially a search for the middle term potentially, while the search for 'what' the middle term is remains unknown. The author references the First Teacher (Aristotle) who distinguishes 'existent by part' (a thing existing as something) from 'existent absolutely' (existence itself). The search for 'whether a triangle exists' is about absolute existence, while 'whether the triangle is such' is about an accident. The author then criticizes those who mistakenly think that every demonstration's 'why' is reducible to a 'what' potentially, and who then extend that to claim that all middle terms are definitions. This is false. Not every search for 'what' is about the middle term, and not every affirmative cause is a genus, differentia, matter, or form. Examples are given: an intermediate genus causes the existence of a higher genus but is not a definition. Many properties are causes of other properties but are external to them. Efficient causes, like the touching of fire causing burning, are not definitional parts; they cause existence, not quiddity. Therefore, definitions capture quiddity, while efficient causes belong to descriptions (rusum). The author concludes that demonstration and definition are distinct, despite their apparent similarity in seeking 'why' and 'what'.

الحد الأوسط
برهان
حد
علة فاعلية

Key Takeaway
A middle term in a demonstration does not have to be a definition; it can be an efficient cause or external property, which are captured in descriptions (rusum), not definitions.

The Syllogism

Refuting a Co-extensive Middle Term as Definition in Syllogisms
Author examines four cases of a co-extensive middle term being used as a definition, refuting each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute any syllogism that attempts to prove a definition using a co-extensive middle term, by applying the critiques of completeness, circularity, and false premises.

Core Idea
Using a co-extensive middle term as a definition leads to absurdities like multiple definitions, circularity, or false premises.

This passage from the logic text examines a critical issue: can a middle term that is co-extensive (musāwī) with the minor term serve as a definition of that minor in a syllogism? The author systematically refutes four cases, showing that each leads to impossibility. Understanding this refutation sharpens your ability to detect invalid proofs in Islamic sciences, especially when definitions are involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of the attempted proof
The syllogism in question has a middle term equal in extension to the minor term. The major term is intended to be a definition (hadd) of the minor. The middle term is asserted as a definition of the minor (either complete, incomplete, or unspecified).
2
Analyze the first case: middle term as complete definition
If the middle term is a complete definition of the minor, then both the middle and major would be complete definitions for the same thing—impossible because a complete definition is unique. Also, either the middle's definition requires proof (leading to circularity or infinite regress) or not (implying some definitions are more evident—false since all are essential).
3
Extend to incomplete and unspecified definitions (cases 2 and 3)
The same issues apply: if the middle is an incomplete definition, the same dilemma of needing proof or not recurs. If unspecified, it must be either complete or incomplete, so the same reasoning holds.
4
Examine the fourth case: middle term not as definition
Here the major term (the intended definition) is asserted either as a definition of itself (absurd), or as a definition of everything the middle holds for (false because the middle holds for properties, not just essence), or as a definition of everything the middle holds for but only yielding the same claim already known (no new proof).

Source Text

إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إن الأوسط المساوي للأصغر إذا حمل عليه على أنه حد له فإما أن يحمل عليه على أنه حد كامل، أو ناقص، أو لا على أنه هذا أو ذلك بل على أنه حد له كيف كان أو على أنه حاصل لكل حاصل للأصغر. [1] فإن كان الأول كان الحد الكامل للشيء الواحد اثنين؛ أحدهما هو الأوسط في إثبات الآخر له، وذلك محال فإن الحد الكامل مركب من جميع مقومات الشيء؛ والجميع لا يمكن أن يكون متعددا. وقد مر من قبل ما يدل على استحالة كونه وهو الحد الكامل متعددا، ولأن كونه حدا للأصغر إما أن يكون مفتقرًا إلى البرهان أو لا يكون فإن كان مفتقرًا إليه يلزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل . وإن لم يكن يلزم أن يكون البعض من الحدود أجلى من البعض؛ وذلك محال؛ إذ الحدود كلها من الذاتيات فلا يمكن أن يكون بعضها أظهر بالنسبة إلى البعض. [٢] وإن كان الثاني وهو أن يحمل عليه على أنه حد ناقص فالكلام فيه نحو الكلام في الأول لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون مفتقرا إلى البرهان أو لا يكون؛ وإنما كان يلزم المحال كما مر . [٣] وإن كان الثالث وهو أن يحمل عليه على ما به حد كيف كان فكذلك؛ لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون كاملا على نفس الأمر أو ناقصا لاستحالة وجود ما يعمها بدون أحدهما؛ والكلام فيهما قد مر . [٤] وإن كان الرابع فالأكبر المساوي للأوسط إما أن يحمل عليه على أنه حد لنفسه، أو لكل ما حصل له الأول، أو حاصل لكل ما حصل له الأوسط، والكل باطل . [1] أما الأول فلأن الكلام فيه نحو الكلام في الأول. [ب] وأما الثاني فلأنه مصادرة على المطلوب الأول لأن ما حصل له الأوسط يلزم أن يكون هو المحدود فقط لامتناع أن يكون القول الواحد حدا للمختلفين بالماهية. ولأن الكبرى كاذبة حينئذ فإن الأوسط كما حصل للأصغر حصل أيضًا لفصوله وخواصه؛ ولا يمكن أن يكون الأكبر حدا لشيء منها . [ت] وأما الثالث فلأن القياس حينئذ لا ينتج إلا حمل الأكبر على الأصغر بالوجه الذي مر ذكره؛ وإنه من جملة ما لا حاجة له إلى البيان. ولأن المطلوب في هذا المقام هو أن يكون الأكبر حدا للأصغر؛ وذلك لا يلزم من القياس.
If you know this, we say: The middle term equal to the minor term, when asserted of it as a definition for it, then either it is asserted as a complete definition, or an incomplete definition, or not as this or that but as a definition for it in whatever way, or as that which holds for everything that holds for the minor term. [1] If the first, then the complete definition for one thing would be two; one of them is the middle term in proving the other for it, and that is impossible because the complete definition is composed of all the constituents of the thing; and all cannot be multiple. And what indicates the impossibility of it—the complete definition—being multiple has already been mentioned, and because its being a definition for the minor term is either in need of a proof or not. If it is in need, then either circularity or infinite regress occurs. And if it is not, then some definitions would be more evident than others; that is impossible because definitions are all of essential attributes, so it is not possible that some of them be more apparent than others. [2] If the second, i.e., it is asserted as an incomplete definition, then the discussion about it is similar to the discussion about the first because it cannot be but in need of a proof or not; the impossibility would follow as before. [3] If the third, i.e., it is asserted as that by which it is defined however it is, then likewise; because it cannot be but either complete in itself or incomplete, since it is impossible for something to encompass them without one of them; and the discussion about them has already passed. [4] If the fourth, then the major term equal to the middle term is either asserted as a definition for itself, or for everything that the first holds for, or for everything that the middle term holds for, and all are false. [a] As for the first, because the discussion about it is like the discussion about the first. [b] As for the second, because it is a begging of the original question because what the middle term holds for necessarily is only the defined thing, since it is impossible for one statement to be a definition for two things different in essence. And because the major premise would then be false, for the middle term holds for the minor term's differentia and properties as well; and the major cannot be a definition for any of them. [c] As for the third, because the syllogism then only yields the assertion of the major about the minor in the manner already mentioned; and that is among what needs no demonstration. And because the desired point here is that the major should be a definition for the minor; and that does not follow from the syllogism.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage refutes the idea that a middle term co-extensive with the minor term can be used in a syllogism to prove a definition. The author examines four scenarios. First, if the middle term is a complete definition of the minor, then there would be two complete definitions for the same thing (the middle term and the major term), which is impossible because a complete definition is unique. Moreover, either the middle term's being a definition requires a proof (leading to circularity or regression), or it does not, implying some definitions are more evident than others—impossible since all definitions are essential. Second, if the middle term is an incomplete definition, the same issues apply. Third, if the middle term is a definition in an unspecified way, it must be either complete or incomplete, so the same discussion applies. Fourth, if the middle term is not taken as a definition but as co-extensive, then the major term (the definition) is either a definition of itself (absurd), or of everything the middle holds for (which includes properties, not just essence—so false), or of everything the middle holds for (which only yields the same conclusion as before, not establishing the major as a definition of the minor). Thus, every attempt fails. The argument relies on the uniqueness and essential nature of definitions.

Key Terms
الأوسط
الأصغر
الأكبر
الحد الكامل
الحد الناقص

Checkpoint
In the fourth case, why is the second sub-case (where the major is a definition for everything the middle term holds for) invalid?
A) Because the middle term does not hold for everything.
B) Because the major premise would be false, as the middle term also holds for properties and differentia.
C) Because the syllogism would be invalid in mood.
D) Because it leads to an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
A co-extensive middle term cannot serve as a definition in a syllogism without leading to logical absurdities; definitions must be unique and self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the claim: 'Every human is rational.' Suppose someone tries to prove this by using 'rational animal' as the middle term, claiming it is a complete definition of human. Identify which of the four cases this falls under and explain the refutation.
?
In a debate about the definition of 'riba,' a scholar argues that 'riba is any excess in a barter of like goods' and uses that as a middle term to prove that 'date-for-date riba is forbidden.' Show why this attempt fails according to the passage (hint: the middle term is a definition of the minor term).
