The Four Causes in Definitions and Demonstrations
Author details how the four causes enter into definitions and demonstrations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four causes and identify their logical relationships in definitions and demonstrations.

Core Idea
The four causes are not equal: formal and final causes necessitate the effect; material and efficient causes require conditions.

In this passage, the author explores how the four causes function in definitions and demonstrations. He clarifies which causes necessarily entail the effect and which require further conditions, using examples from natural and artificial things. Understanding these distinctions is essential for evaluating the completeness of causal explanations in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four causes
Extract the four causes mentioned: material (mādda), formal (ṣūra), efficient (fāʿila), and final (ghāya). Note how each is defined in the passage.
The passage also discusses attributes like proximate/remote, essential/accidental, and potential/actual.
2
Distinguish necessary vs. conditional causes
Observe that the author states the final cause and formal cause each necessarily entail the effect by their existence. The material cause does not always necessitate; it often requires additional conditions like complete disposition and a moving cause.
The formal cause is simultaneous with the effect; the final cause may be later in time but both are prior in causality.
3
Analyze the molar teeth example
The author uses the example of molar teeth: their broad shape is necessary given the material disposition, but its purpose is grinding food – necessity and finality coexist. Understand that necessity does not negate purpose.
Similarly, incisors are sharp for cutting, showing purpose within natural necessity.
4
Compare natural vs. artificial efficient causes
In natural things, if the efficient cause is a natural power (like fire) and the complete disposition is present, the effect follows necessarily. In artificial things, the efficient cause (e.g., carpenter) may be extrinsic and may not always act. Volitional causes do not necessitate action even when the passive power is present.
The author distinguishes between natural powers that act by subjugation and per se, and artificial/volitional powers that do not.
5
Apply to definitions
Consider how this causal schema helps evaluate definitions: a complete definition should mention the relevant causes, especially the formal and final causes, as they are necessary. Incomplete definitions may omit crucial causal conditions.
For example, defining a human as a 'rational animal' includes the formal cause (rationality) but may need to imply final cause (purpose).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في تفصيل دخول أصناف العلل في الحدود والبراهين ليتم الوقوف به على مشاركة ما بين البرهان والحد

أما أصناف العلل نحو المادية والصورية وغيرهما فقد مرَّ من قبل غير مرة. وكذلك ما يجب أن تعلم من أحوالها نحو كونها قريبة وبعيدة، وكونها بالذات وبالعرض، وكونها بالقوة وبالفعل، ولا يستراب في أن منها ما يكون خاصا، ومنها ما يكون عاماً، وكذلك منها ما يكون جزئياً بإزاء المعلول الجزئي، ومنها ما يكون كليًّا. ثمَّ يجب أن تعلم أن وجود الغاية ووجود الصورة يلزم من كل واحد منهما وجود المعلول لا محالة، فالصورة مع المعلول في الزمان والغاية قد تكون بعده في الزمان؛ وكلاهما أقدم بالعلية. وأما المادة ففي كثير من الأمور الطبيعية تلزمها الصور بالضرورة ويوجد بوجودهما المعلول والغاية لا محالة؛ والضرورة لا تمنع الغاية فإن كثيرًا من الأمور الطبيعية تكون بالضرورة والغاية معا مثل أن المادة التي خلقت من الأسنان الطواحن عريضة إذا حصلت بتمام الاستعداد تلزمها الصورة ضرورة. ومع ذلك فإن خلقة عرضها لتمام وغاية وهو طحن الطعام كما أن خلقه حدة الأنياب لتمام وغاية وهو قطع الطعام. ثمَّ في كثير من المواد لا يلزم عند حصول الاستعداد أن يحصل التمام لأن تمام تلك المادة تحصل بحركة من علة محرّكة لا تقع إلا في زمان؛ وفي آخره ما ينتهي إلى الصورة. وكذلك الحال في الأمور الصناعية فإنه لا يلزم فيها وجود الصورة لوجود العُنصُر وحده؛ إذ العنصر في كل موضع لا يشتاق إلى الصورة إلا بعلة فاعلة : فإن كانت العلة الفاعلة غريبة فربَّما وردت على العُنصر ، وربَّما لم يرد وإن كانت العلة طبيعية وموجودة في جوهر الشيء؛ وكانت مما يفعل بالتسخير وبالذات لأنها قوة طبيعية لم يمكن أن لا يصدر عنها فعلها إذا كان الاستعداد التام حاصلا.

واعلم أن من قبل العلة التي هي مبدأ حركة ما ليس يجب من وضعه مع وضع القابل وضع المعلول ؛ ومنها ما يجب. فإن جميع القوى الطبيعية إذا لاقت المنفعلة وجب الفعل، والقوى الصناعية والإرادية والسوقية وما أشبه ذلك ليس يجب من اجتمعاهما مع القوى المنفعلة فعل وانفعال؛ وهذه العلل وإن كانت قد تخالطها ضرورة فهي تفعل الغاية لا بالاتفاق. وقد مرّ من قبل أنه كلما وضع المعلول بالفعل وضعت الأسباب كلها لكن الغاية ربَّما كانت
Section: on detailing the entry of types of causes into definitions and demonstrations, so that one may understand the commonality between demonstration and definition.

As for the types of causes – material, formal, etc. – they have passed previously many times. Likewise, what must be known about their conditions, such as being proximate and remote, being per se and per accidens, being potential and actual; and there is no doubt that among them are particular and general, and likewise among them are particular in relation to a particular effect, and among them are universal. Then you must know that the existence of the final cause and the existence of the formal cause each necessarily entail the existence of the effect: the formal cause is simultaneous with the effect in time, and the final cause may be after it in time; both are prior in causality. As for the material cause, in many natural things the forms necessarily accompany it, and by their existence the effect and the final cause exist necessarily; and necessity does not preclude finality, for many natural things exist by necessity and finality together, such as the material from which the molar teeth are created – broad – when it obtains complete disposition, the form necessarily accompanies it. Yet the creation of its breadth is for a completion and a purpose, namely grinding food, just as the creation of the sharpness of the incisors is for a completion and a purpose, namely cutting food. Then, in many materials, when disposition obtains, the completion does not necessarily occur, because the completion of that material occurs by a motion from a moving cause that only takes place in time; at its end, it culminates in the form. Similarly, in artificial things, the existence of the form does not necessarily follow from the existence of the element alone; for the element in each case does not desire the form except through an efficient cause. If the efficient cause is extrinsic, it may sometimes befall the element and sometimes not. But if the cause is natural and exists in the essence of the thing, and is of those that act by subjugation and per se because it is a natural power, then it is impossible that its act not proceed from it when the complete disposition is present.

And know that among causes that are the principle of motion, it is not necessary that from their position together with the position of the recipient the effect is posited; and among them it is necessary. For all natural powers, when they encounter the passive power, the act is necessary; while artificial, volitional, and coercive powers, etc., it is not necessary that from their coming together with the passive powers there is action and passion. And these causes, even if necessity may intermingle with them, they act for a purpose, not by accident. And it has passed before that whenever the effect is posited in actuality, all the causes are posited, but the final cause perhaps...
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage begins a section that details how the different types of causes (material, formal, final, efficient) enter into definitions (ḥudūd) and demonstrations (barāhīn). The author first reminds us that the types of causes and their attributes (proximate/remote, essential/accidental, potential/actual) have been discussed before. He then states that some causes are particular (in relation to a particular effect) and some are universal. He proceeds to explain the logical relationships: the existence of the final cause (ghāya) and the formal cause (ṣūra) each necessarily entail the existence of the effect. However, the formal cause is simultaneous with the effect, while the final cause may be temporally after it; both are prior in causality. The material cause (mādda) is discussed next: in many natural things, the forms necessarily accompany the material cause, and by the existence of both, the effect and the final cause exist necessarily. The author emphasizes that necessity does not negate purpose; for example, the broad shape of molar teeth is necessary given their material disposition, but its purpose is grinding food. Similarly, the sharpness of incisors is for cutting. Thus, natural objects can have both necessity and purpose. However, in many materials, the completion (the form) does not necessarily follow from the disposition because it requires a moving cause that acts over time. This is illustrated in artificial things: the form (e.g., a chair) does not follow from the wood alone; an efficient cause (a carpenter) is needed. If the efficient cause is extrinsic (e.g., a separate agent), it may or may not act; if the efficient cause is natural and intrinsic (like a natural power), then when the complete disposition is present, the effect necessarily follows. Finally, the author distinguishes between causes that necessitate their effect and those that do not. Natural powers (like fire) when meeting the passive subject produce action necessarily; artificial, voluntary, and coercive powers do not necessitate action even when the passive power is present. These causes act for a purpose, not by accident. The passage concludes by recalling a previous principle: when the effect is actual, all its causes are actual, although the final cause may perhaps not be fully realized? (The text ends mid-sentence).

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism
beginner

Non-Demonstrative Syllogisms: Purpose and Classification
Author introduces non-demonstrative syllogism types and their order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish demonstrative from non-demonstrative syllogisms based on their premises and purpose, and classify syllogisms as beneficial (universal/particular) or deceptive.

Core Idea
Not all valid syllogisms yield certainty; dialectical syllogisms serve debate, not self-knowledge.

After completing the study of demonstrative syllogisms (burhan), the author now turns to the remaining types of syllogisms. He explains that while all valid syllogisms share the same formal structure, they differ in their subject matter and the nature of their premises. Understanding this classification is essential for knowing when a syllogism can produce certainty and when it only yields probability or serves persuasion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main categories
The author divides non-demonstrative syllogisms into three: beneficial in universal matters, beneficial in particular matters, and deceptive (mughalita). Focus on where each is studied and why.
The beneficial universal syllogisms include dialectical ones.
2
Distinguish demonstrative from non-demonstrative
Demonstrative syllogisms have premises that are certain and natural (subject in nature). Non-demonstrative syllogisms have premises that are either opinable (zann) or conceded (musallama) by an opponent.
The form is identical; only the premises differ.
3
Understand the order of learning
The author argues we should study particular benefits first, then universal benefits, then harmful/deceptive syllogisms (to guard against them). This order mirrors the natural priority of seeking benefit before avoiding harm.
4
Grasp the role of dialectical syllogisms
Dialectical syllogisms use premises accepted by the opponent (musallama). They are useful in debate to persuade or refute, but they cannot produce certainty for oneself. Their benefit is accidental, not essential.
Certainty comes only from demonstrative syllogisms.

الجملة الخامسة : في الأقيسة الباقية | والنوع الأول منه في معرفة القياسات الجدلية وما يتعلق بها

كما أنه لا سبيل إلى معرفة القياس إلا بعد معرفة القضايا، ولا سبيل إلى معرفة القضايا إلا بعد معرفة البسائط كذلك لا سبيل إلى معرفة أصناف القياسات إلا بعد معرفة القياس المطلق. وأهم الأشياء عند الإنسان أن يفعل بما يكمل ذاته الشخصية ثمَّ يشتغل بما ينفع وقوعه أو يحفظ نوعه. وذات الإنسان بالحقيقة إما أن تكون هي النفس الناطقة ، أو الجزء الأشرف وهي الشيء المقصود يكمله من ذاته، وكمالها مكتسب بمعرفة الشيء: والمعرفة إلى معرفة فقط، وإما المعرفة لما تعمل به والمعرفة إنما يكون اكتسابها بالقياس اليقيني وهو البرهان. فإذن لا بد من تقدم معرفة القياس، وبعد ذلك من تقدم البرهان. وقد أعطينا ما أمكننا إعطاؤه على سبيل الاقتصار من علم البرهان ولكن هنا قياسات أخرى نافعة في الأمور الكلية، وقياسات أخرى مغلطة والنافعة في الأمور الكلية منها ما يتعلق أول تعلقها بالأمور الجزئية ويجب أن نتعلم هذه الأصناف أيضًا لما فيها من المنافع، وأن نتعلم المغلطات كذلك ليكون لنا قدرة على التحرُّز عنها. وقد علمت أن النوافع الكلية من حقها أن تؤخّر عن النوافع الجزئية. ولا يخفى عليك أن المنافع إنما يتعرف بالذات ليطلبه، والضار إنما يتعرض بالعرض ليتحرز فيجب إذن أن يكون تعرف حال القياسات المغلطة بعد تعرف حال القياسات النافعة في الأمور «النظرية» أي «الفكرية». ولأن المقاييس على الأمور الكلية أقرب إلى الدرجة العقلية من القياسات النافعة في الأمور الجزئية؛ فيجب أن يكون ما ينفع في الكليات على أي وجه كان فهو مقدم فيجب أن يكون أول نظرنا في هذا الصنف من القياسات.

فنقول: إن القياسات لا تتخالف في صورها بل كل ما إذا وضع فيه أقاويل لم يلزمه قول آخر فليس بقياس؛ وكل ما لزمه قول آخر فهو قياس كما مرَّ لكن الموضوعات مختلفة : فمنها ما وضعه في الطبيعة، ومنها ما وضعه بحسب الواضع. فالذي وضع ما فيه بحسب الطبيعة فهو القياس البرهاني؛ وقد فرغنا عنه / وجميع ما يتلوه يجب أن يخالفه فإنه ليس يجب أن يكون الموضوع فيه هو الموضوع في الطبيعة. وما يكون من هذه القياسات فإنما يتميز عن البرهان بحكم مقدماته فإنه أعم من حكم المقدّمات البرهانية، وقد سبق منا القول أن جميع المقدمات البرهانية التي لا وسط لها مشهورة؛ ولا تنعكس فإن المقدمات المشهورة المستعملة في الجدل ليس كذلك. فلنطلب الآن مقدمات أقرب القياسات إلى البرهان، ولننظر إما حدها مظنونات فقط أو مشهورات فحد الظنون الصرفية إنما تفيد القياسات المعمولة منها في الأمور الجزئية. وأما الأحكام على الأمور الكلية فلا تنتفع فيها بالمظنونات الساذجة بل الأولى أن يكون أحكامنا على الأمور الكلية إذا تعذر علينا البرهان. ولو تعرض لـغـيـره نحو القياس الجدلي فالأولى هو التعرض لمقدمات كانت مسلمة عند الخصم. ولا يتوهم أن هذا القياس قد ينتفع استعماله الإنسان مع نفسه بالقصد الأول؛ إذ المفيد لليقين هو البرهان؛ ليس إلا. وما دون اليقين فأكثره ظنُّ؛ وذلك مما يستلزم عدم الكمال وإنه هو الجهل بالحقيقة وإن أطلق عليه لفظ العلم، فإن العلم هو الذي لا يمكن أن يتصور على خلافه. والظن والوهم والشك كلها لا يكون كذلك، فإذن منفعته المخصوصة به في أمر مشترك بينه وبين غيره لكنه ينفع صاحبه لا بالذات بل بالعرض إن كانت العلية مطلقة عنده؛ وإنما ينتفع بالذات إذا كان المطلوب هو القدح في التيقن.
The Fifth Section: On the Remaining Syllogisms | And the first type therein is knowledge of dialectical syllogisms and what pertains to them.

Just as there is no way to know the syllogism except after knowing the propositions, and no way to know the propositions except after knowing the simple terms, likewise there is no way to know the types of syllogisms except after knowing the absolute syllogism. The most important thing for man is to do what perfects his personal essence, then to occupy himself with what benefits his occurrence or preserves his species. Man's essence in truth is either the rational soul, or the noblest part, and it is the intended thing that perfects itself from itself, and its perfection is acquired by knowledge of the thing: knowledge is only to knowledge, or knowledge of what one acts with, and knowledge is acquired only by certain syllogism, which is demonstration. So it is necessary to precede knowledge of the syllogism, and after that knowledge of demonstration. We have given what we could give concisely from the science of demonstration, but here there are other syllogisms beneficial in universal matters, and other deceptive syllogisms. Among the beneficial in universal matters, some are primarily connected to particular matters, and we must learn these types also for the benefits therein, and we must learn the deceptive ones as well to have the ability to guard against them. You have known that universal benefits ought to be postponed after particular benefits. It is not hidden from you that benefits are known essentially to be sought, and harm is encountered accidentally to be guarded against. So it is necessary that knowledge of the state of deceptive syllogisms come after knowledge of the state of syllogisms beneficial in theoretical, i.e., intellectual, matters. And because syllogisms on universal matters are closer to the intellectual degree than syllogisms beneficial in particular matters, whatever benefits in universals in any way should be given priority. So our first consideration should be in this type of syllogisms.

We say: Syllogisms do not differ in their forms; rather, every [collection] in which statements are placed and no other statement follows from it is not a syllogism, and everything from which another statement follows is a syllogism, as has passed. But the subject matters differ: some have their placement by nature, and some have their placement according to the positor. That whose placement is by nature is the demonstrative syllogism; we have finished with it. And everything that follows it must differ from it, for it is not necessary that the subject in it be the subject in nature. Whatever is among these syllogisms is distinguished from demonstration by the status of its premises, for it is more general than the status of demonstrative premises. We have already said that all indemonstrable demonstrative premises are well-known and do not convert, whereas the well-known premises used in dialectic are not like that. So let us now seek premises of the syllogisms closest to demonstration, and let us see: are they only opinions or well-known [premises]? The limit of pure opinions is that the syllogisms made from them benefit only in particular matters. As for judgments on universal matters, they do not benefit from raw opinions; rather, the first [best] is that our judgments on universal matters, when demonstration is impossible for us, [use something else]. If one turns to something else like dialectical syllogism, the first [best] is to turn to premises that are conceded by the opponent. Let no one suppose that this syllogism may be used by a person with himself as the primary intention; for what yields certainty is demonstration alone. What is below certainty is mostly opinion, and that entails lack of perfection and is ignorance of truth, even if the term 'knowledge' is applied to it. For knowledge is what cannot be conceived otherwise. Opinion, illusion, and doubt are all not like that. So its specific benefit is in a matter shared between him and others, but it benefits its possessor not essentially but accidentally, if the causation is absolute for him; and it benefits essentially only if the desired [goal] is to cast doubt on certainty.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage is an introductory section that sets the stage for the remainder of the book on non-demonstrative syllogisms. The author, writing in a commentarial style, begins by asserting the logical order of learning: just as one must know simple terms before propositions, and propositions before syllogisms in general, one must know the absolute syllogism before its various species. He then connects this to human perfection: the highest human goal is to perfect the rational soul through certain knowledge, which is acquired only through demonstrative syllogism (burhan). However, since we have already treated demonstration concisely, we must now turn to other syllogisms that are beneficial in universal matters, as well as deceptive ones that must be studied to guard against error. The author argues that the beneficial universal syllogisms should be studied after the particular ones, and the deceptive after the beneficial, because benefits are sought directly while harms are avoided indirectly. On page 506, he clarifies that syllogisms do not differ in their formal structure—they all have premises and a necessary conclusion—but rather in their subject matter. Demonstrative syllogisms have premises that are natural and certain. Other syllogisms, like dialectical ones, differ because their premises are not necessarily true in nature but are accepted by the opponent. The author then explains that the closest syllogisms to demonstration are those using premises that are either generally accepted (mashhurat) or opinable (maznunat). Pure opinions only yield syllogisms for particular matters, but for universal judgments when demonstration is unavailable, we should use premises conceded by the opponent—this is the dialectical syllogism. He warns against thinking dialectical syllogisms can produce certainty for oneself; only demonstration does that. What is below certainty is opinion, illusion, or doubt, not true knowledge. Therefore, the benefit of dialectical syllogisms is accidental: they help in debate with others, not in attaining certainty for oneself.

البرهان
القياسات الجدلية
المقدمات

Key Takeaway
Syllogisms share the same form; their type is determined by the certainty of their premises, with only demonstrative syllogisms yielding absolute knowledge.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Distinguishing Demonstrative and Dialectical Syllogisms
Author distinguishes burhani and jadali syllogisms by their subject matter and premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish demonstrative and dialectical syllogisms by their subject matter and premise convertibility.

Core Idea
Syllogisms share form but differ in subject matter and premise convertibility.

In this passage, the author classifies syllogisms into two types based on their subject matter: natural (burhani) and conventional (jadali). Understanding this distinction is crucial because the certainty of a syllogistic conclusion depends on the nature of its premises. Let us explore how the author differentiates them.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of syllogism
A syllogism is a discourse where certain statements entail another. All syllogisms share this formal structure.
2
Identify the two types of subject matter
Some subject matters are natural (corresponding to reality), others are conventional (posited by the disputant).
3
Match subject matter to syllogism type
Natural subject matter yields a demonstrative syllogism; conventional subject matter yields a dialectical syllogism.
4
Examine premise convertibility
Demonstrative premises do not convert, while dialectical premises do convert.
5
Summarize the key distinction
The difference lies not in form but in subject matter and whether premises convert.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: إن القياسات لا تتخالف في صورها بل كل ما إذا وضع فيه أقاويل لم يلزمه قول آخر فليس بقياس؛ وكل ما لزمه قول آخر فهو قياس كما مرَّ لكن الموضوعات مختلفة : فمنها ما وضعه في الطبيعة، ومنها ما وضعه بحسب الواضع. فالذي وضع ما فيه بحسب الطبيعة فهو القياس البرهاني؛ وقد فرغنا عنه وجميع ما يتلوه يجب أن يخالفه فإنه ليس يجب أن يكون الموضوع فيه هو الموضوع في الطبيعة. وما يكون من هذه القياسات فإنما يتميز عن البرهان بحكم مقدماته فإنه أعم من حكم المقدّمات البرهانية، وقد سبق منا القول أن جميع المقدمات البرهانية التي لا وسط لها مشهورة؛ ولا تنعكس فإن المقدمات المشهورة المستعملة في الجدل ليس كذلك.
We say: Syllogisms do not differ in their forms, but rather everything in which, when statements are placed, another statement does not follow from it, is not a syllogism; and everything from which another statement follows is a syllogism, as has passed. But the subjects differ: some of them are placed in nature, and some of them are placed according to the positor. So that which is placed according to nature is the demonstrative syllogism; and we have finished with it. And everything that follows it must be contrary to it, for it is not necessary that its subject be the subject in nature. And whatever is of these syllogisms, it is distinguished from demonstration by the rule of its premises, for it is more general than the rule of demonstrative premises. And we have already said that all demonstrative premises that have no middle term are well-known (mashhura) and do not convert; but the well-known premises used in dialectic are not like that.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage classifies syllogisms into two types based on their subject matter: the demonstrative (burhani) and the dialectical (jadali). The author begins by reiterating the definition of a syllogism: it is a discourse in which, when certain statements are posited, another statement necessarily follows. All syllogisms share this formal structure. However, they differ in their subject matter (الموضوعات). Some subject matters are natural (في الطبيعة), i.e., they correspond to the real nature of things, while others are conventional (بحسب الواضع), i.e., they are assumed by the positor or disputant. The demonstrative syllogism is the one whose subject is according to nature; the author notes that he has already treated it. Any syllogism that follows (i.e., other types) must be contrary to it in that its subject is not necessarily in nature. These other syllogisms are distinguished from demonstration by the nature of their premises: they are more general than demonstrative premises. The author recalls that all indemonstrable (immediate) demonstrative premises are well-known (مشهورة) and do not convert (لا تنعكس), meaning they are not subject to conversion or reversal. In contrast, the well-known premises used in dialectic are not like that; they do convert. This highlights a key difference: dialectical premises are accepted by the disputants and can be turned around, whereas demonstrative premises are fixed and certain.

Key Terms
القياس البرهاني
القياس الجدلي
المقدمات المشهورة

B) Its subject matter

Key Takeaway
Syllogisms are distinguished not by form but by subject matter and whether their premises convert.

The Syllogism

The Utility and Limitations of Dialectical Syllogism
Author explains when dialectical syllogism is useful and its limitations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the essential and accidental benefits of dialectical syllogism and identify when it is appropriate to use.

Core Idea
Dialectical syllogism benefits essentially only by undermining certitude, not by producing it.

In this passage, the author clarifies the proper role of dialectical syllogism. Unlike demonstration, which yields certainty, dialectical syllogism is used in debate and particular matters. Its primary purpose is not to gain knowledge for oneself but to engage with an opponent using premises they accept. Understanding this distinction helps you apply the right logical tool in Islamic theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of premises
Notice that the author distinguishes between conjectural (maznunat) and well-known (mashhurat) premises. Conjectural premises are based on opinion and are only useful for particular matters, not universal judgments.
2
Recognize the role of well-known premises in dialectic
Well-known premises are widely accepted and serve as the basis for dialectical syllogism. The author says it is better to use premises that are conceded by the opponent when demonstration is unavailable.
This is crucial for debate: you must use premises the opponent already accepts.
3
Understand the essential vs accidental benefit
The author states that dialectical syllogism benefits essentially only when the goal is to cast doubt on certitude. Otherwise, its benefit is accidental, occurring in a shared context with another person.
Essential benefit means it directly achieves its intended purpose; accidental means it happens as a side effect.
4
Note the warning about self-use
Do not imagine that dialectical syllogism can be used for oneself as a primary intention to gain certainty. Only demonstration produces certainty. What is below certainty is not true knowledge.
This prevents confusion between dialectic and demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلنطلب الآن مقدمات أقرب القياسات إلى البرهان، ولننظر إما حدها مظنونات فقط أو مشهورات فحد الظنون الصرفية إنما تفيد القياسات المعمولة منها في الأمور الجزئية. وأما الأحكام على الأمور الكلية فلا تنتفع فيها بالمظنونات الساذجة بل الأولى أن يكون أحكامنا على الأمور الكلية إذا تعذر علينا البرهان. ولو تعرض لغيره نحو القياس الجدلي فالأولى هو التعرض لمقدمات كانت مسلمة عند الخصم. ولا يتوهم أن هذا القياس قد ينتفع استعماله الإنسان مع نفسه بالقصد الأول؛ إذ المفيد لليقين هو البرهان؛ ليس إلا. وما دون اليقين فأكثره ظنُّ؛ وذلك مما يستلزم عدم الكمال وإنه هو الجهل بالحقيقة وإن أطلق عليه لفظ العلم، فإن العلم هو الذي لا يمكن أن يتصور على خلافه. والظن والوهم والشك كلها لا يكون كذلك، فإذن منفعته المخصوصة به في أمر مشترك بينه وبين غيره لكنه ينفع صاحبه لا بالذات بل بالعرض إن كانت العلية مطلقة عنده؛ وإنما ينتفع بالذات إذا كان المطلوب هو القدح في التيقن. وقد ينتفع إذا لم يجد من اليقينيات فيأخذ من المشهورات ما ينتج أحد طرفي اليقين. وكذلك يأخذ منها ما ينتج الطرف الآخر فلا يزال يترجح بينهما ترجيحا بعد ترجيح حتى ربما يلوج له الحق. ويخرج به إلى اليقين لكن هذا النفع لا يكون بما هو قياس؛ فإن نفع القياس بما هو قياس أنه ينتج. وكذلك النفع الأول فإن نفعه لا يكون لأنه ينتج بل لأنه ينتج نتيجة هو الغلبة. وقد ينتفع به في البرهان أيضًا لما أن اكتساب المقدّمات المشهورة وإعدادها أعم من اكتساب المقدمات البرهانية وإعدادها؛ والمشهور أعم من البرهاني فيتّفق له في اكتساب المشهورات وإعدادها أن ستكتسب البرهانية. فيتبين من جملة هذا أن النمط من القياسات لا ينفع الإنسان استعماله فيما بينه وبين نفسه بالذات بل إنما تكون منفعته على سبيل المخاطبة، ولا أن ينفع المخاطب في أن يكمل ذاته بل فيما يؤدي إلى تكميل ذاته بالقصد الثاني. وبالجملة فالقياس الجدلي نافع في سائر العلوم إما بالذات وإما بالعرض. ومن المعلوم أن استعمال القياس لا يكون نافعا إلا وأن يحصل غرض المستعمل من استعماله؛ وذلك بوجوه في المباحث العلمية. وقد علمت بأن المبحث قبل منع السائل مقدمة من مقدمات القياس غير المبحث بعد المنع لما أن البحث في المقدمة غير البحث في النتيجة؛ وهذا ظاهر.
Let us now seek the premises of the syllogisms closest to demonstration, and let us consider whether they are only conjectural (maznunat) or well-known (mashhurat). For the boundary of pure conjectures only yields syllogisms made from them in particular matters. But judgments on universal matters are not benefited by naive conjectures; rather, it is better for our judgments on universal matters, when demonstration is impossible for us, and if one engages in something other than it, such as the dialectical syllogism, then it is better to engage in premises that are conceded by the opponent. And let one not imagine that this syllogism can be used by a person with himself as a primary intention; for that which produces certainty is only demonstration. And what is below certainty is mostly conjecture; and that entails imperfection and it is ignorance of the truth, even if the term 'knowledge' is applied to it, for knowledge is that which cannot be conceived otherwise. Conjecture, illusion, and doubt are all not like that. So its specific benefit lies in a matter shared between him and another, but it benefits its user not essentially but accidentally if the causality is absolute for him; and it benefits essentially only if the desired outcome is to cast doubt on certitude. And it may also be beneficial if one does not find certainties, so he takes from well-known premises what produces one side of certainty. Similarly, he takes from them what produces the other side, and he continues to weigh between them, weighing after weighing, until perhaps the truth becomes clear to him, and he arrives at certainty. But this benefit is not insofar as it is a syllogism; for the benefit of a syllogism insofar as it is a syllogism is that it produces a conclusion. Similarly, the first benefit is not because it produces a conclusion, but because it produces a conclusion that is dominance. And it may also be beneficial in demonstration because acquiring well-known premises and preparing them is more general than acquiring demonstrative premises and preparing them; and the well-known is more general than the demonstrative, so it happens in acquiring well-known premises and preparing them that one acquires demonstrative ones. So it becomes clear from all this that this type of syllogism does not benefit a person in its use between himself and himself essentially, but rather its benefit is in the manner of addressing others, nor does it benefit the addressee in completing his essence, but rather in what leads to the completion of his essence secondarily. In summary, the dialectical syllogism is beneficial in all sciences, either essentially or accidentally. And it is known that using a syllogism is only beneficial if the user's purpose from its use is achieved; and that is in various ways in scientific investigations. And you have known that the investigation before denying a premise of the syllogism is different from the investigation after denial, because investigation into the premise is different from investigation into the conclusion; and this is clear.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage discusses the utility of the dialectical syllogism. The author begins by seeking the premises of syllogisms closest to demonstration, examining whether they are conjectural (مظنونات) or well-known (مشهورات). He states that pure conjectures only yield syllogisms applicable to particular matters, not universal judgments. For universal matters, when demonstration is unavailable, it is better to use premises conceded by the opponent in dialectical debate. He then warns that one should not think that dialectical syllogism can be used for oneself as a primary intention to gain certainty; only demonstration produces certainty. What is below certainty (conjecture, illusion, doubt) is not true knowledge, which is defined as that which cannot be conceived otherwise. Therefore, the specific benefit of dialectical syllogism is in a shared context between two parties; it benefits the user accidentally, not essentially, unless the goal is to undermine certainty. The author then describes how dialectical syllogism can lead to certainty indirectly: if one lacks certain premises, he takes well-known premises that produce one side of a certainty, then takes others that produce the opposite side, and by weighing them repeatedly, truth may become apparent and lead to certainty. However, this benefit is not due to the syllogism as a syllogism (which simply produces a conclusion), but because it produces a conclusion that is dominant or overwhelming. Additionally, dialectical syllogism benefits demonstration because acquiring and preparing well-known premises is more general than acquiring demonstrative ones; thus, in acquiring well-known premises, one might also acquire demonstrative ones. The conclusion is that dialectical syllogism does not benefit a person when used by himself essentially, but only in addressing others, and it does not directly perfect the addressee's essence but leads to it secondarily. Overall, it is beneficial in all sciences either essentially or accidentally. The author also notes that investigating a premise before denying it is different from investigating after denial, as the focus shifts from premise to conclusion.

المشهورات
اليقين

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
How Dialectical Syllogism Can Lead to Certainty Indirectly
Author explains how dialectical syllogism can indirectly lead to certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the indirect path from dialectical syllogism to certainty through weighing well-known premises and describe its secondary benefit in acquiring demonstrative premises.

Core Idea
Dialectical syllogism can lead to certainty indirectly by weighing opposite conclusions from well-known premises.

The author explains that even though dialectical syllogism does not produce certainty directly, it can still lead to certainty through a process of weighing well-known premises that yield opposite sides of a certainty. This indirect benefit, along with its role in preparing demonstrative premises, makes dialectical syllogism valuable across the sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario for indirect certainty
When one does not have certain premises, he uses well-known premises (mashhurat) to produce one side of a certainty (e.g., a probable conclusion). Then he takes other well-known premises that produce the opposite side.
This is not a single syllogism but a series of arguments.
2
Understand the weighing process
The person continually weighs the two conclusions against each other (ترجيحا بعد ترجيح) until the truth becomes clear and he arrives at certainty. This is a dialectical method, not a demonstrative one.
The weighing is based on strength of evidence, not formal proof.
3
Distinguish the benefit from the syllogism as such
The benefit here is not because the syllogism produces a conclusion (which is its nature), but because it produces a conclusion that is dominant or overwhelming (الغلبة).
This is a practical benefit, not a logical one.
4
Recognize the secondary benefit for demonstration
Acquiring well-known premises is more general than acquiring demonstrative premises. In the process of collecting well-known premises, one may also come across demonstrative ones, thus benefiting demonstration.
Well-known premises are a broader category that includes some demonstrative premises.
5
Summarize the overall utility
Dialectical syllogism is beneficial in all sciences either essentially (by undermining certitude) or accidentally (by leading to certainty or aiding demonstration).
The indirect path to certainty is an accidental benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلنطلب الآن مقدمات أقرب القياسات إلى البرهان، ولننظر إما حدها مظنونات فقط أو مشهورات فحد الظنون الصرفية إنما تفيد القياسات المعمولة منها في الأمور الجزئية. وأما الأحكام على الأمور الكلية فلا تنتفع فيها بالمظنونات الساذجة بل الأولى أن يكون أحكامنا على الأمور الكلية إذا تعذر علينا البرهان. ولو تعرض لغيره نحو القياس الجدلي فالأولى هو التعرض لمقدمات كانت مسلمة عند الخصم. ولا يتوهم أن هذا القياس قد ينتفع استعماله الإنسان مع نفسه بالقصد الأول؛ إذ المفيد لليقين هو البرهان؛ ليس إلا. وما دون اليقين فأكثره ظنُّ؛ وذلك مما يستلزم عدم الكمال وإنه هو الجهل بالحقيقة وإن أطلق عليه لفظ العلم، فإن العلم هو الذي لا يمكن أن يتصور على خلافه. والظن والوهم والشك كلها لا يكون كذلك، فإذن منفعته المخصوصة به في أمر مشترك بينه وبين غيره لكنه ينفع صاحبه لا بالذات بل بالعرض إن كانت العلية مطلقة عنده؛ وإنما ينتفع بالذات إذا كان المطلوب هو القدح في التيقن. وقد ينتفع إذا لم يجد من اليقينيات فيأخذ من المشهورات ما ينتج أحد طرفي اليقين. وكذلك يأخذ منها ما ينتج الطرف الآخر فلا يزال يترجح بينهما ترجيحا بعد ترجيح حتى ربما يلوج له الحق. ويخرج به إلى اليقين لكن هذا النفع لا يكون بما هو قياس؛ فإن نفع القياس بما هو قياس أنه ينتج. وكذلك النفع الأول فإن نفعه لا يكون لأنه ينتج بل لأنه ينتج نتيجة هو الغلبة. وقد ينتفع به في البرهان أيضًا لما أن اكتساب المقدّمات المشهورة وإعدادها أعم من اكتساب المقدمات البرهانية وإعدادها؛ والمشهور أعم من البرهاني فيتّفق له في اكتساب المشهورات وإعدادها أن ستكتسب البرهانية. فيتبين من جملة هذا أن النمط من القياسات لا ينفع الإنسان استعماله فيما بينه وبين نفسه بالذات بل إنما تكون منفعته على سبيل المخاطبة، ولا أن ينفع المخاطب في أن يكمل ذاته بل فيما يؤدي إلى تكميل ذاته بالقصد الثاني. وبالجملة فالقياس الجدلي نافع في سائر العلوم إما بالذات وإما بالعرض. ومن المعلوم أن استعمال القياس لا يكون نافعا إلا وأن يحصل غرض المستعمل من استعماله؛ وذلك بوجوه في المباحث العلمية. وقد علمت بأن المبحث قبل منع السائل مقدمة من مقدمات القياس غير المبحث بعد المنع لما أن البحث في المقدمة غير البحث في النتيجة؛ وهذا ظاهر.
Let us now seek the premises of the syllogisms closest to demonstration, and let us consider whether they are only conjectural (maznunat) or well-known (mashhurat). For the boundary of pure conjectures only yields syllogisms made from them in particular matters. But judgments on universal matters are not benefited by naive conjectures; rather, it is better for our judgments on universal matters, when demonstration is impossible for us, and if one engages in something other than it, such as the dialectical syllogism, then it is better to engage in premises that are conceded by the opponent. And let one not imagine that this syllogism can be used by a person with himself as a primary intention; for that which produces certainty is only demonstration. And what is below certainty is mostly conjecture; and that entails imperfection and it is ignorance of the truth, even if the term 'knowledge' is applied to it, for knowledge is that which cannot be conceived otherwise. Conjecture, illusion, and doubt are all not like that. So its specific benefit lies in a matter shared between him and another, but it benefits its user not essentially but accidentally if the causality is absolute for him; and it benefits essentially only if the desired outcome is to cast doubt on certitude. And it may also be beneficial if one does not find certainties, so he takes from well-known premises what produces one side of certainty. Similarly, he takes from them what produces the other side, and he continues to weigh between them, weighing after weighing, until perhaps the truth becomes clear to him, and he arrives at certainty. But this benefit is not insofar as it is a syllogism; for the benefit of a syllogism insofar as it is a syllogism is that it produces a conclusion. Similarly, the first benefit is not because it produces a conclusion, but because it produces a conclusion that is dominance. And it may also be beneficial in demonstration because acquiring well-known premises and preparing them is more general than acquiring demonstrative premises and preparing them; and the well-known is more general than the demonstrative, so it happens in acquiring well-known premises and preparing them that one acquires demonstrative ones. So it becomes clear from all this that this type of syllogism does not benefit a person in its use between himself and himself essentially, but rather its benefit is in the manner of addressing others, nor does it benefit the addressee in completing his essence, but rather in what leads to the completion of his essence secondarily. In summary, the dialectical syllogism is beneficial in all sciences, either essentially or accidentally. And it is known that using a syllogism is only beneficial if the user's purpose from its use is achieved; and that is in various ways in scientific investigations. And you have known that the investigation before denying a premise of the syllogism is different from the investigation after denial, because investigation into the premise is different from investigation into the conclusion; and this is clear.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage discusses the utility of the dialectical syllogism. The author begins by seeking the premises of syllogisms closest to demonstration, examining whether they are conjectural (مظنونات) or well-known (مشهورات). He states that pure conjectures only yield syllogisms applicable to particular matters, not universal judgments. For universal matters, when demonstration is unavailable, it is better to use premises conceded by the opponent in dialectical debate. He then warns that one should not think that dialectical syllogism can be used for oneself as a primary intention to gain certainty; only demonstration produces certainty. What is below certainty (conjecture, illusion, doubt) is not true knowledge, which is defined as that which cannot be conceived otherwise. Therefore, the specific benefit of dialectical syllogism is in a shared context between two parties; it benefits the user accidentally, not essentially, unless the goal is to undermine certainty. The author then describes how dialectical syllogism can lead to certainty indirectly: if one lacks certain premises, he takes well-known premises that produce one side of a certainty, then takes others that produce the opposite side, and by weighing them repeatedly, truth may become apparent and lead to certainty. However, this benefit is not due to the syllogism as a syllogism (which simply produces a conclusion), but because it produces a conclusion that is dominant or overwhelming. Additionally, dialectical syllogism benefits demonstration because acquiring and preparing well-known premises is more general than acquiring demonstrative ones; thus, in acquiring well-known premises, one might also acquire demonstrative ones. The conclusion is that dialectical syllogism does not benefit a person when used by himself essentially, but only in addressing others, and it does not directly perfect the addressee's essence but leads to it secondarily. Overall, it is beneficial in all sciences either essentially or accidentally. The author also notes that investigating a premise before denying it is different from investigating after denial, as the focus shifts from premise to conclusion.

Distinguishing Dialectical from Demonstrative Syllogisms
Author defines dialectical syllogisms and categorizes types of common premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate dialectical syllogisms from demonstrative syllogisms based on the source and certainty of their premises.

Core Idea
Demonstration builds on certain principles; dialectic builds on accepted opinions.

In this passage, the author sharpens the boundary between two central types of syllogisms: those that produce certain knowledge (burhān) and those that produce persuasion through common acceptance (jadal). Understanding this distinction is key to knowing which logical tool to deploy in different Islamic intellectual contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of syllogisms under comparison
The passage explicitly contrasts 'al-qiyās al-burhānī' (demonstrative) and 'al-qiyās al-jadalī' (dialectical). Note that both are universal in conclusion.
2
Compare the source of their premises
Demonstrative premises come from 'al-awā’il' (primary intellectual principles) or what is derived from them. Dialectical premises come from 'al-mashhūrāt' (common/prevalent opinions).
This is the fundamental epistemic difference.
3
Understand the criterion of truth vs. acceptance
The author states: 'al-ḥaqq yunẓaru ilayhi fī nafsih, wa al-shuhra yunẓaru ilayhi min ḥaythu al-ta‘āruf'. Truth is considered in itself; commonness is considered from the angle of common acceptance.
This clarifies why dialectical premises are not inherently false, but are accepted by a group.
4
Recognise the scope of dialectical premises
The passage lists five subtypes of common premises, showing that 'commonness' varies from universal public acceptance to specific school doctrines. Not all common premises are equal.
This nuance prevents overgeneralisation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في التفرقة بين القياسات الجدلية وغيرها من القياسات الكلية النتائج أما القياس المطلق فقد تحققته، والصنف البرهاني منه أيضًا؛ والجدلي كذلك وبالجملة ما هو . وإنه كيف يخالف البرهان فإن البرهاني مقدماته من الأوائل في العقل أو بيئته من الأوائل، والجدلي عن المشهورات؛ والحق يُنظر إليه في نفسه، والشهرة ينظر إليه من حيث التعارف لتسليمه: فمنه المشهور المطلق الذي هو مسلم عند الجمهور، ومنه ما هو مسلم عند أكثرهم، ومنه ما هو مشهور محمود عند الفلاسفة، ومنه ما هو مشهور محمود عند أكثر علمائهم كما أن السماء كرية، ومنه ما هو مشهور محمود عند أصحاب المعلّم الأوّل من أن الفلك طبيعة خامسة؛ وهذه المشهورة بحسب عدة من الحكماء. وما يكون مخالفا لما عليه المشهور المطلق فلا ينتفع به في القياسات الجدلية المطلقة. والقياسات الجدلية منها ما هي مطلقة يخاطب بها الجمهور ومن يجري مجراهم، ومنها ما يخاطب به صاحب رأي خاص ولما كانت القياسات الخطابية والشعرية خارجة عن الانتفاع بها في المخاطبات التي تنحو نحو الأمور الكلية. فليس بنا كثرة حاجة إلى الفرق بين الجدلية وبينها لكن ما يشبه للجدل من المقاييس فهي البرهانية؛ وقد عرفت الفرق بينهما . وبعد البرهانية المشاغبية والسوفسطائية والعنادية والامتحانية لكن المشاغبية لا تخالف العنادية إلا بالقصد والغرض.
Chapter: On the distinction between dialectical syllogisms and other syllogisms that produce universal conclusions. As for the absolute syllogism, you have verified it; likewise the demonstrative type from it; and the dialectical likewise and in brief what it is. And how it differs from demonstration: for the demonstrative's premises are from primary principles in the intellect or derived from primary principles, while the dialectical is from common premises; truth is considered in itself, while commonness is considered from the aspect of common acceptance for its admission. Among it: the absolute common which is accepted by the public; and among it what is accepted by most of them; and among it what is commonly praised among the philosophers; and among it what is commonly praised among most of their scholars, like 'the heavens are spherical'; and among it what is commonly praised among the followers of the First Teacher (Aristotle) such as 'the celestial sphere is a fifth nature'; these are common according to a number of sages. Whatever opposes what is absolutely common is not beneficial in absolute dialectical syllogisms. Among dialectical syllogisms, some are absolute, used to address the public and those like them, and some are used to address a holder of a specific opinion. Since rhetorical and poetical syllogisms are outside the scope of benefiting from them in discourses that aim at universal matters, we do not have much need to distinguish between dialectical and them; but what resembles dialectical among syllogisms is demonstrative; and you have known the distinction between them. After demonstrative are the contentious, sophistical, obstinate, and test syllogisms, but the contentious does not differ from the obstinate except in intention and purpose.
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This chapter formally defines dialectical syllogisms and distinguishes them from others. The author begins by stating that the absolute syllogism, the demonstrative type, and the dialectical type have already been understood. The key difference is that demonstrative premises are derived from primary intellectual principles (al-awa'il), while dialectical premises are common premises (mashhurat). Truth is considered in itself, whereas commonness relies on general acceptance. The author then enumerates five subtypes of common premises: (1) absolutely common, accepted by the general public; (2) accepted by the majority; (3) praised among philosophers; (4) praised among most scholars (e.g., 'the heavens are spherical'); (5) praised among followers of Aristotle (e.g., 'the celestial sphere is a fifth nature'). Any premise contradicting the absolutely common is useless in absolute dialectical reasoning. Dialectical syllogisms themselves are of two kinds: absolute, for the public, and specific, for those with a particular opinion. Rhetorical and poetical syllogisms are set aside as irrelevant to universal matters. The author clarifies that the only syllogism that resembles dialectic in terms of being demonstrative is the demonstrative one; other types like contentious (mushaghahiyya), sophistical (sufista'iyya), obstinate ('inadiyya), and test (imtihaniyya) are distinguished later, noting that contentious and obstinate differ only in intent.

القياس البرهاني
القياس الجدلي
المشهورات

Key Takeaway
Demonstrative premises are certain; dialectical premises are common—this epistemic difference defines their use.

Classifying Common Premises (Mashhurat) in Dialectic
Author defines dialectical syllogisms and categorizes types of common premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and categorize the five types of common premises (mashhūrāt) that the author enumerates for dialectical syllogisms.

Core Idea
Common premises range from universal public acceptance to specialized school doctrines—each with a different persuasive power.

The passage not only distinguishes dialectical from demonstrative premises but also provides a detailed classification of common premises. This taxonomy helps the logician and debater choose the right premise for the right audience, from the general public to the followers of Aristotle.

Guided Path
1
List the five types of common premises
Read the passage carefully and extract each of the five subtypes. The author uses 'minhu...' to enumerate them.
Write them in order: (1) absolutely common, (2) common to most, (3) common among philosophers, (4) common among their scholars, (5) common among followers of Aristotle.
2
Understand the scope of each type
Note the change in scope: from 'the public' (jamhūr) to 'most of them' (aktharihim) to 'philosophers' (falāsifa) to 'their scholars' (ulamā'ihim) to 'followers of the First Teacher' (aṣḥāb al-mu‘allim al-awwal).
This shows that 'common' is graded by community size and specificity.
3
Identify examples given for types 4 and 5
Type 4 example: 'the heavens are spherical' (al-samā’ kuriya). Type 5 example: 'the celestial sphere is a fifth nature' (al-falak ṭabī‘a khāmissa).
These are widely accepted within their respective groups, not necessarily true in themselves.
4
Recognise the condition for absolute dialectical syllogisms
The author states that any premise contradicting the absolutely common (al-mashhūr al-muṭlaq) is useless in absolute dialectical syllogisms.
Thus, absolute dialectic must use premises accepted by the general public.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is an example of a premise that is 'commonly praised among most of their scholars'?
A) The world is contingent
B) The heavens are spherical
C) The celestial sphere is a fifth nature
D) The public accepts that fire burns

Key Takeaway
Common premises are not monolithic; they range from absolute public acceptance to specialized school doctrines, and must be chosen according to the audience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Categorize the following premise according to the five types: 'The sun rises in the east' – is it absolutely common, common to most, or specific to a group? Justify.
?
Imagine you are in a debate with a follower of Aristotle. Which of the five premise types would be most persuasive to him? Provide an example from the passage or your own.

The Syllogism

Dialectical vs Demonstrative Syllogisms
Author contrasts demonstrative and dialectical syllogisms based on their premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish dialectical syllogisms from demonstrative syllogisms by identifying the source of their premises.

Core Idea
Knowledge comes from self-evident principles; debate comes from common opinion.

The author has already explained the absolute syllogism. Now he clarifies the crucial distinction between the two universal syllogisms: demonstration (burhān) and dialectic (jadal). Their difference lies entirely in the material: what kind of propositions they use.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of demonstrative syllogism
Find the sentence: 'the demonstrative has its premises from primary principles in the intellect or from what is derived from primary principles'
2
Locate the definition of dialectical syllogism
Find the sentence: 'the dialectical is from generally accepted statements (mashhūrāt)'
3
Understand the epistemic difference
Truth is considered in itself; general acceptance is considered in terms of common knowledge
This is the key difference in the aim of each syllogism.

فصل : في التفرقة بين القياسات الجدلية وغيرها من القياسات الكلية النتائج أما القياس المطلق فقد تحققته، والصنف البرهاني منه أيضًا؛ والجدلي كذلك وبالجملة ما هو . وإنه كيف يخالف البرهان فإن البرهاني مقدماته من الأوائل في العقل أو بيئته من الأوائل، والجدلي عن المشهورات؛ والحق يُنظر إليه في نفسه، والشهرة ينظر إليه من حيث التعارف لتسليمه: [أ] فمنه المشهور المطلق الذي هو مسلم عند الجمهور، [ب] ومنه ما هو مسلم عند أكثر هم [ت] ومنه ما هو مشهور محمود عند الفلاسفة، [ث] ومنه ما هو مشهور محمود عند أكثر علمائهم كما أن السماء كرية [ج] ومنه ما هو مشهور محمود عند أصحاب المعلّم الأوّل من أن الفلك طبيعة خامسة؛ وهذه المشهورة بحسب عدة من الحكماء. وما يكون مخالفا لما عليه المشهور المطلق فلا ينتفع به في القياسات الجدلية المطلقة. والقياسات الجدلية منها ما هي مطلقة يخاطب بها الجمهور ومن يجري مجراهم، ومنها ما يخاطب به صاحب رأي خاص ولما كانت القياسات الخطابية والشعرية خارجة عن الانتفاع بها في المخاطبات التي تنحو نحو الأمور الكلية. فليس بنا كثرة حاجة إلى الفرق بين الجدلية وبينها لكن ما يشبه للجدل من المقاييس فهي البرهانية؛ وقد عرفت الفرق بينهما . وبعد البرهانية المشاغبية والسوفسطائية والعنادية والامتحانية لكن المشاغبية لا تخالف العنادية إلا بالقصد والغرض. فأما في مادة القياس وصورته فحكمهما واحد. وكذلك السوفسطائية لا تخالف الامتحانية إلا بالقصد والغرض والمشاغبية هي إما من جهة المادة؛ وذلك أن المقدّمات ليست مشهورة على الحقيقة ولكنها مشهورة على الظاهر أو مشبّه بالمشهورة، فهذا إذا كانت صورته منتجة صلح أن يقال له قياس لأنه قول كذا وكذا. وأما من جهة الصورة فإن يكون القياس غير منتج لكن المقدّمات مشهورة؛ وإنه لا يسمَّى قياسًا لأنه له حد القياس وإن كان قياسًا عند المماري كما يقال سفينة حجر، ولا يقال سفينة من حجر لكنه إذا قَبِلَ فَهِمَ أنه ليس يعني أنه سفينة بالحقيقة بل أنه مشابه بوجه ما للسفينة: فهذه الأنواع من الأقيسة أعني المشبَّهة بالقياسات الجدلية إذا كانت الغاية فيها نفس العلية لا لأنها نفس العلية فقط بل ليظهر عجز المدعي سميت عنادية. وإذا استعملها المغالطون ليوقعوا الغلط سميت مشاغبية، وإذا استعملها الحكماء ليستوضح محل المدعي الكاذب سميت امتحانية. والجدلية قد تشاركها الامتحانية والعنادية في الموضوع؛ وذلك لأنهما في المشهورات لكن يفارق كل واحدة منهما الجدلية من حيث الغاية؛ إذ استعمال المشهورات في العلوم مغالطة لكنها يفارق السوفسطائية أيضًا في الغرض والغاية. وكل واحد من هذه الأقيسة يجيء من بعد كما ينبغي. وأما ما يكون مخصوصا باسم السوفسطائي من القياسات المشبهة فنسبته من حيث المادة إلى القياس البرهاني تشبه المماري إلى الجدلي؛ وهذا القياس يخالف البرهان بأن البرهان أصل وحق. وهذا مشبه به ويخالف الجدلي أيضًا بأن الجدلي لا يأخذ المقدّمات على أنه حق في نفس الأمر بل من جهة الشهرة، ولا يأخذها على أنها مناسبة المبادي، وهذا يأخذها على أنه حق في نفس الأمر؛ وإنها مناسبة ويخالف المشاغبي بأنه ليس يأخذ مقدماته على أنها مشهورة أو مسلمة بل لأنه يأخذها على أنها حقه ويكذب فيها.
Chapter: On the distinction between dialectical syllogisms and other syllogisms with universal conclusions. As for the absolute syllogism, you have understood it, and also the demonstrative kind; and likewise the dialectical, and in general what it is. And how it differs from demonstration: the demonstrative has its premises from primary principles in the intellect or from what is derived from primary principles, while the dialectical is from generally accepted statements (mashhūrāt). Truth is considered in itself, while general acceptance is considered in terms of common knowledge for its acceptance. A) Among them is the absolutely generally accepted, which is accepted by the public. B) Among them is what is accepted by most of them. C) Among them is what is generally accepted and praised among philosophers. D) Among them is what is generally accepted and praised among most of their scholars, such as that the heaven is spherical. E) Among them is what is generally accepted and praised among the followers of the First Teacher, that the celestial sphere is a fifth nature. These generally accepted statements are according to a number of sages. What contradicts the absolutely generally accepted is not useful in absolute dialectical syllogisms. Dialectical syllogisms are either absolute, addressed to the public and those like them, or addressed to a particular opinion holder. Since rhetorical and poetical syllogisms fall outside usefulness in discourses that tend toward universal matters, we have no great need to distinguish between dialectical and them. But what resembles dialectical among syllogisms are the demonstrative; and you have known the difference between them. After demonstrative are the contentious (mushāghabiyya), sophistical (sūfistā'iyya), contestational (ʻinādiyya), and examinational (imtiḥāniyya). But the contentious does not differ from the contestational except in intention and purpose. As for the matter and form of the syllogism, their ruling is one. Likewise, the sophistical does not differ from the examinational except in intention and purpose. The contentious is either from the aspect of matter: that the premises are not actually generally accepted but are apparently generally accepted or resemble generally accepted; so if its form is productive, it is fitting to call it a syllogism because it is a certain statement. As for from the aspect of form: the syllogism is not productive but the premises are generally accepted; and it is not called a syllogism because it does not have the definition of syllogism, although it is a syllogism according to the contentious person, just as one says 'stone ship' and does not say 'ship made of stone', but if one accepts it, he understands that it does not mean a ship in reality but something resembling a ship in some way. These types of syllogisms, namely those resembling dialectical syllogisms, if the goal in them is merely opposition, not because it is merely opposition, but to show the incapacity of the claimant, they are called contestational. If the sophists use them to cause error, they are called contentious. If the sages use them to clarify the position of the false claimant, they are called examinational. Dialectical may share with examinational and contestational in subject matter, because they are in generally accepted statements, but each of them differs from dialectical in terms of purpose; for using generally accepted statements in the sciences is a fallacy, but it also differs from sophistical in purpose and goal. Each of these syllogisms will come later as appropriate. As for what is specifically called sophistical among the resembling syllogisms, its relation in terms of matter to the demonstrative syllogism resembles the contentious to the dialectical. This syllogism differs from demonstration in that demonstration is original and true, while this resembles it. It also differs from the dialectical in that the dialectical does not take premises as true in themselves but from the aspect of general acceptance, and does not take them as appropriate to principles, while this takes them as true in themselves and as appropriate. It differs from the contentious in that it does not take its premises as generally accepted or admitted, but rather takes them as true and falsifies them.
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definitional discussion distinguishing dialectical syllogisms from other types of syllogisms. The author begins by recalling that the absolute syllogism, the demonstrative, and the dialectical have been understood. He then explains how demonstrative syllogisms differ from dialectical: demonstrative premises are derived from primary principles of the intellect, whereas dialectical premises are from commonly accepted statements (mashhūrāt). Truth is considered in itself, while common acceptance is considered in terms of general agreement. The author enumerates several types of commonly accepted statements: absolutely accepted by the public, accepted by the majority, praised among philosophers (e.g., the heaven is spherical), praised among most scholars (e.g., the celestial sphere is a fifth nature). Any premise contradicting the absolutely accepted is useless in absolute dialectical syllogisms. Dialectical syllogisms themselves are either absolute (addressed to the public) or directed to a particular opinion holder. Rhetorical and poetical syllogisms are excluded because they do not tend toward universal matters. The author then introduces contentious, sophistical, contestational, and examinational syllogisms, noting that contentious and contestational differ only in purpose, as do sophistical and examinational. The distinction is based on matter (premises that are apparently commonly accepted) and form (unproductive syllogisms with commonly accepted premises). Examples illustrate the misuse of terms. Finally, the author compares the sophistical syllogism to the demonstrative in matter, but clarifies that it is merely resemblance, not truth. The commentary explains that the author's aim is to prevent confusion between types of syllogisms, especially the dialectical and those that resemble it, by clarifying their material and formal differences as well as their purposes. An example from kalam: in a dialectical debate, the premise 'the world is temporally originated' is commonly accepted among theologians, but a demonstrative proof would require primary premises like 'everything originated has a cause'.

Key Terms
القياس الجدلي
القياس البرهاني
المشهورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Generally accepted statements.
B) Primary principles of the intellect.
C) Poetic images.
D) Sophistical deceptions.

Key Takeaway
Demonstrative syllogisms use self-evident premises; dialectical syllogisms use commonly accepted premises.

Categories of Commonly Accepted Statements (Mashhūrāt)
Author lists and explains the five types of mashhūrāt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five categories of mashhūrāt given in the passage and identify which are useful in absolute dialectic.

Core Idea
Not all popular opinions are equal; some are universally accepted, some only among experts.

The author presents a detailed classification of generally accepted statements (mashhūrāt), which form the material of dialectical syllogisms. Understanding these categories helps us know which premises are admissible in absolute dialectical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of categories
The author enumerates five types, labelled (أ) through (ج) in the text. Find them.
2
Understand each type
Type A: absolutely accepted by the public. Type B: accepted by most of the public. Type C: accepted among philosophers. Type D: accepted among most scholars (e.g., heaven is spherical). Type E: accepted among followers of the First Teacher (Aristotle), e.g., the celestial sphere is a fifth nature.
Note that types C, D, E are specifically praised among certain groups.
3
Assess admissibility in absolute dialectic
The author says that what contradicts the absolutely generally accepted (type A) is not useful in absolute dialectical syllogisms.
So only premises that do not contradict type A are admissible.

The Syllogism
advanced
5 min
Contentious, Contestational, Examinational, and Sophistical Syllogisms
Author distinguishes non-demonstrative syllogisms by intention and purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between contentious, contestational, examinational, and sophistical syllogisms based on their purpose and material.

Core Idea
The same flawed syllogism can be used for different ends: to trick, to test, or to show inability.

After discussing demonstrative and dialectical syllogisms, the author turns to other types that resemble them in form or matter but differ in purpose. These include contentious (mushāghabiyya), contestational (ʻinādiyya), examinational (imtiḥāniyya), and sophistical (sūfistā'iyya) syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types after demonstrative
Find where the list says 'After demonstrative are the contentious, sophistical, contestational, and examinational'
2
Distinguish between matter and form
The author explains that contentious can be due to matter (premises not actually generally accepted but appearing so) or form (unproductive form but premises are generally accepted).
Examples: 'stone ship' analogy.
3
Understand the purpose differentiation
The author says the same syllogism is called contentious if used to cause error, contestational if to show incapacity, examinational if to test a false claimant. Sophistical is specifically designed to deceive by appearing demonstrative.
Contestational and examinational share the same subject matter as dialectical (mashhūrāt) but differ in purpose.

فصل : في التفرقة بين القياسات الجدلية وغيرها من القياسات الكلية النتائج أما القياس المطلق فقد تحققته، والصنف البرهاني منه أيضًا؛ والجدلي كذلك وبالجملة ما هو . وإنه كيف يخالف البرهان فإن البرهاني مقدماته من الأوائل في العقل أو بيئته من الأوائل، والجدلي عن المشهورات؛ والحق يُنظر إليه في نفسه، والشهرة ينظر إليه من حيث التعارف لتسليمه: [أ] فمنه المشهور المطلق الذي هو مسلم عند الجمهور، [ب] ومنه ما يخاطب به صاحب رأي خاص ولما كانت القياسات الخطابية والشعرية خارجة عن الانتفاع بها في المخاطبات التي تنحو نحو الأمور الكلية. فليس بنا كثرة حاجة إلى الفرق بين الجدلية وبينها لكن ما يشبه للجدل من المقاييس فهي البرهانية؛ وقد عرفت الفرق بينهما . وبعد البرهانية المشاغبية والسوفسطائية والعنادية والامتحانية لكن المشاغبية لا تخالف العنادية إلا بالقصد والغرض. فأما في مادة القياس وصورته فحكمهما واحد. وكذلك السوفسطائية لا تخالف الامتحانية إلا بالقصد والغرض والمشاغبية هي إما من جهة المادة؛ وذلك أن المقدّمات ليست مشهورة على الحقيقة ولكنها مشهورة على الظاهر أو مشبّه بالمشهورة، فهذا إذا كانت صورته منتجة صلح أن يقال له قياس لأنه قول كذا وكذا. وأما من جهة الصورة فإن يكون القياس غير منتج لكن المقدّمات مشهورة؛ وإنه لا يسمَّى قياسًا لأنه له حد القياس وإن كان قياسًا عند المماري كما يقال سفينة حجر، ولا يقال سفينة من حجر لكنه إذا قَبِلَ فَهِمَ أنه ليس يعني أنه سفينة بالحقيقة بل أنه مشابه بوجه ما للسفينة: فهذه الأنواع من الأقيسة أعني المشبَّهة بالقياسات الجدلية إذا كانت الغاية فيها نفس العلية لا لأنها نفس العلية فقط بل ليظهر عجز المدعي سميت عنادية. وإذا استعملها المغالطون ليوقعوا الغلط سميت مشاغبية، وإذا استعملها الحكماء ليستوضح محل المدعي الكاذب سميت امتحانية. والجدلية قد تشاركها الامتحانية والعنادية في الموضوع؛ وذلك لأنهما في المشهورات لكن يفارق كل واحدة منهما الجدلية من حيث الغاية؛ إذ استعمال المشهورات في العلوم مغالطة لكنها يفارق السوفسطائية أيضًا في الغرض والغاية. وكل واحد من هذه الأقيسة يجيء من بعد كما ينبغي. وأما ما يكون مخصوصا باسم السوفسطائي من القياسات المشبهة فنسبته من حيث المادة إلى القياس البرهاني تشبه المماري إلى الجدلي؛ وهذا القياس يخالف البرهان بأن البرهان أصل وحق. وهذا مشبه به ويخالف الجدلي أيضًا بأن الجدلي لا يأخذ المقدّمات على أنه حق في نفس الأمر بل من جهة الشهرة، ولا يأخذها على أنها مناسبة المبادي، وهذا يأخذها على أنه حق في نفس الأمر؛ وإنها مناسبة ويخالف المشاغبي بأنه ليس يأخذ مقدماته على أنها مشهورة أو مسلمة بل لأنه يأخذها على أنها حقه ويكذب فيها.
Chapter: On the distinction between dialectical syllogisms and other syllogisms with universal conclusions. As for the absolute syllogism, you have understood it, and also the demonstrative kind; and likewise the dialectical, and in general what it is. And how it differs from demonstration: the demonstrative has its premises from primary principles in the intellect or from what is derived from primary principles, while the dialectical is from generally accepted statements (mashhūrāt). Truth is considered in itself, while general acceptance is considered in terms of common knowledge for its acceptance. A) Among them is the absolutely generally accepted, which is accepted by the public. B) Among them is what is accepted by most of them. C) Among them is what is generally accepted and praised among philosophers. D) Among them is what is generally accepted and praised among most of their scholars, such as that the heaven is spherical. E) Among them is what is generally accepted and praised among the followers of the First Teacher, that the celestial sphere is a fifth nature. These generally accepted statements are according to a number of sages. What contradicts the absolutely generally accepted is not useful in absolute dialectical syllogisms. Dialectical syllogisms are either absolute, addressed to the public and those like them, or addressed to a particular opinion holder. Since rhetorical and poetical syllogisms fall outside usefulness in discourses that tend toward universal matters, we have no great need to distinguish between dialectical and them. But what resembles dialectical among syllogisms are the demonstrative; and you have known the difference between them. After demonstrative are the contentious (mushāghabiyya), sophistical (sūfistā'iyya), contestational (ʻinādiyya), and examinational (imtiḥāniyya). But the contentious does not differ from the contestational except in intention and purpose. As for the matter and form of the syllogism, their ruling is one. Likewise, the sophistical does not differ from the examinational except in intention and purpose. The contentious is either from the aspect of matter: that the premises are not actually generally accepted but are apparently generally accepted or resemble generally accepted; so if its form is productive, it is fitting to call it a syllogism because it is a certain statement. As for from the aspect of form: the syllogism is not productive but the premises are generally accepted; and it is not called a syllogism because it does not have the definition of syllogism, although it is a syllogism according to the contentious person, just as one says 'stone ship' and does not say 'ship made of stone', but if one accepts it, he understands that it does not mean a ship in reality but something resembling a ship in some way. These types of syllogisms, namely those resembling dialectical syllogisms, if the goal in them is merely opposition, not because it is merely opposition, but to show the incapacity of the claimant, they are called contestational. If the sophists use them to cause error, they are called contentious. If the sages use them to clarify the position of the false claimant, they are called examinational. Dialectical may share with examinational and contestational in subject matter, because they are in generally accepted statements, but each of them differs from dialectical in terms of purpose; for using generally accepted statements in the sciences is a fallacy, but it also differs from sophistical in purpose and goal. Each of these syllogisms will come later as appropriate. As for what is specifically called sophistical among the resembling syllogisms, its relation in terms of matter to the demonstrative syllogism resembles the contentious to the dialectical. This syllogism differs from demonstration in that demonstration is original and true, while this resembles it. It also differs from the dialectical in that the dialectical does not take premises as true in themselves but from the aspect of general acceptance, and does not take them as appropriate to principles, while this takes them as true in themselves and as appropriate. It differs from the contentious in that it does not take its premises as generally accepted or admitted, but rather takes them as true and falsifies them.
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definitional discussion distinguishing dialectical syllogisms from other types of syllogisms. The author begins by recalling that the absolute syllogism, the demonstrative, and the dialectical have been understood. He then explains how demonstrative syllogisms differ from dialectical: demonstrative premises are derived from primary principles of the intellect, whereas dialectical premises are from commonly accepted statements (mashhūrāt). Truth is considered in itself, while common acceptance is considered in terms of general agreement. The author enumerates several types of commonly accepted statements: absolutely accepted by the public, accepted by the majority, praised among philosophers (e.g., the heaven is spherical), praised among most scholars (e.g., the celestial sphere is a fifth nature). Any premise contradicting the absolutely accepted is useless in absolute dialectical syllogisms. Dialectical syllogisms themselves are either absolute (addressed to the public) or directed to a particular opinion holder. Rhetorical and poetical syllogisms are excluded because they do not tend toward universal matters. The author then introduces contentious, sophistical, contestational, and examinational syllogisms, noting that contentious and contestational differ only in purpose, as do sophistical and examinational. The distinction is based on matter (premises that are apparently commonly accepted) and form (unproductive syllogisms with commonly accepted premises). Examples illustrate the misuse of terms. Finally, the author compares the sophistical syllogism to the demonstrative in matter, but clarifies that it is merely resemblance, not truth. The commentary explains that the author's aim is to prevent confusion between types of syllogisms, especially the dialectical and those that resemble it, by clarifying their material and formal differences as well as their purposes. An example from kalam: in a dialectical debate, the premise 'the world is temporally originated' is commonly accepted among theologians, but a demonstrative proof would require primary premises like 'everything originated has a cause'.

العنادية
الامتحانية
السوفسطائية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the contestational (ʻinādiyya) from the contentious (mushāghabiyya)?
A) The matter
B) The form
C) The purpose
D) The conclusion

Key Takeaway
Contentious, contestational, and examinational syllogisms share the same material and form; only the purpose differentiates them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
A debater uses a syllogism with premises that are only seemingly accepted by the opponent, to make the opponent look foolish. Which type of syllogism is this? (Answer: contestational).
?
Create an example of a sophistical syllogism in fiqh: e.g., 'Wine is forbidden; this drink is not wine; therefore it is not forbidden' (fallacy of denying the antecedent). Explain why it is sophistical.
