The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Three Types of Syllogisms Analogous to Dialectic
Classifies three types of syllogisms analogous to dialectic by purpose

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of syllogisms analogous to dialectic (eristic, contentious, examinatory) by identifying their purpose and material characteristics.

Core Idea
The same syllogistic form serves truth, deception, or examination depending on the user's purpose.

In this passage, the author explains that syllogisms similar to dialectical ones share the same material and form but differ in their ultimate purpose. He identifies three types: eristic (mushāghabiyya) used by sophists to cause error, contentious ('inādiyya) used to show the opponent's incapacity, and examinatory (imtiḥāniyya) used by wise men to test a false claim. Understanding this classification helps you see that logic is a neutral tool that can be applied for noble or ignoble ends, a key insight for navigating Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common foundation
Note that all three syllogisms share the same material (well-known premises, mashhūrāt, or what appears so) and the same form (productive syllogistic structure). This commonality is why they are called 'analogous to dialectical syllogisms'.
The passage begins: 'أما في مادة القياس وصورته فحكمهما واحد'.
2
Distinguish the three purposes
Find how each type is defined by its goal (ghāya): eristic aims at causing error, contentious aims at showing the claimant's incapacity, and examinatory aims at clarifying the false claimant's position. The passage lists each with the phrase 'سميت ...'.
Look for 'إذا كانت الغاية فيها ...' for contentious, 'استعملها المغالطون ...' for eristic, 'استعملها الحكماء ...' for examinatory.
3
Differentiate from dialectical and sophistical
Notice that all three share subject matter (mashhūrāt) with dialectical syllogisms, but differ in purpose. Also, the examinatory and contentious differ from sophistry in intention, though sophistry shares the material. The passage contrasts 'الغاية' for each.
The passage says: 'والجدلية قد تشاركها الامتحانية والعنادية في الموضوع ... لكن يفارق كل واحدة منهما الجدلية من حيث الغاية'.
4
Understand the material flaws
Distinguish two ways a syllogism can be defective: (1) in material: premises are not truly well-known but only appear so; (2) in form: the syllogism is non-productive even if premises are sound. This helps identify the eristic type precisely.
The passage splits the eristic into 'إما من جهة المادة' and 'وأما من جهة الصورة'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما في مادة القياس وصورته فحكمهما واحد. وكذلك السوفسطائية لا تخالف الامتحانية إلا بالقصد والغرض والمشاغبية هي إما من جهة المادة؛ وذلك أن المقدّمات ليست مشهورة على الحقيقة ولكنها مشهورة على الظاهر أو مشبّه بالمشهورة، فهذا إذا كانت صورته منتجة صلح أن يقال له قياس لأنه قول كذا وكذا. وأما من جهة الصورة فإن يكون القياس غير منتج لكن المقدّمات مشهورة؛ وإنه لا يسمَّى قياسًا لأنه له حد القياس وإن كان قياسًا عند المماري كما يقال سفينة حجر، ولا يقال سفينة من حجر لكنه إذا قَبِلَ فَهِمَ أنه ليس يعني أنه سفينة بالحقيقة بل أنه مشابه بوجه ما للسفينة: فهذه الأنواع من الأقيسة أعني المشبَّهة بالقياسات الجدلية إذا كانت الغاية فيها نفس العلية لا لأنها نفس العلية فقط بل ليظهر عجز المدعي سميت عنادية. وإذا استعملها المغالطون ليوقعوا الغلط سميت مشاغبية، وإذا استعملها الحكماء ليستوضح محل المدعي الكاذب سميت امتحانية. والجدلية قد تشاركها الامتحانية والعنادية في الموضوع؛ وذلك لأنهما في المشهورات لكن يفارق كل واحدة منهما الجدلية من حيث الغاية؛ إذ استعمال المشهورات في العلوم مغالطة لكنها يفارق السوفسطائية أيضًا في الغرض والغاية. وكل واحد من هذه الأقيسة يجيء من بعد كما ينبغي.
As for the material of the syllogism and its form, their ruling is one. Likewise, the sophistical does not differ from the examinatory except in intention and purpose. And the eristic is either from the aspect of material; that is because the premises are not well-known in reality but are well-known in appearance or resemble the well-known. So if its form is productive, it is suitable to be called a syllogism because it is a statement such-and-such. And from the aspect of form, the syllogism may be non-productive but the premises are well-known; and it is not called a syllogism because it does not have the definition of syllogism, even if it is considered a syllogism by the contentious person, just as it is said 'a stone ship' and it is not said 'a ship of stone', but if he accepts the term, he understands that he does not mean a ship in reality but rather something similar in some way to a ship. These types of syllogisms, I mean those analogous to dialectical syllogisms, if the goal in them is the very cause (al-illiyya) not because it is the cause itself, but rather to show the incapacity of the claimant, they are called contentious ('inādiyya). And if they are used by sophists to cause error, they are called eristic (mushāghabiyya). And if they are used by the wise to clarify the position of the false claimant, they are called examinatory (imtiḥāniyya). And dialectical shares with the examinatory and contentious in subject matter; that is because they both [deal with] well-knowns, but each of them differs from the dialectical in terms of purpose; because the use of well-knowns in sciences is deception, but it also differs from the sophistical in intention and purpose. And each of these syllogisms will come later as is appropriate.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage classifies different types of syllogisms that are analogous to dialectical syllogisms. The author begins by stating that the material and form of these syllogisms are the same as other types, but they differ in intention and purpose. He then distinguishes three types based on the purpose: (1) The examinatory (imtiḥāniyya) is used by wise men to clarify a false claim. (2) The eristic (mushāghabiyya) is used by sophists to cause error. (3) The contentious ('inādiyya) is used to show the opponent's incapacity, not because the syllogism itself is the cause but to reveal the claimant's weakness. The author emphasizes that these types share the use of well-known premises (mashhūrāt) with dialectical syllogisms, but differ in purpose. He also notes that using well-known premises in the context of sciences is actually deceptive, which is why the examinatory and contentious differ from the sophistical in intention. The passage concludes that each of these syllogisms will be discussed in due course. This classification is crucial for understanding that the same logical form can serve different ends, and that the moral purpose (gharad) distinguishes legitimate dialectical training from sophistry.

Key Terms
مشاغبية
عنادية
امتحانية
جدلية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the examinatory syllogism (imtiḥāniyya) from the sophistical (sūfisṭā'iyya)?
A) The examinatory uses true premises, while sophistry uses false premises.
B) The examinatory differs in intention and purpose, not in material or form.
C) The examinatory is used for scientific discovery, while sophistry is used for deception.
D) The examinatory is always formally valid, while sophistry is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The classification of syllogisms analogous to dialectic depends on their purpose (ghāya), not on material or form, so the same logical structure can serve truth, deception, or examination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following syllogism is examinatory, contentious, eristic, or dialectical: A wise scholar says: 'You claim that all knowledge is acquired. But you also accept that knowledge of the Creator is necessary. Please explain how necessary knowledge can be acquired?' The syllogism is: 'If all knowledge is acquired, then knowledge of the Creator is acquired; but you say knowledge of the Creator is necessary; therefore, your claim is contradictory.' Justify your classification using the passage's criteria.
?
Construct a brief contentious syllogism (one that aims to show an opponent's incapacity) on a topic from fiqh, such as the permissibility of photography. Use premises that are well-known (mashhūrāt) but structure the syllogism so that it is formally valid but the opponent cannot respond to it without contradicting a known position.

The Syllogism
beginner

Identifying the Sophistical Syllogism and Its Contrast with Other Types
Defining the sophistical syllogism and contrasting it with three other types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the sophistical syllogism from demonstrative, dialectical, and eristic syllogisms based on its material and claims.

Core Idea
Sophistry imitates demonstration by claiming truth, while eristic lies openly.

In this passage, the author defines the sophistical syllogism as an imitation of demonstration, and distinguishes it from three other types: demonstration, dialectic, and eristic. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for identifying flawed reasoning that masquerades as certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of syllogism
List the four types mentioned: demonstrative (burhan), dialectical (jadali), sophistical (sufistai), and eristic (mushaghibi). Notice that the author focuses on the sophistical and compares it to the others.
2
Understand the analogy
The author says sophistical relates to demonstrative as contentious (mumari) relates to dialectical. This means sophistry is an imperfect imitation of demonstration, just as contentious argument is an imperfect version of dialectic.
3
Compare sophistical vs demonstrative
Demonstration is original and true; sophistical is merely an imitation. Sophistical premises claim to be true in themselves and appropriate to principles, but they are not.
4
Compare sophistical vs dialectical
Dialectical premises are taken because they are well-known (mashhur), not because they are true in themselves. Sophistical premises are taken as true in themselves and appropriate to principles, but they are actually false.
5
Compare sophistical vs eristic
Eristic does not claim its premises are well-known or accepted; it lies openly. Sophistical presents its premises as if they are true, making it more deceptive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يكون مخصوصا باسم السوفسطائي من القياسات المشبهة فنسبته من حيث المادة إلى القياس البرهاني تشبه المماري إلى الجدلي؛ وهذا القياس يخالف البرهان بأن البرهان أصل وحق. وهذا مشبه به ويخالف الجدلي أيضًا بأن الجدلي لا يأخذ المقدّمات على أنه حق في نفس الأمر بل من جهة الشهرة، ولا يأخذها على أنها مناسبة المبادي، وهذا يأخذها على أنه حق في نفس الأمر؛ وإنها مناسبة ويخالف المشاغبي بأنه ليس يأخذ مقدماته على أنها مشهورة أو مسلمة بل لأنه يأخذها على أنها حقه ويكذب فيها.
As for what is specifically called by the name 'sophistical' among the analogous syllogisms, its relation in terms of material to the demonstrative syllogism resembles the relation of the contentious (mumārī) to the dialectical. This syllogism differs from demonstration in that demonstration is original and true, while this is an analogue to it. It also differs from the dialectical in that the dialectical does not take the premises as true in themselves, but rather from the aspect of renown (shuhra), and does not take them as being appropriate to the principles. But this one takes them as true in themselves and as appropriate. And it differs from the eristic (mushāghibī) in that [the eristic] does not take its premises as being well-known or accepted, but rather takes them as being true and lies in them.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage defines the sophistical syllogism specifically and distinguishes it from three other types: demonstrative, dialectical, and eristic. The author first notes that the sophistical syllogism relates to the demonstrative in material as the contentious (mumārī) relates to the dialectical. In other words, it is an imperfect imitation. Then he contrasts it with demonstration: demonstration is original and true, while the sophistical is merely an imitation. Next, it differs from dialectic because dialectic uses premises based on their being well-known (mashhūr), not on their inherent truth; dialectical premises are not taken as appropriate to the principles (mabādi') of a science. In contrast, the sophistical claims its premises are true in themselves and appropriate to the principles. Finally, it differs from eristic (mushāghabiyya) because eristic does not claim its premises are well-known or accepted; rather, it lies in them. The sophistical, however, presents its premises as if they are truthful. This distinction shows that sophistry is more dangerous than eristic because it masquerades as truth, while eristic is openly deceptive.

Key Terms
سوفسطائي
برهان
جدلي
مشاغبي

Checkpoint
How does the sophistical syllogism differ from the dialectical according to the passage?
A) It uses demonstrative premises

C) It does not care about truth
D) It uses well-known premises

Key Takeaway
The sophistical syllogism is dangerous because it imitates demonstration by claiming its premises are true and appropriate, while actually being false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Accident vs. Essential Predicates: The Negation Test
Types of affirmation/negation and accident vs. essential predicates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between accident and essential predicates (definition and property) by applying the partial negation test as described in the passage.

Core Idea
Partial negation of an accident does not negate it as accident, but for definition and property it does.

This passage draws a critical distinction: negating a part of an accident does not mean it is not an accident, but for definition and property, any partial negation negates the whole. Understanding this difference is essential for constructing valid definitions and arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three predicate types
The passage discusses accident (عرض), property (خاصة), and definition (حد). Note that accident is non-essential, while property and definition are essential in the sense that they are co-extensive or reveal essence.
2
Understand the universal/particular scope
The author states that universal claims may be about individuals or states. For accident, it can be affirmed universally or partially. For property and definition, only universal affirmation holds.
3
Apply the partial negation test
If you negate a particular instance of an accident, it does not follow that the subject lacks that accident entirely. But if you negate a part of a definition or property, you negate the whole because they require universal predication.
Example: 'This whiteness is not white' does not make whiteness not an accident; but 'Man is not rational' negates the definition of man.
4
Contrast with error in essentials
The passage warns against including unnecessary conditions (e.g., 'whiteness is an accident if it is a color') or omitting necessary conditions (e.g., claiming a genus by derivation). This relates to the correct application of the test.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في مواضع الإثبات والإبطال المأخوذة من جوهر الوضع فنقول المطالب التي يساق إليها المقايس كلية وجزئية؛ والجزئية في ضمن الكلية إثباتًا وإبطالاً. والكلية قد تكون بحسب الأشخاص، وقد تكون بحسب الأحوال سواء كان في العرض أو في غيره. والعرض قد يثبت كليا، وقد يثبت جزئيا؛ ولا يلزم من سلبه جزئياً أن لا يكون عرضًا. وأما ما سواه فإنه يلزم من سلبه جزئياً أن لا يكون ذلك كما في الخاصة والحد؛ فإنه يلزم أن تكون الكليتين في الإثبات. وأما العرض فإنه لا يمكن أن يثبت تعاكسا للموضوع؛ ولا ينقلب خاصة لكن ذلك ينفع في إبطاله. فإنه إذا كان تعاكسا لا يكون عرضًا لكن إثبات ذلك صعب؛ فالأولى أن يُترك . ثم إذا كان العرض وجوده أقرب إلى أن يكون وجودًا فلا يحتاج في وجوده إلى نظر زائد بخلاف ما يكون مقوما. وما أشبهه فإنه يحتاج في أمره إلى نظر زائد والبحث قد يكون في الوجود، وقد يكون في الشيء بعد الوجود كما في كون الشيء جنسًا أو فصلا مكان إلحاق مواضع العرض في التعليم الأول بمواضع الإثبات المطلق والنظر في المحمولات التي هي أجناس وحدود وخواص نظر أن أحدهما هل هي موجودة لموضوعاتها وهذا النظر يدخل في اعتبار الوجود. وأما النظر الثاني في أنه هل هو جنس أو هل هو حد أو هل هو خاصة فهو نظر في عارض طبيعة لأن الجنسية آخر ما يعرض للطبيعة الموضوعة للجنسية؛ فيكون النظر فيها أخص من النظر في الوجود. ولا يظن أن السلب مقدم على الإيجاب؛ إذ السلب قد يكون من جانب المجيب، وقد يكون من جانب السائل. والإبطال الكلي قد يكون بموجب جزئي، وقد يكون بسالب جزئي، وقد يكون بالكلي منهما جميعًا. ثمَّ الغلط في الذاتيات قد يكون بإدخال الشرط كما يقال إن البياض عرض له إن كان لونا وإنما اللون جنسه ، وقد يكون بترك الشرط كما يقال إنه جنس له لا بالتواطؤ بل بالاشتقاق كالأعراض فيقال إن البياض لون، وإن المشي تحرك وأمثال هذه الأشياء أعم من موضوعاتها فلا يمكن أن يكون من الخواص ؛ وهذا الموضع برهاني، والقياس عليه من الشكل الثاني وإنه يصلح للإثبات والإبطال، إما الإبطال فيقال مثلا إن هذا المحمول جنس للموضوع، ولا شيء مما هو عرض للموضوع خاصة أو غير خاصة بجنس؛ فيكفي في الإبطال قياس [۳۲۸ظ] واحد. وأما الإثبات فيحتاج إلى مقدمات كثيرة حتى يقال إنه ليس بجنس ولا حد ولا خاصة، وكل ما ليس كذا ولا كذا ولا كذا فهو عرض لكن أمثال هذه القياسات بالحقيقة مركبة من منفصلات كثيرة؛ والبحث في المنفصلات قد مر . ولا يستراب في أن الاستثناء لا يفيد إلا إذا كانت القسمة منحصرة. ويجب أن تعلم بأن إثبات الشيء وإبطاله في طرفي نقيض بالحقيقة حتى إذا قال المجيب هذا ثابت بالضرورة يكفى من جانب أنه لا يكون ثابتا بالضرورة بل
Section: On the places of affirmation and negation taken from the essence of position. We say: The goals to which syllogisms are directed are universal and particular; the particular is included within the universal in affirmation and negation. The universal may be according to individuals, or according to states, whether in accident or in other than it. Accident may be affirmed universally or partially; from its partial negation it does not follow that it is not an accident. As for other than accident, from its partial negation it follows that it is not that, as in the case of property and definition; for it is necessary that the two universals be in affirmation. As for accident, it cannot be affirmed as convertible with the subject; nor does it turn into a property, but that is useful in its negation. For if it were convertible, it would not be an accident, but proving that is difficult; so it is better to leave it. Then, if an accident's existence is closer to being existence, it does not need extra consideration in its existence, unlike what is constitutive. And what resembles it needs extra consideration. Investigation may be about existence, or about the thing after existence, as in whether something is a genus or a species, in place of attaching the topics of accident in the First Teaching to the topics of absolute affirmation and consideration of predicates that are genera, definitions, and properties: consideration of whether they exist for their subjects—this consideration enters into the consideration of existence. As for the second consideration—whether it is a genus, a definition, or a property—it is a consideration of an accident of the nature, because generality is the last thing that occurs to nature placed under generality; thus considering it is more specific than considering existence. Do not think that negation precedes affirmation; for negation may be on the part of the respondent, or on the part of the questioner. Universal negation may be through a particular affirmative, or through a particular negative, or through the universal of both together. Then error in essentials may be by including a condition, as when it is said that whiteness is an accident of it if it is a color, while color is its genus; or by omitting a condition, as when it is said that it is a genus for it not univocally but by derivation, like the accidents: whiteness is a color, walking is a motion. Such things are more general than their subjects, so they cannot be among the properties. This topic is demonstrative, and the syllogism about it is from the second figure, and it is suitable for affirmation and negation. As for negation, one says, for example, that this predicate is a genus of the subject, and nothing that is an accident of the subject, whether property or not, is a genus; thus a single syllogism suffices for negation. As for affirmation, it needs many premises until it is said that it is not a genus, not a definition, not a property, and everything that is not this nor that nor that is an accident. But such syllogisms are in reality composed of many disjunctives; the discussion on disjunctives has passed. No doubt that exception (istithnāʾ) is useful only if the division is exhaustive. And you must know that affirmation and negation of a thing are at the two extremes of true contradiction, so that if the respondent says 'this is necessarily true', it suffices on the part that it is not necessarily true, but...
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage is a structured section on the logical principles of affirmation (إثبات) and negation (إبطال) as derived from the 'essence of position' (جوهر الوضع). The author first distinguishes universal and particular claims, noting that particulars are subsumed under universals. He then discusses accident (عرض): an accident can be affirmed universally or partially, but negating it partially does not negate it as accident. In contrast, for definition (حد) and property (خاصة), negating a part negates the whole because they require universal predication. Accident cannot be converted with the subject because that would be a property. The author then explains two levels of investigation: existence (whether a predicate exists for a subject) and the nature of the predicate (whether it is a genus, definition, or property). The latter is more specific. He clarifies that negation is not prior to affirmation; both sides can initiate negation. He identifies two types of error in essential predications: including an unnecessary condition (e.g., saying 'whiteness is an accident if it is a color') or omitting a necessary condition (e.g., claiming a genus by derivation rather than univocally). The author then asserts that this topic is demonstrative (برهاني) and uses the second figure of categorical syllogism, which suits both proof and refutation. For refutation, a single syllogism suffices: if a predicate is a genus and nothing accidental is a genus, then the predicate is not accidental. For proof, many premises are needed to exclude genus, definition, and property. He notes the importance of exhaustive division for disjunctive syllogisms. Finally, he affirms that contradiction is at the heart of these issues.

Key Terms
العرض
الخاصة
الحد
الذاتيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what follows from a partial negation of an accident?
A) It necessarily negates the accident entirely.
B) It does not negate the accident entirely.
C) It turns the accident into a property.
D) It makes the accident convertible with the subject.

Key Takeaway
Partial negation distinguishes accident from essential predicates: accident survives partial negation, but definition and property do not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following predicate is an accident or a property: 'The world is temporally originated'. Explain using the partial negation test: if you say 'some parts of the world are not temporally originated', does that negate the predicate 'temporally originated' entirely?
?
In a hadith discussion, the predicate 'just' is said to be a property of the Prophet. If someone argues that 'some acts of the Prophet are not just', does that negate the property? Use the passage to support your answer.

The Syllogism
advanced

The Second Figure Syllogism for Affirmation and Negation
Use of second figure syllogism for demonstrating predicate types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a second figure categorical syllogism to prove or disprove that a predicate is a genus, definition, property, or accident.

Core Idea
The second figure syllogism is efficient for negation (one premise) but requires many premises for affirmation.

The author explains that the demonstrative topic of whether a predicate is a genus, definition, property, or accident uses the second figure. For refutation, a single syllogism suffices; for proof, multiple premises are needed. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate and subject
Determine what is being predicated and what it is predicated of. The passage uses examples like 'this predicate is a genus of the subject'.
2
Form the negation syllogism (refutation)
The author gives: 'This predicate is a genus of the subject. Nothing that is an accident of the subject is a genus. Therefore, this predicate is not an accident.' This is a second figure syllogism (middle term 'genus' is predicate in both premises).
The mood is Cesare (EIO) or Camestres (AEE) depending on the quantifiers. Here: Major: No accident is a genus (universal negative); Minor: This predicate is a genus (universal affirmative); Conclusion: This predicate is not an accident (universal negative).
3
Form the affirmation syllogism (proof)
To prove something is an accident, one must exclude all other possibilities: it is not a genus, not a definition, not a property. This requires a compound syllogism or a disjunctive syllogism with exhaustive division.
The author says such syllogisms are 'in reality composed of many disjunctives' – a series of disjunctive syllogisms each eliminating a possibility.
4
Understand the role of exhaustive division
The passage notes that 'exception (istithnāʾ) is useful only if the division is exhaustive.' This is crucial for disjunctive syllogisms used in proof.
For affirmation, you must ensure the division (genus, definition, property, accident) covers all possibilities.

Source Text

بالضرورة يكفى من جانب أنه لا يكون ثابتا بالضرورة بل
Section: On the places of affirmation and negation taken from the essence of position. We say: The goals to which syllogisms are directed are universal and particular; the particular is included within the universal in affirmation and negation. The universal may be according to individuals, or according to states, whether in accident or in other than it. Accident may be affirmed universally or partially; from its partial negation it does not follow that it is not an accident. As for other than accident, from its partial negation it follows that it is not that, as in the case of property and definition; for it is necessary that the two universals be in affirmation. As for accident, it cannot be affirmed as convertible with the subject; nor does it turn into a property, but that is useful in its negation. For if it were convertible, it would not be an accident, but proving that is difficult; so it is better to leave it. Then, if an accident's existence is closer to being existence, it does not need extra consideration in its existence, unlike what is constitutive. And what resembles it needs extra consideration. Investigation may be about existence, or about the thing after existence, as in whether something is a genus or a species, in place of attaching the topics of accident in the First Teaching to the topics of absolute affirmation and consideration of predicates that are genera, definitions, and properties: consideration of whether they exist for their subjects—this consideration enters into the consideration of existence. As for the second consideration—whether it is a genus, a definition, or a property—it is a consideration of an accident of the nature, because generality is the last thing that occurs to nature placed under generality; thus considering it is more specific than considering existence. Do not think that negation precedes affirmation; for negation may be on the part of the respondent, or on the part of the questioner. Universal negation may be through a particular affirmative, or through a particular negative, or through the universal of both together. Then error in essentials may be by including a condition, as when it is said that whiteness is an accident of it if it is a color, while color is its genus; or by omitting a condition, as when it is said that it is a genus for it not univocally but by derivation, like the accidents: whiteness is a color, walking is a motion. Such things are more general than their subjects, so they cannot be among the properties. This topic is demonstrative, and the syllogism about it is from the second figure, and it is suitable for affirmation and negation. As for negation, one says, for example, that this predicate is a genus of the subject, and nothing that is an accident of the subject, whether property or not, is a genus; thus a single syllogism suffices for negation. As for affirmation, it needs many premises until it is said that it is not a genus, not a definition, not a property, and everything that is not this nor that nor that is an accident. But such syllogisms are in reality composed of many disjunctives; the discussion on disjunctives has passed. No doubt that exception (istithnāʾ) is useful only if the division is exhaustive. And you must know that affirmation and negation of a thing are at the two extremes of true contradiction, so that if the respondent says 'this is necessarily true', it suffices on the part that it is not necessarily true, but...

Commentary
This passage is a structured section on the logical principles of affirmation (إثبات) and negation (إبطال) as derived from the 'essence of position' (جوهر الوضع). The author first distinguishes universal and particular claims, noting that particulars are subsumed under universals. He then discusses accident (عرض): an accident can be affirmed universally or partially, but negating it partially does not negate it as accident. In contrast, for definition (حد) and property (خاصة), negating a part negates the whole because they require universal predication. Accident cannot be converted with the subject because that would be a property. The author then explains two levels of investigation: existence (whether a predicate exists for a subject) and the nature of the predicate (whether it is a genus, definition, or property). The latter is more specific. He clarifies that negation is not prior to affirmation; both sides can initiate negation. He identifies two types of error in essential predications: including an unnecessary condition (e.g., saying 'whiteness is an accident if it is a color') or omitting a necessary condition (e.g., claiming a genus by derivation rather than univocally). The author then asserts that this topic is demonstrative (برهاني) and uses the second figure of categorical syllogism, which suits both proof and refutation. For refutation, a single syllogism suffices: if a predicate is a genus and nothing accidental is a genus, then the predicate is not accidental. For proof, many premises are needed to exclude genus, definition, and property. He notes the importance of exhaustive division for disjunctive syllogisms. Finally, he affirms that contradiction is at the heart of these issues.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does negation require only one syllogism while affirmation requires many premises?
A) Because negation uses the first figure and affirmation uses the second.
B) Because to deny that something is an accident, you only need to show it is a genus; to affirm it is an accident, you must exclude all other categories.
C) Because negation is always prior to affirmation.
D) Because affirmation requires disjunctive syllogisms which are invalid.

Key Takeaway
The second figure syllogism is efficient for refutation but not for proof; proof requires exhaustive elimination of alternatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a second figure syllogism to refute the claim that 'the attribute of speech is a property of God' (assuming property is coextensive and not essential). Use the pattern: property is coextensive; accident is not coextensive; therefore it is not a property.
?
In usul al-fiqh, a jurist wants to prove that a certain ruling is essential to the act of prayer, not accidental. Using the passage's method, list the premises required to prove it is essential (not genus, definition, property) and then conclude it is essential.

The Syllogism

Second Figure Syllogism: One-Step Refutation
Author demonstrates second figure refutation with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a second-figure categorical syllogism that refutes a claim by showing a predicate cannot be both a genus and an accident.

Core Idea
Refutation in the second figure is a single step; proof requires a chain.

The second figure of categorical syllogism is uniquely suited for refutation. The author explains that while proof may require many premises, a single syllogism can suffice to refute a claim, as when we show that a predicate cannot be both a genus and an accident. Let us see how this efficient refutation works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim to refute
Locate the proposition that the respondent asserts, e.g., 'This predicate is a genus for the subject.' The refutation target is that the predicate is an accident.
In the example, the claim implicitly is that the predicate is an accident.
2
Extract the middle term
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. Here the middle term is 'genus' (jins) because it appears as predicate in both premises.
The middle term links the two premises.
3
Construct the refutation syllogism
Use the author's example: 'This predicate is a genus for the subject. Nothing that is an accident is a genus. Therefore, this predicate is not an accident.' This is a valid second-figure syllogism (Camestres or Cesare mood).
The conclusion contradicts the claim that the predicate is an accident.
4
Verify the figure and validity
Check that the middle term 'genus' is predicate in both premises. The second figure requires the middle term to be predicate in both; this satisfies it. The syllogism is valid and refutes the original claim.
No conversion needed; the refutation is immediate.

وهذا الموضع برهاني، والقياس عليه من الشكل الثاني وإنه يصلح للإثبات والإبطال، إما الإبطال فيقال مثلا إن هذا المحمول جنس للموضوع، ولا شيء مما هو عرض للموضوع خاصة أو غير خاصة بجنس؛ فيكفي في الإبطال قياس واحد. وأما الإثبات فيحتاج إلى مقدمات كثيرة حتى يقال إنه ليس بجنس ولا حد ولا خاصة، وكل ما ليس كذا ولا كذا ولا كذا فهو عرض لكن أمثال هذه القياسات بالحقيقة مركبة من منفصلات كثيرة؛ والبحث في المنفصلات قد مر . ولا يستراب في أن الاستثناء لا يفيد إلا إذا كانت القسمة منحصرة. ويجب أن تعلم بأن إثبات الشيء وإبطاله في طرفي نقيض بالحقيقة حتى إذا قال المجيب هذا ثابت بالضرورة يكفى من جانب أنه لا يكون ثابتا بالضرورة بل يمكن أن يكون ثابتا، ويمكن أن لا يكون. ولو تعرض السائل للقياس في مقابلة ما يضعه المجيب بالقياس فذلك لا يكون إبطالاً للشيء بحسب ذاته بل يكون إثباتا يلزم منه الإبطال.
And this topic is demonstrative, and the syllogism upon it is from the second figure, and it is suitable for affirmation and refutation. As for refutation, for example one says: this predicate is a genus for the subject, and nothing that is an accident of the subject, whether specific or not, is a genus; so a single syllogism suffices for refutation. As for affirmation, it requires many premises until one says: it is not a genus, nor a definition, nor a property, and everything that is not such and such and such is an accident; but such syllogisms are in reality composed of many disjunctives; and the discussion of disjunctives has passed. And there is no doubt that the exceptive (syllogism) is useful only if the division is exhaustive. And you must know that the affirmation of a thing and its refutation are in reality at two opposite ends, so that when the respondent says 'this is necessarily true', it suffices from the other side that it is not necessarily true, but rather it may be true and may not be. And if the questioner undertakes a syllogism in opposition to what the respondent posits by a syllogism, that is not a refutation of the thing in itself, but rather an affirmation that entails refutation.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage discusses the use of the second figure of categorical syllogism in demonstrative contexts (برهاني). The author states that the second figure is suitable for both proof (إثبات) and refutation (إبطال). For refutation, a single syllogism suffices: e.g., 'This predicate is a genus for the subject, and nothing that is an accident is a genus, so this predicate is not an accident.' For proof, however, one needs many premises to show that the predicate is not a genus, not a definition, not a property, and then conclude it is an accident. The author notes that such proofs are actually composed of multiple disjunctive syllogisms (منفصلات). He then reminds us that exceptive syllogisms are only useful if the division is exhaustive. Finally, he explains that affirmation and refutation are opposites: if the respondent claims necessary truth, it is enough to show that it is not necessarily true, i.e., it may or may not be. Also, if the questioner constructs a syllogism against the respondent's position, that is not a direct refutation of the thing itself but rather an affirmation that logically entails a refutation.

Key Terms
الشكل الثاني
الإبطال
جنس
عرض

A) One syllogism.
B) Many syllogisms.
C) An exhaustive division.
D) A disjunctive syllogism.

Key Takeaway
A single second-figure categorical syllogism can efficiently refute a claim by showing a contradiction between the predicate's properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a second-figure refutation syllogism for the following claim in usul al-fiqh: 'Qiyas is a valid source of law.' Use the refutation pattern: 'Qiyas is a valid source; nothing that is not based on textual evidence is a valid source; therefore qiyas is based on textual evidence.' (Adapt as needed.)
?

Second Figure for Proof: The Multi-Step Approach
Author explains complexity of proof in second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to outline the process of using a second-figure syllogism for proof, recognising that it involves multiple disjunctive syllogisms and requires exhaustive division.

Core Idea
Proof in the second figure is a composition of many disjunctive syllogisms.

While refutation is straightforward, proof in the second figure requires a series of disjunctive syllogisms. The author explains that to prove something is an accident, one must eliminate all other possibilities: genus, definition, property. This process relies on exhaustive division and the exceptive syllogism. Let us unpack this method.

Guided Path
1
Understand the goal: prove a predicate is an accident
The aim is to demonstrate that the predicate (e.g., 'accident') applies to the subject. The proof must exclude all other predicables: genus, definition, property.
The four predicables are: genus, definition, property, accident.
2
Use separate refutation syllogisms for each alternative
For each alternative (e.g., 'It is a genus'), construct a second-figure refutation to show it is false. The author implies that each elimination uses the efficient refutation pattern from the previous lesson.
E.g., 'It is not a genus because ...', 'It is not a definition because ...'.
3
Combine the eliminations into a disjunctive syllogism
After eliminating genus, definition, and property, we have: 'It is either genus, definition, property, or accident; it is not genus, not definition, not property; therefore, it is an accident.' This is an exceptive syllogism (istithna'i) with an exhaustive division.
The disjunction must be exhaustive, covering all possibilities.
4
Verify the exhaustive division condition
The exceptive syllogism is only valid if the division (qisma) is exhaustive (munhasira), meaning no possibility is left out. If the list of predicables is incomplete, the conclusion may be false.
The author stresses: 'الاستثناء لا يفيد إلا إذا كانت القسمة منحصرة'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Syllogism

Proof and Refutation via Conditional Syllogisms from External Matters
Author introduces places of proof and refutation drawn from external matters using conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two methods of conditional argument (affirming the antecedent for proof and denying the consequent for refutation) and apply them to Islamic examples using external matters.

Core Idea
Proof uses antecedent affirmation; refutation uses consequent denial — both via external matters.

In Islamic logic, proof (ithbat) and refutation (ibtal) often rely on definitions. But sometimes we must use external matters — indications outside the essence of the subject. This passage from the logic text teaches how to construct valid arguments using conditional syllogisms when the evidence is external. You will learn two powerful tools: antecedent exception (istithna' al-muqaddim) for proof, and contradictory of the consequent (naqid al-tali) for refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two uses of external matters
Read the Arabic: external matters are used in two ways: (1) matters that entail the sought proposition (يلزم منها المطلوب) — used for proof; (2) matters that follow from the sought proposition (ما يلزم المطلوب) — used for refutation. Notice the direction of entailment: for proof we go from external to sought; for refutation we go from sought to consequent and then falsify it.
This is the core distinction: proof seeks causes, refutation seeks consequences.
2
Understand the conditional form for proof
Proof uses 'استثناء المقدم' (exception of the antecedent). In a conditional 'If P then Q', if we find that P (the external matter) is true, then we conclude Q (the sought proposition). This is modus ponens. The text says: 'إذ هو نافع في الإثبات فقط باستثناء المقدم' (for it is useful in proof only by antecedent exception).
Here P is the external matter, Q is the desired proposition.
3
Understand the conditional form for refutation
Refutation uses 'استثناء نقيض التالي' (exception of the contradictory of the consequent). In the same conditional 'If P then Q', if we know Q is false (i.e., its contradictory holds), then we conclude P is false. This is modus tollens. The text says: 'نافع في الإبطال باستثناء نقيض التالي'.
The refutation denies the consequent to deny the antecedent.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the claim 'The world has a Creator (sought).' We find an external matter: 'The world is contingent (hadith).' Conditional: If the world is contingent, it has a Creator. The world is contingent (external matter). Therefore, it has a Creator (proof by antecedent exception). For refutation: If the world has a Creator, it is not eternal. But we suppose someone claims the world is eternal (contradictory of consequent). Therefore, the world does not have a Creator (refutation by denying consequent).
This is a standard kalam argument; note the use of external matter (contingency) rather than definition of 'world'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في مواضع الإثبات والإبطال المأخوذة من أمور خارجة. وليست المواضع كلها مأخوذة من حدي المطلوب بل قد تكون مأخوذة من أمور خارجة يلزم منها المطلوب فتطلب تلك الأمور؛ إذ هو نافع في الإثبات فقط باستثناء المقدم. ويطلب أيضًا ما يلزم المطلوب فإنه نافع في الإبطال باستثناء نقيض التالي؛ وقد مرّ الكلام في حقيقة الاستثناء وإنتاجه
Chapter: On the places of proof and refutation taken from external matters. Not all places are taken from the two boundaries of the sought proposition, but rather they may be taken from external matters from which the sought proposition follows, so those matters are sought; for it is useful in proof only by antecedent exception. And also what follows from the sought proposition is sought, for it is useful in refutation by exception of the contradictory of the consequent; and the discussion has already passed concerning the reality of exception and its production.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is a new section (فصل) that introduces the places of proof and refutation derived from external matters (أمور خارجة), i.e., not from the definition (حدّي) of the subject of inquiry. The author explains that in proof, one seeks external matters that entail the desired proposition (يلزم منها المطلوب), and then uses a method called 'استثناء المقدم' (exception of the antecedent), which refers to a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) where affirming the antecedent produces the conclusion. In refutation, one seeks what follows from the desired proposition (ما يلزم المطلوب) and uses 'استثناء نقيض التالي' (exception of the contradictory of the consequent)—that is, if the consequent is denied, the antecedent is denied. The author reminds the reader that the nature of exception (istithna') and its production of conclusions has already been discussed. This section thus presents a method for constructing proofs and refutations using conditional arguments when evidence is external to the subject's essence.

Key Terms

أمور خارجة
حدي المطلوب
استثناء المقدم
نقيض التالي

C) By directly defining the subject of the desired proposition.
D) By using a categorical syllogism with a middle term.

Key Takeaway
Proof using external matters requires affirming the antecedent of a conditional; refutation requires denying the consequent.

External Matters as Loci of Proof and Refutation
Author discusses topics of proof and refutation taken from external matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify external topics (mawadi' kharija) and distinguish them from internal (definitional) topics in a logical argument.

Core Idea
Proof and refutation often rely on matters outside the definitions of the subject.

In classical Islamic logic, arguments are not always drawn from the definitions of the terms involved. Sometimes, they come from external matters—facts or considerations that are outside the direct subject matter but have a necessary connection to it. This lesson introduces the concept of external topics and their role in dialectic and demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction between internal and external topics
Internal topics come from the definitions (hadd) of the terms; external topics come from matters beyond the definitions.
The passage states: 'ليست المواضع كلها مأخوذة من حدي المطلوب بل قد تكون مأخوذة من أمور خارجة'.
2
Recognize that external topics are used when the required conclusion is not directly in definitions
The author explains that external matters imply the required conclusion, so those matters are sought for proof or refutation.
3
Note that external topics are useful for both proof and refutation
Proof uses affirming the antecedent; refutation uses denying the consequent. Both are common to dialectic and demonstration.
The passage says: 'وهما من المواضع من جملة ما يكون مشتركا بين الجدل والبرهان'.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
In proving the existence of God from the contingency of the world, the contingency is an external matter, not a definition of God.
This example is from Kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في مواضع الإثبات والإبطال المأخوذة من أمور خارجة وليست المواضع كلها مأخوذة من حدي المطلوب بل قد تكون مأخوذة من أمور خارجة يلزم منها المطلوب فتطلب تلك الأمور؛ إذ هو نافع في الإثبات فقط باستثناء المقدم. ويطلب أيضًا ما يلزم المطلوب فإنه نافع في الإبطال باستثناء نقيض التالي؛ وقد مرّ الكلام في حقيقة الاستثناء وإنتاجه وإنهما من المواضع من جملة ما يكون مشتركا بين الجدل والبرهان. وقد ينتفع من الأمور الخارجة باعتبار الزمان كما يقال مثلا كل مغتذ تام ثم نجد الحيوان الواقف في السِّنِّ والنحط يغتذي في ذلك الزمان ولا ينمو ؛ وهذا أيضًا يشترك فيه الجدل والبرهان. وينتفع في الإبطال دون الإثبات أو كل ما اختلف زمانه فهو مختلف، وليس كل ما اتفق زمانه فهو متفق؛ فهذا الموضوع من جملة المواضع الخارجة عن حدي المطلوب. وكثيرا ما يقع الانتقال من الكلام في الشيء إلى الكلام في أمور خارجة في ملزوماته أو لوازم تكون إذا صحت أو بطلت انتقل منها إلى الحكم في الشيء. فربَّما كان ذلك الانتقال ضروريًا في الحقيقة حيث يكون القياس المنتقل إليه أو الاستقراء المنتقل إليه ليصحح حال لازم أو ملزوم، أو إبانة صدق الاستثناء فيه ضروريًا في الحقيقة. وربما كانا ضروريتين في ظاهر الأمر وفي المشهور. وكلاهما مقبول في الجدل؛ ويجب أن يكون الانتقال إلى اللوازم والملزومات التي بينها وبين المطلوب علاقة حقيقية مشهورة مقبولة في الظاهر وإلا لكان من المغالطات. ثم كل شيء يقال فله لوازم كثيرة غاية الكثرة؛ وملزومات كذلك. فكل من قال شيئًا بالفعل فنفسه أن يكون قال أشياء بالقوة. وأنت تعلم كيف ينفع اعتبار اللوازم والملزومات في الإثبات والإبطال، ويكون من الأمور الخارجة النافعة ما ليس على سبيل اللزوم بل على سبيل العناد كما في قولهم إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون الليل موجودًا، فإنه مما ينتفع به بطريق الاستثناء في الإثبات والإبطال؛ وإنه مما يشترك فيه الجدل والبرهان أيضًا. ومن جملة اعتبار الأمور الخارجة نقل الاسم وتبديله فربَّما يقع في البيان وخصوصًا إذا كان الاسم بحسب الصفة لا بحسب الذات؛ فيجب أن نأخذ المعنى ونلحظه في ذاته ونقتصر الاسم عليه. فإن تعدل دلالة الاسم إلى ما هو
Chapter: On the topics of proof and refutation taken from external matters. Not all topics are taken from the two definitions of the required [conclusion], but rather they may be taken from external matters from which the required [conclusion] necessarily follows, so those matters are sought; for it is useful in proof only by way of conditional syllogism affirming the antecedent. And what necessarily follows the required [conclusion] is also sought, for it is useful in refutation by way of conditional syllogism denying the consequent; and the discussion of the nature of conditional syllogism and its productivity has already passed, and both are among the topics that are common between dialectic and demonstration. And benefit may be derived from external matters with regard to time, as it is said, for example: 'Every fully grown nourished being [grows], yet we find the animal that has stopped in age and decline is nourished at that time but does not grow'; and this is also shared by dialectic and demonstration. And it is beneficial in refutation but not in proof, or: 'Everything whose time differs is different, but not everything whose time agrees is agreeing'; so this topic is among the topics external to the two definitions of the required [conclusion]. And often a transition occurs from speech about a thing to speech about external matters that are its necessary conditions (malzumat) or necessary consequences (lawazim), so that when these are true or false, one moves from them to judgment about the thing. Sometimes that transition is necessary in reality, when the syllogism or induction transitioned to serves to correct the state of a necessary consequence or necessary condition, or to show the truth of the conditional premise; and sometimes both are necessary in appearance and in common opinion. Both are acceptable in dialectic; and the transition must be to necessary consequences and necessary conditions that have a real and well-known relationship with the required [conclusion], acceptable in appearance, otherwise it would be from sophistical fallacies. Then everything that is said has very many necessary consequences, and necessary conditions likewise. So whoever says something actually, he himself has said things potentially. And you know how consideration of necessary consequences and necessary conditions benefits in proof and refutation. And among the beneficial external matters are those not by way of necessity but by way of opposition, as in their saying: 'Either the sun is rising or night exists,' for it is beneficial by way of conditional syllogism in proof and refutation; and it is also common between dialectic and demonstration. And among the consideration of external matters is the transfer of a name and its replacement, for it sometimes occurs in exposition, especially if the name is according to attribute not according to essence; so we must take the meaning and observe it in itself and restrict the name to it. Then if the indication of the name changes to what is...
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on topics (mawadi') of proof (ithbat) and refutation (ibtal) that are taken from external matters (umur kharija). The author begins by stating that not all topics come from the definitions of the subject under discussion; some come from external affairs that imply the required conclusion. He explains the logical structure: if one seeks to prove something, one looks for something external that implies it, and uses a conditional syllogism affirming the antecedent (istithna' al-muqaddam). If one seeks to refute, one looks for something implied by the claim (its necessary consequence) and denies the consequent (istithna' nafy al-tali). This is common to both dialectic (jadal) and demonstration (burhan). Next, he gives an example from time: a fully grown being normally grows, but an animal in decline is nourished yet does not grow—this shows that the conclusion (growth) does not follow from nourishment alone. This principle is used in refutation. He then discusses the transition from a thing to its necessary consequences (lawazim) and necessary conditions (malzumat), which can be used to judge the original thing. The connection between them and the original matter must be real and well-known, otherwise it is sophistry (maghalatat). He notes that everything has many consequences and conditions, so stating something actually implies many potential statements. Finally, he discusses naming: transferring a name from one thing to another can be deceptive, especially if the name is based on attribute not essence; one must consider the meaning in itself and restrict the name to it. The text ends abruptly.

Key Terms
المواضع
أمور خارجة
الإثبات والإبطال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, external topics are...
A) always taken from the definitions of the subject
B) taken from matters outside the definitions that imply the conclusion
C) only useful in refutation, not proof
D) exclusive to sophistry

Key Takeaway
External topics are legitimate sources of proof and refutation, used in both dialectic and demonstration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the claim 'The Quran is miraculous due to its eloquence.' Is the eloquence an internal (definitional) topic or an external one? Explain your reasoning.

The Syllogism
intermediate
15 min
Using Conditional Syllogism for Proof and Refutation
Author discusses topics of proof and refutation taken from external matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a conditional syllogism for proof (affirming the antecedent) and for refutation (denying the consequent) using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Proof requires affirming the antecedent; refutation requires denying the consequent.

The author explains that external matters are useful for proof only by way of conditional syllogism affirming the antecedent, and for refutation by way of denying the consequent. This lesson focuses on these two inferential forms and their application in Islamic argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
A conditional statement has the form 'If A then B'. In the context of external topics, A is the external matter, B is the desired conclusion.
2
For proof: affirm the antecedent
If we know that the external matter (A) is true, and (A) implies the conclusion (B), then we can conclude (B). This is modus ponens.
The author says: 'نافع في الإثبات فقط باستثناء المقدم' — useful for proof only by affirming the antecedent.
3
For refutation: deny the consequent
If we know that the conclusion (B) is false, and (A) implies (B), then we can conclude that (A) is false. This is modus tollens.
The author says: 'نافع في الإبطال باستثناء نقيض التالي' — useful for refutation by denying the consequent.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Suppose a hadith expert says: 'If a hadith is authentic, then it is binding. This hadith is authentic.' Therefore, it is binding. (Proof). Conversely, 'If a hadith is authentic, it is binding; but this hadith is not binding (due to contradictions), therefore it is not authentic.' (Refutation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في مواضع الإثبات والإبطال المأخوذة من أمور خارجة وليست المواضع كلها مأخوذة من حدي المطلوب بل قد تكون مأخوذة من أمور خارجة يلزم منها المطلوب فتطلب تلك الأمور؛ إذ هو نافع في الإثبات فقط باستثناء المقدم. ويطلب أيضًا ما يلزم المطلوب فإنه نافع في الإبطال باستثناء نقيض التالي؛ وقد مرّ الكلام في حقيقة الاستثناء وإنتاجه وإنهما من المواضع من جملة ما يكون مشتركا بين الجدل والبرهان. وقد ينتفع من الأمور الخارجة باعتبار الزمان كما يقال مثلا كل مغتذ تام ثم نجد الحيوان الواقف في السِّنِّ والنحط يغتذي في ذلك الزمان ولا ينمو ؛ وهذا أيضًا يشترك فيه الجدل والبرهان. وينتفع في الإبطال دون الإثبات أو كل ما اختلف زمانه فهو مختلف، وليس كل ما اتفق زمانه فهو متفق؛ فهذا الموضوع من جملة المواضع الخارجة عن حدي المطلوب. وكثيرا ما يقع الانتقال من الكلام في الشيء إلى الكلام في أمور خارجة في ملزوماته أو لوازم تكون إذا صحت أو بطلت انتقل منها إلى الحكم في الشيء. فربَّما كان ذلك الانتقال ضروريًا في الحقيقة حيث يكون القياس المنتقل إليه أو الاستقراء المنتقل إليه ليصحح حال لازم أو ملزوم، أو إبانة صدق الاستثناء فيه ضروريًا في الحقيقة. وربما كانا ضروريتين في ظاهر الأمر وفي المشهور. وكلاهما مقبول في الجدل؛ ويجب أن يكون الانتقال إلى اللوازم والملزومات التي بينها وبين المطلوب علاقة حقيقية مشهورة مقبولة في الظاهر وإلا لكان من المغالطات. ثم كل شيء يقال فله لوازم كثيرة غاية الكثرة؛ وملزومات كذلك. فكل من قال شيئًا بالفعل فنفسه أن يكون قال أشياء بالقوة. وأنت تعلم كيف ينفع اعتبار اللوازم والملزومات في الإثبات والإبطال، ويكون من الأمور الخارجة النافعة ما ليس على سبيل اللزوم بل على سبيل العناد كما في قولهم إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون الليل موجودًا، فإنه مما ينتفع به بطريق الاستثناء في الإثبات والإبطال؛ وإنه مما يشترك فيه الجدل والبرهان أيضًا. ومن جملة اعتبار الأمور الخارجة نقل الاسم وتبديله فربَّما يقع في البيان وخصوصًا إذا كان الاسم بحسب الصفة لا بحسب الذات؛ فيجب أن نأخذ المعنى ونلحظه في ذاته ونقتصر الاسم عليه. فإن تعدل دلالة الاسم إلى ما هو
Chapter: On the topics of proof and refutation taken from external matters. Not all topics are taken from the two definitions of the required [conclusion], but rather they may be taken from external matters from which the required [conclusion] necessarily follows, so those matters are sought; for it is useful in proof only by way of conditional syllogism affirming the antecedent. And what necessarily follows the required [conclusion] is also sought, for it is useful in refutation by way of conditional syllogism denying the consequent; and the discussion of the nature of conditional syllogism and its productivity has already passed, and both are among the topics that are common between dialectic and demonstration. And benefit may be derived from external matters with regard to time, as it is said, for example: 'Every fully grown nourished being [grows], yet we find the animal that has stopped in age and decline is nourished at that time but does not grow'; and this is also shared by dialectic and demonstration. And it is beneficial in refutation but not in proof, or: 'Everything whose time differs is different, but not everything whose time agrees is agreeing'; so this topic is among the topics external to the two definitions of the required [conclusion]. And often a transition occurs from speech about a thing to speech about external matters that are its necessary conditions (malzumat) or necessary consequences (lawazim), so that when these are true or false, one moves from them to judgment about the thing. Sometimes that transition is necessary in reality, when the syllogism or induction transitioned to serves to correct the state of a necessary consequence or necessary condition, or to show the truth of the conditional premise; and sometimes both are necessary in appearance and in common opinion. Both are acceptable in dialectic; and the transition must be to necessary consequences and necessary conditions that have a real and well-known relationship with the required [conclusion], acceptable in appearance, otherwise it would be from sophistical fallacies. Then everything that is said has very many necessary consequences, and necessary conditions likewise. So whoever says something actually, he himself has said things potentially. And you know how consideration of necessary consequences and necessary conditions benefits in proof and refutation. And among the beneficial external matters are those not by way of necessity but by way of opposition, as in their saying: 'Either the sun is rising or night exists,' for it is beneficial by way of conditional syllogism in proof and refutation; and it is also common between dialectic and demonstration. And among the consideration of external matters is the transfer of a name and its replacement, for it sometimes occurs in exposition, especially if the name is according to attribute not according to essence; so we must take the meaning and observe it in itself and restrict the name to it. Then if the indication of the name changes to what is...
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on topics (mawadi') of proof (ithbat) and refutation (ibtal) that are taken from external matters (umur kharija). The author begins by stating that not all topics come from the definitions of the subject under discussion; some come from external affairs that imply the required conclusion. He explains the logical structure: if one seeks to prove something, one looks for something external that implies it, and uses a conditional syllogism affirming the antecedent (istithna' al-muqaddam). If one seeks to refute, one looks for something implied by the claim (its necessary consequence) and denies the consequent (istithna' nafy al-tali). This is common to both dialectic (jadal) and demonstration (burhan). Next, he gives an example from time: a fully grown being normally grows, but an animal in decline is nourished yet does not grow—this shows that the conclusion (growth) does not follow from nourishment alone. This principle is used in refutation. He then discusses the transition from a thing to its necessary consequences (lawazim) and necessary conditions (malzumat), which can be used to judge the original thing. The connection between them and the original matter must be real and well-known, otherwise it is sophistry (maghalatat). He notes that everything has many consequences and conditions, so stating something actually implies many potential statements. Finally, he discusses naming: transferring a name from one thing to another can be deceptive, especially if the name is based on attribute not essence; one must consider the meaning in itself and restrict the name to it. The text ends abruptly.

Key Terms
الاستثناء
المقدم
التالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, what is the logical form used for refutation?
A) Affirming the antecedent
B) Denying the consequent
C) Denying the antecedent
D) Affirming the consequent

Key Takeaway
Conditional syllogisms are versatile: affirming the antecedent proves, denying the consequent refutes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional syllogism to refute the claim 'Every intoxicant is forbidden and wine is not an intoxicant' using modus tollens.

Arguments from Time in Proof and Refutation
Author discusses topics of proof and refutation taken from external matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use temporal considerations to refute universal claims or establish differences.

Core Idea
Time can be used to show that a claim is not universally true.

The author gives an example from time: 'Every fully grown nourished being grows, yet an animal in decline is nourished but does not grow.' This temporal exception refutes the universal statement. He also mentions the principle: 'Everything whose time differs is different, but not everything whose time agrees is agreeing.' This lesson examines how time functions as an external topic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal claim that seems to hold generally
For example, 'Every nourished being grows' seems true in most cases.
2
Find a temporal case where the antecedent is true but consequent false
The author gives the example of an animal in decline: it is nourished but does not grow. This is a counterexample at that specific time.
3
Use this counterexample to refute the universal claim
Since the universal claim fails for this temporal case, it is refuted. This method is useful for refutation, not proof.
The author says: 'ينتفع في الإبطال دون الإثبات' – it benefits refutation not proof.
4
Apply the principle of temporal difference
Difference in time can indicate difference in property, but agreement in time does not guarantee agreement. The author states: 'كل ما اختلف زمانه فهو مختلف، وليس كل ما اتفق زمانه فهو متفق'.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Consider the claim 'All prophets performed miracles at all times.' This can be refuted by noting that Prophet Muhammad's miracles were specific to his time and place, not universal across all times.
This example is illustrative; the author's original example from biology is simpler.

The Fallacy of Name Transfer in Logical Arguments
Author discusses topics of proof and refutation taken from external matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect when a name has been transferred from its original meaning and evaluate the validity of the argument.

Core Idea
Names based on attributes, not essences, are especially prone to transfer, leading to fallacies.

The author mentions that among external matters is the transfer of a name and its replacement. This can be deceptive, especially when the name is based on an attribute rather than the essence. In this lesson, we explore how name transfer can affect logical reasoning and how to guard against it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original meaning of the name based on essence
Most names have a core meaning tied to the essence of what they denote. For example, 'wine' (khamr) originally means a specific fermented drink that intoxicates.
2
Determine if the name is being used based on an attribute
Sometimes names are applied based on a property or attribute. For example, calling any intoxicating substance 'khamr' because it shares the attribute of intoxication.
The author says: 'خصوصًا إذا كان الاسم بحسب الصفة لا بحسب الذات'.
3
Check if the name has been transferred to a different meaning in the argument
If the name's meaning shifts from essence to attribute without clarification, the argument may commit the fallacy of equivocation.
4
Evaluate whether the argument relies on the transferred meaning
If the conclusion depends on the shifted meaning, the argument is fallacious. For example, 'Shirk means associating partners with Allah; depending on a doctor is shirk' – here 'shirk' is transferred.
The author advises: 'يجب أن نأخذ المعنى ونلحظه في ذاته ونقتصر الاسم عليه'.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
The term 'riba' originally means a specific increase in certain commodities. Transferring it to any banking profit requires careful definition; otherwise, arguments about interest become equivocal.

Source Text

فصل : في مواضع الإثبات والإبطال المأخوذة من أمور خارجة وليست المواضع كلها مأخوذة من حدي المطلوب بل قد تكون مأخوذة من أمور خارجة يلزم منها المطلوب فتطلب تلك الأمور؛ إذ هو نافع في الإثبات فقط باستثناء المقدم. ويطلب أيضًا ما يلزم المطلوب فإنه نافع في الإبطال باستثناء نقيض التالي؛ وقد مرّ الكلام في حقيقة الاستثناء وإنتاجه وإنهما من المواضع من جملة ما يكون مشتركا بين الجدل والبرهان. وقد ينتفع من الأمور الخارجة باعتبار الزمان كما يقال مثلا كل مغتذ تام ثم نجد الحيوان الواقف في السِّنِّ والنحط يغتذي في ذلك الزمان ولا ينمو ؛ وهذا أيضًا يشترك فيه الجدل والبرهان. وينتفع في الإبطال دون الإثبات أو كل ما اختلف زمانه فهو مختلف، وليس كل ما اتفق زمانه فهو متفق؛ فهذا الموضوع من جملة المواضع الخارجة عن حدي المطلوب. وكثيرا ما يقع الانتقال من الكلام في الشيء إلى الكلام في أمور خارجة في ملزوماته أو لوازم تكون إذا صحت أو بطلت انتقل منها إلى الحكم في الشيء. فربَّما كان ذلك الانتقال ضروريًا في الحقيقة حيث يكون القياس المنتقل إليه أو الاستقراء المنتقل إليه ليصحح حال لازم أو ملزوم، أو إبانة صدق الاستثناء فيه ضروريًا في الحقيقة. وربما كانا ضروريتين في ظاهر الأمر وفي المشهور. وكلاهما مقبول في الجدل؛ ويجب أن يكون الانتقال إلى اللوازم والملزومات التي بينها وبين المطلوب علاقة حقيقية مشهورة مقبولة في الظاهر وإلا لكان من المغالطات. ثم كل شيء يقال فله لوازم كثيرة غاية الكثرة؛ وملزومات كذلك. فكل من قال شيئًا بالفعل فنفسه أن يكون قال أشياء بالقوة. وأنت تعلم كيف ينفع اعتبار اللوازم والملزومات في الإثبات والإبطال، ويكون من الأمور الخارجة النافعة ما ليس على سبيل اللزوم بل على سبيل العناد كما في قولهم إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون الليل موجودًا، فإنه مما ينتفع به بطريق الاستثناء في الإثبات والإبطال؛ وإنه مما يشترك فيه الجدل والبرهان أيضًا. ومن جملة اعتبار الأمور الخارجة نقل الاسم وتبديله فربَّما يقع في البيان وخصوصًا إذا كان الاسم بحسب الصفة لا بحسب الذات؛ فيجب أن نأخذ المعنى ونلحظه في ذاته ونقتصر الاسم عليه. فإن تعدل دلالة الاسم إلى ما هو
Chapter: On the topics of proof and refutation taken from external matters. Not all topics are taken from the two definitions of the required [conclusion], but rather they may be taken from external matters from which the required [conclusion] necessarily follows, so those matters are sought; for it is useful in proof only by way of conditional syllogism affirming the antecedent. And what necessarily follows the required [conclusion] is also sought, for it is useful in refutation by way of conditional syllogism denying the consequent; and the discussion of the nature of conditional syllogism and its productivity has already passed, and both are among the topics that are common between dialectic and demonstration. And benefit may be derived from external matters with regard to time, as it is said, for example: 'Every fully grown nourished being [grows], yet we find the animal that has stopped in age and decline is nourished at that time but does not grow'; and this is also shared by dialectic and demonstration. And it is beneficial in refutation but not in proof, or: 'Everything whose time differs is different, but not everything whose time agrees is agreeing'; so this topic is among the topics external to the two definitions of the required [conclusion]. And often a transition occurs from speech about a thing to speech about external matters that are its necessary conditions (malzumat) or necessary consequences (lawazim), so that when these are true or false, one moves from them to judgment about the thing. Sometimes that transition is necessary in reality, when the syllogism or induction transitioned to serves to correct the state of a necessary consequence or necessary condition, or to show the truth of the conditional premise; and sometimes both are necessary in appearance and in common opinion. Both are acceptable in dialectic; and the transition must be to necessary consequences and necessary conditions that have a real and well-known relationship with the required [conclusion], acceptable in appearance, otherwise it would be from sophistical fallacies. Then everything that is said has very many necessary consequences, and necessary conditions likewise. So whoever says something actually, he himself has said things potentially. And you know how consideration of necessary consequences and necessary conditions benefits in proof and refutation. And among the beneficial external matters are those not by way of necessity but by way of opposition, as in their saying: 'Either the sun is rising or night exists,' for it is beneficial by way of conditional syllogism in proof and refutation; and it is also common between dialectic and demonstration. And among the consideration of external matters is the transfer of a name and its replacement, for it sometimes occurs in exposition, especially if the name is according to attribute not according to essence; so we must take the meaning and observe it in itself and restrict the name to it. Then if the indication of the name changes to what is...
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on topics (mawadi') of proof (ithbat) and refutation (ibtal) that are taken from external matters (umur kharija). The author begins by stating that not all topics come from the definitions of the subject under discussion; some come from external affairs that imply the required conclusion. He explains the logical structure: if one seeks to prove something, one looks for something external that implies it, and uses a conditional syllogism affirming the antecedent (istithna' al-muqaddam). If one seeks to refute, one looks for something implied by the claim (its necessary consequence) and denies the consequent (istithna' nafy al-tali). This is common to both dialectic (jadal) and demonstration (burhan). Next, he gives an example from time: a fully grown being normally grows, but an animal in decline is nourished yet does not grow—this shows that the conclusion (growth) does not follow from nourishment alone. This principle is used in refutation. He then discusses the transition from a thing to its necessary consequences (lawazim) and necessary conditions (malzumat), which can be used to judge the original thing. The connection between them and the original matter must be real and well-known, otherwise it is sophistry (maghalatat). He notes that everything has many consequences and conditions, so stating something actually implies many potential statements. Finally, he discusses naming: transferring a name from one thing to another can be deceptive, especially if the name is based on attribute not essence; one must consider the meaning in itself and restrict the name to it. The text ends abruptly.

Key Terms
نقل الاسم
تبديل الاسم
الصفة
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, name transfer is particularly concerning when the name is based on:
A) Essence (dhat)
B) Attribute (sifa)
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Name transfer can invalidate an argument if it shifts meaning from essence to attribute without acknowledgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Analyze the argument: 'All intoxicants are forbidden (khamr). This drink is called "grape juice" but it is intoxicating, so it is forbidden.' Is there a name transfer? Explain.
