The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
Conditional Syllogisms from Opposites
Introduces external topics from opposites for conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a conditional syllogism from contrary opposites and evaluate whether it is necessary.

Core Idea
Opposites yield conditional arguments, but they are not automatically valid.

This lesson explores a new type of external topic: deriving premises from opposites (al-mutaqābilāt). These are used in connected conditional syllogisms (qiyāsāt istithnā'iyya muttaṣila). The author illustrates how to construct conditionals from pairs of contraries, such as friend/enemy and benevolence/injury, and emphasizes that such inferences are not necessary. Understanding this helps avoid overgeneralization when using opposites in theological or legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposite pairs
Choose two sets of contraries: one set of substances (e.g., friend/enemy) and one set of properties (e.g., benevolence/injury). The example uses 'friend vs. enemy' and 'benevolence vs. injury'.
Opposites here are contraries (ḍiddiyya), not contradictories.
2
Construct a conditional hypothesis
Form a conditional proposition: if a property applies to one substance, then the opposite property applies to the opposite substance. Example: 'If benevolence to friends is beautiful, then injury to enemies is beautiful.'
The antecedent and consequent must align the two opposite pairs.
3
Apply straightforwardly and reversely
The same pattern can be applied in reverse: 'If injury to friends is ugly, then benevolence to enemies is beautiful.' This is called ṭardan wa ‘aksan (straightforwardly and reversely).
This is not a logical equivalence; it is merely a construction method.
4
Assess necessity
The author warns that such conditionals are not necessary (wājiba). It does not follow that because a property holds for one contrary, the opposite property holds for the other contrary. Recognize the limitation.
This prevents fallacious reasoning from opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهنا مواضع خارجة مأخوذة من المتقابلات ومن أحوال الابتداء والانتهاء ومن الاشتقاقات وغير ذلك. وكأنها تنفع بحسب القياسات الاستثنائية المتّصلة: أما ما يكون من المتقابلات الضدية فطريقه أن يؤخذ متقابلان من الأضداد; و متقابلان منها كذلك مؤلّف على أنحاء التي نذكرها، ونجعل منها مقدم وتال فليكن مقابل هو مقابل الأصدقاء والأعداء، والآخر مقابل الإساءة والإحسان فيقال إن كان الإحسان إلى الأصدقاء جميلاً فالإساءة إلى الأعداء جميلًا، أو يقال إن كانت الإساءة إلى الأصدقاء قبيحًا فالإحسان إلى الأعداء جميلا. وعلى هذا طردًا وعكسا; فهذه كلها لا تكون واجبة، ولا يلزم من كون الشيء موجودًا لكل الضد أن يكون ضده موجودًا للضد; ومثل هذه المواضع
And here are external topics taken from opposites (al-mutaqābilāt), from states of beginning and ending, from derivations (al-ishtiqāqāt), and others. And they appear to be beneficial according to connected conditional syllogisms (al-qiyāsāt al-istithnā'iyya al-muttaṣila). As for what is from contrary opposites (al-mutaqābilāt al-ḍiddiyya), its method is that two opposites are taken from the contraries; and two opposites likewise composed according to the ways that we will mention, and we make from them an antecedent and a consequent. So let one opposite be the opposite of friends and enemies, and the other opposite be the opposite of injury and benevolence. So it is said: if benevolence to friends is beautiful, then injury to enemies is beautiful; or it is said: if injury to friends is ugly, then benevolence to enemies is beautiful. And so on straightforwardly and reversely. All these are not necessary, and it does not follow from the thing's being existent for every opposite that its opposite is existent for the opposite. And such topics...
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage presents another category of external topics: those derived from opposites (mutaqābilāt), from states of beginning and ending (aḥwāl al-ibtidā' wa al-intihā'), from derivations (ishtiqāqāt), and others. The author states that these are beneficial in the context of connected conditional syllogisms (qiyāsāt istithnā'iyya muttaṣila). Focusing on contrary opposites (mutaqābilāt ḍiddiyya), he explains the method: take two opposites (e.g., friend vs. enemy) and another pair (e.g., benevolence vs. injury), then construct conditionals such as: 'If benevolence to friends is beautiful, then injury to enemies is beautiful' or 'If injury to friends is ugly, then benevolence to enemies is beautiful.' This can be applied straightforwardly (ṭardan) and in reverse (‘aksan). However, the author emphasizes that these conditionals are not necessary (wājiba) – it does not follow that because a property applies to one opposite, the contrary property applies to the other opposite. This warns against overgeneralizing in reasoning from opposites.

Key Terms
المتقابلات
القياسات الاستثنائية المتصلة
المقدم والتال
طردًا وعكسًا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following examples is given in the text as a conditional syllogism from contrary opposites?
A) If the animal eats, then it grows.
B) If benevolence to friends is beautiful, then injury to enemies is beautiful.
C) If the sun is rising, then the night exists.
D) If the name is transferred, then confusion is removed.

Key Takeaway
Arguments from opposites in conditional form are not automatically necessary; one cannot assume a property transfers from one contrary to the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional syllogism from opposites using an example from fiqh (e.g., rulings on men and women, or on purity and impurity). Then state whether the inference is necessary according to the author's warning.
?

The Syllogism

Logical Topoi of Likeness and Unity/Multiplicity
Author defines and distinguishes two topoi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dialectical and demonstrative topoi and identify the topos of likenesses and the topos of unity/multiplicity in an argument.

Core Idea
Some topoi are dialectical, some demonstrative—knowing which is which prevents misuse.

This passage introduces two fundamental logical topoi: those based on likenesses (المتشابهات) and those based on relation to unity and multiplicity (النسبة إلى الوحدة والكثرة). The author explicitly labels the first as purely dialectical and the second as demonstrative. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating whether a given argumentative move is suitable for rhetorical persuasion or certain proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the topos of likenesses
Find the description: it is an analogy where one part is antecedent and the other consequent. The example given is: if opposites can be known by one science, then opposites can be opined by one opinion.
The author calls this 'purely dialectical'.
2
Determine its nature
Dialectical topoi rely on common opinion, not certain premises. They are useful in persuasive argument but not demonstrative proof.
3
Identify the topos of unity/multiplicity
Find the description: it involves arguing from unity to multiplicity or vice versa. The example: someone claims all knowledge is understanding, but knowledge can be of many things while understanding cannot, so the claim is refuted.
The author calls this 'demonstrative'.
4
Contrast the two topoi
The first is analogical and probable; the second is based on a logical contradiction and yields certain refutation. Identify when each would be appropriate.
Demonstrative topoi are used in certain sciences; dialectical in debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال هذا هو جار مجرى الطبيعة؛ وهذا مأخذ الحكمة فتكون نسبة الجاري مجرى الطبيعة نسبة ما يكون على سبيل التصاريف: وفيه نظر فإن ما يكون على سبيل التصاريف فذلك ما يكون صادرًا عن الشيء كالوضع عن المرض. ثم الانتفاع بهذه المواضع قد تكون بوجه مخصوص بالعلم الخلقي كما يقال إن كانت العدالة محمودة فالعدل محمود، وقد لا تكون ومنها أي من المواضع مواضع المتشابهات وهي كأنها تمثيلات يجعل البعض منها مقدما والآخر تاليا؛ وهذا هو جدلي صرف كقولهم إن كان قد يكون بالأضداد علم واحد فقد يكون بالأضداد ظن واحد . ومنها مواضع النسبة إلى الوحدة والكثرة؛ وهذا يصلح للإبطال كمن يضع أن كل علم فهم فيقال إن العلم قد يكون بأشياء كثيرة معا. والفهم لا يمكن أن يكون كذلك ؛ وهذا الموضع برهاني. ومنها مواضع مأخوذة من الأقل والأكثر وهي أربعة: أحدها أن يجعل هو أكثر في معنى الموضوع أكثر في معنى المحمول كما يقال إن كانت اللذة خيرا فما هو أكثر لذة فهو أكثر خيرًا. والثاني أن يعتبر مع المحمول محمول آخر حكم في أن يحمل أولى من حكم هذا؛ فإن لم يحمل ولم يؤخذ ذلك ففي المشهور أن هذا لا يحمل ولا يؤخذ؛ وهذا للإبطال. وإن وجد ما ليس أولى ففي المشهور أن الأولى يؤخذ؛ وهذا للإثبات. وحال هذا الموضع في العلوم يختلف بحسب اختلاف المفهوم من لفظة «أولى». والثالث عكس ما هذا وهو أن تكون الكثرة في جهة الموضوعين، والمحمول واحد منتظر إن لم يوجد المحمول لما هو أولى أن يوجد له لم يوجد لما ليس أولى، وإن وجد لما ليس أولى فقد وجد لما هو أولى. والرابع هو أن يكون الاعتبار بین محمولين و بین موضوعين، وأحد المحمولين ليس أولى لموضوعه من المحمول الآخر بموضوعه. ثم هنا مواضع مأخوذة من المساواة بعدد هذه المواضع بأعيانها وهي أربعة أيضًا حال محمولين متساويين في الأولى وغير الأولى في الموضوع أو موضوعين بمحمول أو محمولين لموضوعين. ثمَّ يعتبر ذلك بعينه؛ وليس شيء منها علميا . ومن المواضع أيضًا ما إذا قيل المحمول على موضوع أو على شيء بمعنى الأولى والأكثر؛ فهو مقول عليه بالإطلاق مثاله إن كان خمرًا أقل إسكارًا من خمر وأكثر فهو مسكر على الإطلاق وهذا ليس يصلح للإبطال فإنه ليس إذا لم يقل بأقل وأكثر لم يقل بإطلاق. وكثير من الأشياء لا يقبل الأقل والأكثر؛ ومثل هذا الموضع ليس له عناد علمي بل له عناد جدلي. وقريب من هذا ما إذا كان الشيء ممكناً أو موجوداً أو كذا وكذا في حال من الأحوال فهو أيضًا بتلك الحال على الإطلاق. فإن ما ليس بممكن في نفسه فليس بممكن في حال؛ وهذا أيضًا للإثبات. ويعاند هذا الموضع بأمثلة مشهورة؛ قد يوجد الشيء صالحًا في وقت؛ وليس صالحًا على الإطلاق، ويوجد الشيء غير فاسد في وقت؛ وليس غير فاسد على الإطلاق. وعلى هذا فإن المواضع كثيرة ولكل واحد منها نظائر كثيرة تعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى.
It is said: this follows the course of nature; and this is the origin of wisdom, so the ratio of that which follows the course of nature is the ratio of what occurs by way of variations. But there is consideration: for what occurs by way of variations is that which emanates from a thing, like recovery from illness. Then the benefit from these topoi may be in a specific manner related to ethical science, as it is said: if justice is praiseworthy, then the just person is praiseworthy; and sometimes not. Among them—that is, among the topoi—are the topoi of likenesses, which are like analogies, some placed as antecedent and others as consequent; and this is purely dialectical, like their saying: if opposites can be known by one science, then opposites can be opined by one opinion. Among them are the topoi of relation to unity and multiplicity; and this is suitable for refutation, as when someone posits that all knowledge is understanding, then it is said that knowledge can be of many things together, while understanding cannot be like that. And this topos is demonstrative. Among them are topoi taken from the less and the more, and they are four: First, that the more in the sense of the subject is made more in the sense of the predicate, as it is said: if pleasure is good, then what is more pleasurable is more good. Second, that another predicate is considered with the predicate, a judgment that it is more worthy of being predicated than the judgment of this one; if it is not predicated and that is not taken, then according to common opinion it is not predicated and not taken; and this is for refutation. And if what is not more worthy is found, then according to common opinion the more worthy is taken; and this is for proof. The status of this topos in the sciences differs according to the different understanding of the word 'more worthy'. Third is the opposite of this, which is that multiplicity is on the side of the two subjects, while the predicate is one; if the predicate is not found for that which is more worthy to have it, then it is not found for that which is not more worthy; and if it is found for that which is not more worthy, then it has been found for that which is more worthy. Fourth is that the consideration is between two predicates and between two subjects, and one of the two predicates is not more worthy for its subject than the other predicate for its subject. Then here there are topoi taken from equality, the same number as these topoi, and they are also four: cases of two predicates equal in the more worthy and the not more worthy in the subject, or two subjects with one predicate, or two predicates for two subjects. Then that is considered in the same way; and none of them is scientific. Among the topoi also is when the predicate is said of a subject or of something in the sense of the more worthy and the more; then it is said of it absolutely. For example: if a wine is less intoxicating than another wine and more, then it is intoxicating absolutely. And this is not suitable for refutation, for if it is not said with less and more, it is not said absolutely. And many things do not admit less and more. Such a topos has no scientific opposition, but rather dialectical opposition. Close to this is when something is possible or existent or so-and-so in some state, then it is also in that state absolutely. For what is not possible in itself is not possible in any state. And this is for proof. This topos is opposed by well-known examples: something may be good at one time, but not good absolutely; and something may be not corrupt at one time, but not not corrupt absolutely. Upon this, the topoi are many, and each has many analogues known through reflection, God willing.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of various argumentative topoi (مواضع) used in logical reasoning. The author begins by critiquing a previous view about 'following the course of nature' and then explains that these topoi can be used in ethical science. The first type mentioned is topoi of likenesses (المتشابهات), which are analogical, and purely dialectical. The second type is topoi of relation to unity and multiplicity (النسبة إلى الوحدة والكثرة), useful for refutation and demonstrative in nature. Then the author goes into detail on topoi from less and more (الأقل والأكثر), listing four specific forms. The first form deals with making the subject more in sense correlates with the predicate more; example using pleasure and goodness. The second form involves considering another predicate that is more worthy to be predicated; used for refutation or proof depending on existence of the more worthy. The third form reverses the second, focusing on multiplicity of subjects with one predicate. The fourth form considers two predicates and two subjects with no precedence. After these, the author describes topoi from equality (المساواة), which mirror the four forms of less-more but with equality instead. He notes these are not scientific. Another topos is introduced: when a predicate is said of a subject in the sense of 'more worthy', it is taken absolutely; example with wine intoxication. This is suitable for proof but not refutation, and many things do not admit degrees. A related topos involves possibility and existence: if something is possible in some state, it is possible absolutely; used for proof but opposed by counterexamples. The passage concludes by stating that these topoi are many and their analogues can be found through reflection.

Key Terms
مواضع
المتشابهات
النسبة إلى الوحدة والكثرة
جدلي
برهاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes the topos of unity and multiplicity?
A) It is purely dialectical and used for refutation.
B) It is demonstrative and suitable for refutation.
C) It is used for proof only.
D) It is neither dialectical nor demonstrative.

Key Takeaway
Topoi can be dialectical or demonstrative; the topos of unity/multiplicity is a demonstrative refutation tool, while the topos of likenesses is dialectical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a passage in a kalām work that uses an analogy (tamthīl) and determine whether it is a dialectical topos of likenesses or a demonstrative one.
?
Construct a refutation using the topos of unity/multiplicity against the claim that 'all valid reasoning is syllogistic' by showing that reasoning can be both syllogistic and analogical, but syllogistic cannot be both.

The Four Subtypes of the Less-More Topos
Author enumerates four subtypes of the less-more topos.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the four subtypes of the less-more topos and apply each to construct arguments for proof or refutation.

Core Idea
The less-more topos comes in four forms, each tailored to a specific logical configuration.

The author details four distinct patterns within the topos of less and more (الأقل والأكثر). These patterns involve relationships between subjects and predicates, and between two predicates or two subjects. Understanding each form allows you to construct arguments that either prove or refute a claim based on degrees of intensity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first subtype
It makes the more in subject correspond to more in predicate. Example: if pleasure is good, then more pleasure is more good. This is a straightforward scaling.
2
Understand the second subtype
It considers another predicate that is more worthy of being predicated. If the more worthy is not predicated, then the less worthy is not predicated either (refutation). If the less worthy is found, then the more worthy is found (proof). The meaning of 'more worthy' (أولى) may vary by science.
3
Understand the third subtype
It reverses the second: multiplicity on the side of two subjects, one predicate. If the predicate is not found for the more worthy subject, it is not found for the less worthy; if found for the less worthy, then found for the more worthy.
4
Understand the fourth subtype
It considers two predicates and two subjects where neither predicate is more worthy for its subject than the other. This is a neutral comparison.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال هذا هو جار مجرى الطبيعة؛ وهذا مأخذ الحكمة فتكون نسبة الجاري مجرى الطبيعة نسبة ما يكون على سبيل التصاريف: وفيه نظر فإن ما يكون على سبيل التصاريف فذلك ما يكون صادرًا عن الشيء كالوضع عن المرض. ثم الانتفاع بهذه المواضع قد تكون بوجه مخصوص بالعلم الخلقي كما يقال إن كانت العدالة محمودة فالعدل محمود، وقد لا تكون ومنها أي من المواضع مواضع المتشابهات وهي كأنها تمثيلات يجعل البعض منها مقدما والآخر تاليا؛ وهذا هو جدلي صرف كقولهم إن كان قد يكون بالأضداد علم واحد فقد يكون بالأضداد ظن واحد . ومنها مواضع النسبة إلى الوحدة والكثرة؛ وهذا يصلح للإبطال كمن يضع أن كل علم فهم فيقال إن العلم قد يكون بأشياء كثيرة معا. والفهم لا يمكن أن يكون كذلك ؛ وهذا الموضع برهاني. ومنها مواضع مأخوذة من الأقل والأكثر وهي أربعة: أحدها أن يجعل هو أكثر في معنى الموضوع أكثر في معنى المحمول كما يقال إن كانت اللذة خيرا فما هو أكثر لذة فهو أكثر خيرًا. والثاني أن يعتبر مع المحمول محمول آخر حكم في أن يحمل أولى من حكم هذا؛ فإن لم يحمل ولم يؤخذ ذلك ففي المشهور أن هذا لا يحمل ولا يؤخذ؛ وهذا للإبطال. وإن وجد ما ليس أولى ففي المشهور أن الأولى يؤخذ؛ وهذا للإثبات. وحال هذا الموضع في العلوم يختلف بحسب اختلاف المفهوم من لفظة «أولى». والثالث عكس ما هذا وهو أن تكون الكثرة في جهة الموضوعين، والمحمول واحد منتظر إن لم يوجد المحمول لما هو أولى أن يوجد له لم يوجد لما ليس أولى، وإن وجد لما ليس أولى فقد وجد لما هو أولى. والرابع هو أن يكون الاعتبار بین محمولين و بین موضوعين، وأحد المحمولين ليس أولى لموضوعه من المحمول الآخر بموضوعه. ثم هنا مواضع مأخوذة من المساواة بعدد هذه المواضع بأعيانها وهي أربعة أيضًا حال محمولين متساويين في الأولى وغير الأولى في الموضوع أو موضوعين بمحمول أو محمولين لموضوعين. ثمَّ يعتبر ذلك بعينه؛ وليس شيء منها علميا . ومن المواضع أيضًا ما إذا قيل المحمول على موضوع أو على شيء بمعنى الأولى والأكثر؛ فهو مقول عليه بالإطلاق مثاله إن كان خمرًا أقل إسكارًا من خمر وأكثر فهو مسكر على الإطلاق وهذا ليس يصلح للإبطال فإنه ليس إذا لم يقل بأقل وأكثر لم يقل بإطلاق. وكثير من الأشياء لا يقبل الأقل والأكثر؛ ومثل هذا الموضع ليس له عناد علمي بل له عناد جدلي. وقريب من هذا ما إذا كان الشيء ممكناً أو موجوداً أو كذا وكذا في حال من الأحوال فهو أيضًا بتلك الحال على الإطلاق. فإن ما ليس بممكن في نفسه فليس بممكن في حال؛ وهذا أيضًا للإثبات. ويعاند هذا الموضع بأمثلة مشهورة؛ قد يوجد الشيء صالحًا في وقت؛ وليس صالحًا على الإطلاق، ويوجد الشيء غير فاسد في وقت؛ وليس غير فاسد على الإطلاق. وعلى هذا فإن المواضع كثيرة ولكل واحد منها نظائر كثيرة تعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى.
It is said: this follows the course of nature; and this is the origin of wisdom, so the ratio of that which follows the course of nature is the ratio of what occurs by way of variations. But there is consideration: for what occurs by way of variations is that which emanates from a thing, like recovery from illness. Then the benefit from these topoi may be in a specific manner related to ethical science, as it is said: if justice is praiseworthy, then the just person is praiseworthy; and sometimes not. Among them—that is, among the topoi—are the topoi of likenesses, which are like analogies, some placed as antecedent and others as consequent; and this is purely dialectical, like their saying: if opposites can be known by one science, then opposites can be opined by one opinion. Among them are the topoi of relation to unity and multiplicity; and this is suitable for refutation, as when someone posits that all knowledge is understanding, then it is said that knowledge can be of many things together, while understanding cannot be like that. And this topos is demonstrative. Among them are topoi taken from the less and the more, and they are four: First, that the more in the sense of the subject is made more in the sense of the predicate, as it is said: if pleasure is good, then what is more pleasurable is more good. Second, that another predicate is considered with the predicate, a judgment that it is more worthy of being predicated than the judgment of this one; if it is not predicated and that is not taken, then according to common opinion it is not predicated and not taken; and this is for refutation. And if what is not more worthy is found, then according to common opinion the more worthy is taken; and this is for proof. The status of this topos in the sciences differs according to the different understanding of the word 'more worthy'. Third is the opposite of this, which is that multiplicity is on the side of the two subjects, while the predicate is one; if the predicate is not found for that which is more worthy to have it, then it is not found for that which is not more worthy; and if it is found for that which is not more worthy, then it has been found for that which is more worthy. Fourth is that the consideration is between two predicates and between two subjects, and one of the two predicates is not more worthy for its subject than the other predicate for its subject. Then here there are topoi taken from equality, the same number as these topoi, and they are also four: cases of two predicates equal in the more worthy and the not more worthy in the subject, or two subjects with one predicate, or two predicates for two subjects. Then that is considered in the same way; and none of them is scientific. Among the topoi also is when the predicate is said of a subject or of something in the sense of the more worthy and the more; then it is said of it absolutely. For example: if a wine is less intoxicating than another wine and more, then it is intoxicating absolutely. And this is not suitable for refutation, for if it is not said with less and more, it is not said absolutely. And many things do not admit less and more. Such a topos has no scientific opposition, but rather dialectical opposition. Close to this is when something is possible or existent or so-and-so in some state, then it is also in that state absolutely. For what is not possible in itself is not possible in any state. And this is for proof. This topos is opposed by well-known examples: something may be good at one time, but not good absolutely; and something may be not corrupt at one time, but not not corrupt absolutely. Upon this, the topoi are many, and each has many analogues known through reflection, God willing.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of various argumentative topoi (مواضع) used in logical reasoning. The author begins by critiquing a previous view about 'following the course of nature' and then explains that these topoi can be used in ethical science. The first type mentioned is topoi of likenesses (المتشابهات), which are analogical, and purely dialectical. The second type is topoi of relation to unity and multiplicity (النسبة إلى الوحدة والكثرة), useful for refutation and demonstrative in nature. Then the author goes into detail on topoi from less and more (الأقل والأكثر), listing four specific forms. The first form deals with making the subject more in sense correlates with the predicate more; example using pleasure and goodness. The second form involves considering another predicate that is more worthy to be predicated; used for refutation or proof depending on existence of the more worthy. The third form reverses the second, focusing on multiplicity of subjects with one predicate. The fourth form considers two predicates and two subjects with no precedence. After these, the author describes topoi from equality (المساواة), which mirror the four forms of less-more but with equality instead. He notes these are not scientific. Another topos is introduced: when a predicate is said of a subject in the sense of 'more worthy', it is taken absolutely; example with wine intoxication. This is suitable for proof but not refutation, and many things do not admit degrees. A related topos involves possibility and existence: if something is possible in some state, it is possible absolutely; used for proof but opposed by counterexamples. The passage concludes by stating that these topoi are many and their analogues can be found through reflection.

Key Terms
الأقل والأكثر
أولى
إبطال
إثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the second subtype of the less-more topos is used for which purposes?
A) Proof only
B) Refutation only
C) Both proof and refutation
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The less-more topos has four subtypes; the second subtype uses the concept of 'more worthy' for both refutation and proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a less-more argument using the second subtype: If the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade a minor sin, then a major sin is even more forbidden. Use the concept of 'awlā to show that if the minor sin is forbidden, the major sin is more worthy of being forbidden.
?
Identify which of the four subtypes is used in the argument: 'If a small amount of wine is intoxicating, then a large amount is even more intoxicating.'

Topos of Equality (المساواة)
Author introduces equality topos with four forms and notes they are not scientific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four forms of the equality topos and explain why they are not scientific.

Core Idea
Equality topoi mirror less-more topoi but are not scientific—they yield only dialectical results.

The author briefly presents the topos of equality, which has the same four forms as the less-more topos but with equality instead of degrees. He explicitly states that none of these is scientific ('ليست شيء منها علميا'). This is a important reminder that not all argument forms yield certainty.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four forms of less-more
The equality topos mirrors them exactly: cases of two predicates equal in worthiness, two subjects with one predicate, two predicates for two subjects.
2
Understand the first equality form
Two predicates equal in worthiness for a subject.
3
Understand the second equality form
Two subjects with one predicate, equal in worthiness.
4
Understand the third equality form
Two predicates for two subjects, equal in worthiness.
5
Understand the author's verdict
The author says none of these is scientific. Why? Because equality topoi lead to probable conclusions, not certain ones.

Source Text

Topoi of Absolute Predication and Possibility
Author introduces absolute predication and possibility topoi with examples and counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the topos of absolute predication based on degrees and evaluate its validity using counterexamples.

Core Idea
If something is said of a thing in a more or less sense, it can be said absolutely—but this topos has dialectical, not scientific, force.

The author introduces a topos where if a predicate is said of a subject in the sense of more or less, it is said absolutely. For example, if a wine is less intoxicating than another and more, it is intoxicating absolutely. He also discusses a related topos about possibility and existence in a state implying absolute possibility/existence. However, he provides counterexamples showing these topoi are not universally valid, categorizing them as dialectical.

Guided Path
1
Understand the absolute predication topos
If X is predicated of Y in a more or less degree, then X is predicated of Y absolutely. Example with wine intoxication.
2
Identify its limitations
The author says many things do not admit less and more, so this topos does not apply universally. Also, it is not suitable for refutation.
3
Understand the possibility topos
If something is possible in some state, it is possible absolutely. Example: if can exist in a state, then can exist absolutely.
4
Examine counterexamples
The author provides: something good at one time but not good absolutely; something not corrupt at one time but absolute not corrupt. These refute the absolute predication.
5
Evaluate the topos classification
The author says these topoi have no scientific opposition but only dialectical opposition.

Source Text

يقال هذا هو جار مجرى الطبيعة؛ وهذا مأخذ الحكمة فتكون نسبة الجاري مجرى الطبيعة نسبة ما يكون على سبيل التصاريف: وفيه نظر فإن ما يكون على سبيل التصاريف فذلك ما يكون صادرًا عن الشيء كالوضع عن المرض. ثم الانتفاع بهذه المواضع قد تكون بوجه مخصوص بالعلم الخلقي كما يقال إن كانت العدالة محمودة فالعدل محمود، وقد لا تكون ومنها أي من المواضع مواضع المتشابهات وهي كأنها تمثيلات يجعل البعض منها مقدما والآخر تاليا؛ وهذا هو جدلي صرف كقولهم إن كان قد يكون بالأضداد علم واحد فقد يكون بالأضداد ظن واحد . ومنها مواضع النسبة إلى الوحدة والكثرة؛ وهذا يصلح للإبطال كمن يضع أن كل علم فهم فيقال إن العلم قد يكون بأشياء كثيرة معا. والفهم لا يمكن أن يكون كذلك ؛ وهذا الموضع برهاني. ومنها مواضع مأخوذة من الأقل والأكثر وهي أربعة: أحدها أن يجعل هو أكثر في معنى الموضوع أكثر في معنى المحمول كما يقال إن كانت اللذة خيرا فما هو أكثر لذة فهو أكثر خيرًا. والثاني أن يعتبر مع المحمول محمول آخر حكم في أن يحمل أولى من حكم هذا؛ فإن لم يحمل ولم يؤخذ ذلك ففي المشهور أن هذا لا يحمل ولا يؤخذ؛ وهذا للإبطال. وإن وجد ما ليس أولى ففي المشهور أن الأولى يؤخذ؛ وهذا للإثبات. وحال هذا الموضع في العلوم يختلف بحسب اختلاف المفهوم من لفظة «أولى». والثالث عكس ما هذا وهو أن تكون الكثرة في جهة الموضوعين، والمحمول واحد منتظر إن لم يوجد المحمول لما هو أولى أن يوجد له لم يوجد لما ليس أولى، وإن وجد لما ليس أولى فقد وجد لما هو أولى. والرابع هو أن يكون الاعتبار بین محمولين و بین موضوعين، وأحد المحمولين ليس أولى لموضوعه من المحمول الآخر بموضوعه. ثم هنا مواضع مأخوذة من المساواة بعدد هذه المواضع بأعيانها وهي أربعة أيضًا حال محمولين متساويين في الأولى وغير الأولى في الموضوع أو موضوعين بمحمول أو محمولين لموضوعين. ثمَّ يعتبر ذلك بعينه؛ وليس شيء منها علميا . ومن المواضع أيضًا ما إذا قيل المحمول على موضوع أو على شيء بمعنى الأولى والأكثر؛ فهو مقول عليه بالإطلاق مثاله إن كان خمرًا أقل إسكارًا من خمر وأكثر فهو مسكر على الإطلاق وهذا ليس يصلح للإبطال فإنه ليس إذا لم يقل بأقل وأكثر لم يقل بإطلاق. وكثير من الأشياء لا يقبل الأقل والأكثر؛ ومثل هذا الموضع ليس له عناد علمي بل له عناد جدلي. وقريب من هذا ما إذا كان الشيء ممكناً أو موجوداً أو كذا وكذا في حال من الأحوال فهو أيضًا بتلك الحال على الإطلاق. فإن ما ليس بممكن في نفسه فليس بممكن في حال؛ وهذا أيضًا للإثبات. ويعاند هذا الموضع بأمثلة مشهورة؛ قد يوجد الشيء صالحًا في وقت؛ وليس صالحًا على الإطلاق، ويوجد الشيء غير فاسد في وقت؛ وليس غير فاسد على الإطلاق. وعلى هذا فإن المواضع كثيرة ولكل واحد منها نظائر كثيرة تعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى.
It is said: this follows the course of nature; and this is the origin of wisdom, so the ratio of that which follows the course of nature is the ratio of what occurs by way of variations. But there is consideration: for what occurs by way of variations is that which emanates from a thing, like recovery from illness. Then the benefit from these topoi may be in a specific manner related to ethical science, as it is said: if justice is praiseworthy, then the just person is praiseworthy; and sometimes not. Among them—that is, among the topoi—are the topoi of likenesses, which are like analogies, some placed as antecedent and others as consequent; and this is purely dialectical, like their saying: if opposites can be known by one science, then opposites can be opined by one opinion. Among them are the topoi of relation to unity and multiplicity; and this is suitable for refutation, as when someone posits that all knowledge is understanding, then it is said that knowledge can be of many things together, while understanding cannot be like that. And this topos is demonstrative. Among them are topoi taken from the less and the more, and they are four: First, that the more in the sense of the subject is made more in the sense of the predicate, as it is said: if pleasure is good, then what is more pleasurable is more good. Second, that another predicate is considered with the predicate, a judgment that it is more worthy of being predicated than the judgment of this one; if it is not predicated and that is not taken, then according to common opinion it is not predicated and not taken; and this is for refutation. And if what is not more worthy is found, then according to common opinion the more worthy is taken; and this is for proof. The status of this topos in the sciences differs according to the different understanding of the word 'more worthy'. Third is the opposite of this, which is that multiplicity is on the side of the two subjects, while the predicate is one; if the predicate is not found for that which is more worthy to have it, then it is not found for that which is not more worthy; and if it is found for that which is not more worthy, then it has been found for that which is more worthy. Fourth is that the consideration is between two predicates and between two subjects, and one of the two predicates is not more worthy for its subject than the other predicate for its subject. Then here there are topoi taken from equality, the same number as these topoi, and they are also four: cases of two predicates equal in the more worthy and the not more worthy in the subject, or two subjects with one predicate, or two predicates for two subjects. Then that is considered in the same way; and none of them is scientific. Among the topoi also is when the predicate is said of a subject or of something in the sense of the more worthy and the more; then it is said of it absolutely. For example: if a wine is less intoxicating than another wine and more, then it is intoxicating absolutely. And this is not suitable for refutation, for if it is not said with less and more, it is not said absolutely. And many things do not admit less and more. Such a topos has no scientific opposition, but rather dialectical opposition. Close to this is when something is possible or existent or so-and-so in some state, then it is also in that state absolutely. For what is not possible in itself is not possible in any state. And this is for proof. This topos is opposed by well-known examples: something may be good at one time, but not good absolutely; and something may be not corrupt at one time, but not not corrupt absolutely. Upon this, the topoi are many, and each has many analogues known through reflection, God willing.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of various argumentative topoi (مواضع) used in logical reasoning. The author begins by critiquing a previous view about 'following the course of nature' and then explains that these topoi can be used in ethical science. The first type mentioned is topoi of likenesses (المتشابهات), which are analogical, and purely dialectical. The second type is topoi of relation to unity and multiplicity (النسبة إلى الوحدة والكثرة), useful for refutation and demonstrative in nature. Then the author goes into detail on topoi from less and more (الأقل والأكثر), listing four specific forms. The first form deals with making the subject more in sense correlates with the predicate more; example using pleasure and goodness. The second form involves considering another predicate that is more worthy to be predicated; used for refutation or proof depending on existence of the more worthy. The third form reverses the second, focusing on multiplicity of subjects with one predicate. The fourth form considers two predicates and two subjects with no precedence. After these, the author describes topoi from equality (المساواة), which mirror the four forms of less-more but with equality instead. He notes these are not scientific. Another topos is introduced: when a predicate is said of a subject in the sense of 'more worthy', it is taken absolutely; example with wine intoxication. This is suitable for proof but not refutation, and many things do not admit degrees. A related topos involves possibility and existence: if something is possible in some state, it is possible absolutely; used for proof but opposed by counterexamples. The passage concludes by stating that these topoi are many and their analogues can be found through reflection.

Key Terms
الإطلاق
أقل وأكثر
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Proof
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Topoi of absolute predication based on degrees are dialectical, not scientific, and are subject to counterexamples.

Introduction to the Topos of the Preferable
Author introduces chapter on preferable and most preferred topoi, linking to ethics and degrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the topos of the preferable (al-ūlā) from the topos of the more and less, and identify its primary domain in ethical reasoning.

Core Idea
The topos of the preferable links ethical preference to logical degrees.

In this chapter, the author turns from general topoi of more and less to a more specific one: the preferable (الأولى) and the most preferred (الأثر). While initially appearing to belong to ethics (moral matters), the author reveals that this topos extends beyond, connecting to the logic of degrees. Understanding its scope is crucial for applying it correctly in arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two topoi
Find the terms 'al-ūlā' (preferable) and 'al-athar' (most preferred). Note that they are introduced as a paired topos, with the latter being the superlative form.
These are distinct from the earlier topos of 'more and less' (al-azīd wa al-anqaṣ), but related.
2
Recognize the initial ethical domain
The passage states that the apparent subject of this topos is moral matters (umūr khuluqiyya) – what is worthy of preference or avoidance. This is the prima facie scope.
The phrase 'ظاهر الحال' indicates this is the initial impression, not the full reality.
3
Observe the expansion beyond ethics
The author then says the true investigation necessitates consideration of the preferable and its opposite, and of the more and less, which can extend beyond moral matters (قد يتعدى الأمور الخلقية). This broadens the topos.
This move links the topos to the previous chapter on degrees.
4
Relate to the previous topos of more and less
The passage explains that everything useful for arguments about more/less is also useful for arguments about the preferable/most preferred, and most of what works for the latter works for the former. This shows a reciprocal relationship.
The passage ends with a caveat ('وإن كان ليس كله كذلك على ما ظنه') indicating possible exceptions, but the text is truncated.
5
Note the incomplete argument
Recognize that the passage is cut off mid-sentence. The full argument about exceptions or differing views is not provided here.
This is a reminder that the manual contains more details in the subsequent pages.

Chapter: On the Preferable and the Most Preferred. The topoi that follow those previously mentioned are the topoi concerning the more preferred and the most preferred. The apparent state of the investigation of the most preferred and the best requires that it pertains to moral matters—only what is more worthy of preference and avoidance. But the reality of the consideration of them necessitates consideration of the preferable and the other, and of the more and the less; and that may extend beyond moral matters. As for the sharing of this investigation of the preferable and the more and what is not preferable and not more, it becomes clear to you from the fact that everything that benefits in the more and the less also benefits in the more preferred and the best, and most of what benefits in the more preferred and the best benefits in both. Even if it is not all like that, according to what he thought...

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on the topoi of the preferable (الأولى) and the most preferred (الأثر). The author states that these topoi follow the previously discussed ones. At first glance, the investigation of the most preferred and the best seems to belong to moral sciences (الأمور الخلقية), dealing with what is preferable to choose or avoid. However, the true nature of this inquiry requires looking at the preferable and its opposite, and at the more and the less (الأزيد والأنقص), which can extend beyond ethics. The author then explains the overlap between this topos and the previous one on less and more: everything useful for arguments about more and less is also useful for arguments about the most preferred and best, and vice versa for most cases. However, the passage ends mid-sentence, noting that it may not be entirely so, according to someone's opinion. This suggests the author will discuss exceptions or differing views.

The Syllogism

Formal Fallacies in Syllogistic Figures
Author classifies form-related fallacies and details their occurrence in syllogistic figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify formal fallacies in syllogisms by verifying the repetition of the middle term and the productivity of the mood.

Core Idea
A syllogism's form can fail either by lacking a valid figure or by using an unproductive mood.

The author provides a comprehensive classification of fallacies, distinguishing errors of form from errors of matter. This lesson focuses on formal fallacies, which occur when the syllogism's structure violates the conditions for productivity. You will learn to spot two common types: when the middle term is not properly repeated, and when the premises do not form a productive mood.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Threefold Classification
Note that the author divides fallacies into three categories: form, matter, or both. Formal errors are about structure, material errors about content. This lesson deals with the first category.
2
Identify the First Type of Formal Fallacy
A syllogism must have a middle term that appears in both premises. If the middle term is not repeated correctly (e.g., it appears as predicate in the minor but not as subject in the major), the argument does not conform to any figure.
Example: 'A is equal to B' and 'B is equal to C' – here the middle term 'B' is the predicate in the minor but not the subject in the major.
3
Identify the Second Type of Formal Fallacy
Even if the syllogism has a proper figure, it may not be in a productive mood. The author gives examples: indefinite premises (treated as particulars) in the first figure, and two universal affirmatives in the second figure.
Example: 'Every A is B' and 'Every C is B' – this is second figure with two affirmatives, which is not productive.
4
Apply the Conditions for Each Figure
Each figure has specific requirements for a mood to be productive. Use the standard conditions: e.g., first figure requires the minor affirmative and the major universal. If a condition is missing, the argument is a formal fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في الكلام الملخص الجامع والكلام الملخص الجامع لجميع المغالطات أن يقال الغلط في القياس المغالطي إما أن يقع من جهة الصورة، أو من جهة المادة، أو من جهة الصورة والمادة. [أ] أما الذي من جهة الصورة فبأن لا يكون القياس على هيئة شكل من الأشكال الأربعة لعدم تكرر الحد الأوسط فيه كنا يقال «أ» مساو لـ «ب» و «ب» مساو لـ «ج» فـ «أ» مساو لـ «ج»؛ فالمحمول في الصغرى وهو قوله مساو لـ «ب» لا يكون موضوعا في الكبرى. أو بأن يكون على هيئة شكل من الأشكال الأربعة لكنه لا يكون على هيئة ضرب من الضروب المنتجة كما يقال «أب»، و «بج» فإنه لا ينتج «أج» ؛ وقد كانت الصغرى مهملة، والكبرى كذلك. والمهملة في قوة الجزئية؛ ولا قياس عن جزئيتين. وكذلك في الشكل الثاني بأن يقال كل «أب»، وكل «ج ب» فإنه لا ينتج كل «أج». وعلى هذا في الثالث والرابع فإنه إذا لم يكن مشتملا على شرط من شرائط كونه منتجا يوهم أنه منتج ؛ ولا يكون منتجًا فيكون من قبل ما يكون الغلط فيه من جهة الصورة وهي التأليف. وقد مرّ من قبل ما يكون شبيها بالقياس ولا يكون قياسا في كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة اقترانية كانت الأشكال أو شرطية؛ وذلك كله من هذا القبيل أيضًا.
Chapter: On the Comprehensive Summary. The comprehensive summary that gathers all fallacies is to say: the error in a fallacy-producing syllogism either occurs from the aspect of form, or from the aspect of matter, or from both form and matter. [A] As for that which is from the aspect of form, it is that the syllogism is not in the figure of one of the four figures due to the lack of repetition of the middle term, like saying 'A is equal to B' and 'B is equal to C' so 'A is equal to C'; but the predicate in the minor premise, which is his saying 'equal to B', is not the subject in the major. Or that it is in the figure of one of the four figures but not in the form of a productive mood, as is said 'A B' and 'B C' for it does not produce 'A C'; and the minor was indefinite, and the major likewise. The indefinite is equivalent to the particular; and no syllogism from two particulars. Likewise in the second figure by saying 'Every A is B' and 'Every C is B' — it does not produce 'Every A is C'. And similarly in the third and fourth: when it does not include one of the conditions for being productive, it gives the impression that it is productive, while it is not productive. So the error is from the aspect of form, which is composition. And it has passed before what resembles a syllogism but is not a syllogism in each of the four figures, whether the figures are conjunctive or conditional; and all that is of this kind as well.
Source pages p. 598

Commentary
This passage opens a chapter summarizing the causes of fallacies in syllogisms. The author establishes the overarching classification: errors in a fallacy-producing syllogism (al-qiyas al-mughaliti) can arise from form (surah), matter (maddah), or both. The focus here is on formal errors. The author explains two main types of formal fallacies. First, a syllogism may fail to conform to any of the four figures because the middle term is not repeated: for example, saying 'A is equal to B' and 'B is equal to C' — the middle term 'B' appears as the predicate in the minor but is not the subject in the major, thus it is not properly repeated. The second type is when the syllogism has a figure but is not in a productive mood: e.g., 'A B' and 'B C' (both indefinite premises) produce nothing, since indefinite premises are treated as particular, and two particulars yield no conclusion. Similarly, in the second figure, 'Every A is B' and 'Every C is B' does not produce 'Every A is C' because the middle term is the predicate in both, requiring a negative to produce. The author notes that these conditions apply to all four figures, and any syllogism lacking a required condition only appears productive but is not. This passage closely follows the standard Aristotelian logic as adopted in Islamic tradition, where the validity conditions for categorical and conditional syllogisms are rigorously defined.

Key Terms
صورة
مادة
شكل
ضرب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the syllogism 'Every A is B, Every C is B' fail to produce a conclusion?
A) Because the middle term is not repeated.
B) Because the premises are both particular.
C) Because it lacks a negative premise in the second figure.
D) Because it is in the first figure but the minor is not affirmative.

Key Takeaway
Formal fallacies in syllogisms arise either from lacking a valid figure (middle term not properly repeated) or from using an unproductive mood within a figure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the following argument: 'Some intoxicants are forbidden. All wine is an intoxicant. Therefore, all wine is forbidden.' Is it a formal fallacy? If so, identify which type and explain why the conclusion does not follow.
?
In a kalam debate, a mutakallim argues: 'Every created thing has a creator. The world has a creator. Therefore, the world is created.' Is this syllogism valid? Apply the conditions of the second figure to determine if it is a formal fallacy.

The Enthymeme (Damir) as the Fundamental Logical Instrument
Author defines the enthymeme as the essential logical instrument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of an enthymeme and explain why it is considered the essential tool of logic.

Core Idea
The enthymeme is a syllogism with a missing premise, yet it produces assent — it is the core of logic.

In this passage, the author introduces the concept of the 'artful instrument' (al-āla al-ṣinā‘iyya al-ahliyya), which he identifies as the enthymeme (ḍamīr) or thinking (tafkīr). This instrument produces assent through a syllogism whose major premise is omitted. You will learn why this incomplete syllogism is so important and how it underlies many logical arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Artful Instrument
Find the phrase 'al-āla al-ṣinā‘iyya al-ahliyya' in the Arabic. The author says it is 'what produces assent from the perspective that it is a producer of assent through the syllogism whose major premise is omitted.' That is the enthymeme.
2
Understand the Structure
An enthymeme is a syllogism with one premise left unstated, typically the major premise. The author calls it 'thinking' (tafkīr) and says it is part of a complete syllogism. When completed, this syllogism is thought to be dialectical.
The missing premise is often considered obvious or agreed upon.
3
Connect to Action
The author states that action must be based on a principle (al-`umda) not contrary to reason and evidence. The enthymeme captures this principle implicitly.
This links logic to practical reasoning in Islamic law.
4
Apply to Islamic Sciences
Think of a fiqh argument: 'This sale is invalid because it involves gharar (uncertainty).' The missing major premise is: 'Every sale involving gharar is invalid.' That is an enthymeme.
The enthymeme makes reasoning efficient while relying on shared premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكل واحد منهما طرق كثيرة وأسباب متعددة. والخطابة لم تكون بها قوة أن يتكلف الإقناع في جميعها بل الخطابة إنما هي بالضمير وما يحسن به الضمير ؛ ويفخم به شأن الدليل هو تعقل العمدة التي عليها العمل؛ إذ العمل لا يعتبر لا عقلا ولا شرعًا إلا وأن يكون على قانون لا يكون ذلك على خلاف العقل والدليل. وأما الأحكام التشاورية فقيل ما يفترض فيها قوانين من عند أصحاب الشرائع إلا في أمور عنادية وجهادية على خلاف الأحكام التشاجرية؛ وليس لكل واحد في كل حكم بصيرة بل البعض من الأحكام مخصوص بالعض كما مرّ.

وبالجملة كأنه يجب صرف الشغل إلى الآلة الصناعية الأهلية وهو ما يوقع التصديق من حيث هو موقع للتصديق بالقياس المحذوف كبراه؛ وهو الذي يسمَّى تفكيرًا أو ضميرًا. وقد عرفت أن التفكير جزء من قياس ذلك القياس إذا تم كان مظنونا به أنه جدلي؛ وذلك لأن الجدلي هو الذي يكون من محمودات حقيقية، والخطابي هو الذي يكون من محمودات بحسب بادي الرأي. ولما كان النظر في القياس الجدلي الذي يسمى منطقيا لصناعة المنطق . فالنظر في الشبيه به لصناعة إذا كان النظر في الحق، وما يشبه الحق لصناعة واحدة؛ فالنظر في المحمودات حقًا، والمحمودات ظنا، وفي استعمالها لصناعة واحدة كما أن النظر في الصادق. والحق الذي منه ينبعث البرهان، وفي المحمود الذي منه ينبعث الجدل لصناعة واحدة إذا كانت الصناعة المنطقية بالاستحقاق الأول هو البرهان؛ وكان الجدل شبيها به إذا كانت المحمودات تشبه الحق . وغرائز الناس مشغوفة بالتماس الحق لكن السبيل إليه صعب؛ فمنهم من يوفّق له، ومنهم من يضع إلى التشبيه به.
And each of them has many ways and multiple causes. And rhetoric does not have the power to undertake persuasion in all of them; rather, rhetoric is only through the enthymeme (al-ḍamīr) and what beautifies the enthymeme; and what magnifies the significance of the evidence is the comprehension of the principle (al-`umda) upon which action is based. For action is not considered rationally or legally unless it is based on a law that is not contrary to reason and evidence. As for consultative judgments (al-aḥkām al-tashāwuriyya), it is said that laws from the possessors of religious laws are not prescribed in them except in adversarial and jihādī matters, contrary to contentious judgments (al-aḥkām al-tashājiriyya). And not everyone has insight into every judgment; rather, some judgments are specific to some individuals as has passed.

In summary, it seems necessary to direct effort to the artful instrument (al-āla al-ṣinā‘iyya al-ahliyya), which is what produces assent from the perspective that it is a producer of assent through the syllogism whose major premise is omitted; and that is called thinking (tafkīr) or enthymeme (ḍamīr). And you have known that thinking is part of that syllogism; when that syllogism is completed, it is thought to be dialectical. That is because the dialectical is that which is from real praiseworthy things, and the rhetorical is that which is from praiseworthy things according to initial opinion. And since the consideration of the dialectical syllogism, which is called logical (manṭiqī) for the art of logic, is one thing, then the consideration of what resembles it belongs to one art if the consideration is of truth and what resembles truth belongs to one art. Thus the consideration of things praiseworthy in truth, and things praiseworthy in opinion, and their use belong to one art, just as the consideration of the true. And the truth from which demonstration (burhān) emanates, and the praiseworthy from which dialectic emanates, belong to one art, if the logical art in primary deservingness is demonstration; and dialectic is similar to it because the praiseworthy things resemble the truth. And human natures are enamored with seeking truth, but the path to it is difficult; so among them are those who are granted success for it, and among them those who go to what resembles it.
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage begins by noting that both apparent and inner rectitude have many paths and causes. It then focuses on rhetoric: rhetoric does not attempt full persuasion in all cases, but relies on the enthymeme (ḍamīr) and what beautifies it. The key to magnifying the evidence is grasping the principle (al-`umda) upon which action is based; action is only valid if it follows a law not contrary to reason and evidence. Next, it discusses consultative judgments (tashāwuriyya) versus contentious judgments (tashājiriyya). For consultative matters, religious laws are not prescribed except in adversarial or jihādī contexts, and not everyone has insight into every judgment—some judgments are specific to certain people.

The author then summarizes: effort should be directed to the 'artful instrument' (al-āla al-ṣinā‘iyya al-ahliyya), which produces assent through a syllogism with a missing major premise—called tafkīr (thinking) or ḍamīr (enthymeme). Thinking is part of that syllogism, and when completed, it is thought to be dialectical. Dialectical syllogisms use real praiseworthy premises (maḥmūdāt ḥaqīqiyya), while rhetorical ones use praiseworthy according to initial opinion (bādī al-ra’y).

The argument then shifts to the unity of logical arts: since logic (manṭiq) studies the dialectical syllogism, and since dialectic resembles demonstration (burhān) because its premises resemble truth, the study of what resembles truth belongs to the same art. Therefore, the study of truly praiseworthy premises (for demonstration) and apparently praiseworthy premises (for dialectic) and their use all fall under one art—logic—just like the study of truth itself. The primary logical art is demonstration, but dialectic is included because it resembles it. Finally, the passage notes that human nature loves truth but finds the path difficult; some succeed, others settle for what resembles truth.

Key Terms
ضمير (ḍamīr): Enthymeme: a syllogism with an omitted premise, which produces assent; also called tafkīr (thinking). [Note: No significant shift — this term is stable across the tradition.]
تفكير (tafkīr): Thinking, identified as part of the syllogism and synonymous with the enthymeme (ḍamīr) here. [Note: No significant shift.]
الآلة الصناعية الأهلية (al-āla al-ṣinā‘iyya al-ahliyya): The 'artful instrument' that produces assent via enthymeme; the essential tool of logic. [Note: This specific phrase may vary across texts; in this work it refers to the enthymeme as the primary logical instrument.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'artful instrument' (al-āla al-ṣinā‘iyya al-ahliyya) that produces assent?
A) A complete categorical syllogism.
B) A syllogism with an omitted major premise, called an enthymeme (ḍamīr).
C) A rhetorical speech full of persuasive figures.
D) A dialectical debate involving questions and answers.

Key Takeaway
The enthymeme (ḍamīr) is a syllogism with an omitted major premise that produces assent and is the essential instrument of logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an enthymeme on a fiqh topic: e.g., 'Wine is forbidden because it intoxicates.' Identify the missing major premise (e.g., 'All intoxicants are forbidden').
?
Find a real example from a kalam text where a syllogism is presented with a missing premise and explain which premise is omitted.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Demonstration and Dialectic Belong to One Logical Art
Author argues that demonstration and dialectic belong to one logical art based on the resemblance of their premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the analogical argument that demonstration (burhān) and dialectic (jadal) belong to a single logical art.

Core Idea
Truth and its resemblance to truth belong to one art — thus demonstration and dialectic are unified.

The author extends his discussion of the enthymeme to argue that demonstration and dialectic are not separate arts but fall under the one discipline of logic. He uses an analogy: just as truth and what resembles truth are studied together, so the premises of demonstration (true) and dialectic (praiseworthy) belong to one logical art. This lesson will walk you through that analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Types of Premises
Dialectic uses real praiseworthy premises (maḥmūdāt ḥaqīqiyya), rhetoric uses apparent praiseworthy premises (maḥmūdāt bi-ḥasab bādī al-ra’y). Demonstration uses true premises.
2
Understand the Analogy
The author states: 'if the consideration is of truth and what resembles truth belongs to one art, then the consideration of praiseworthy premises in truth and in opinion and their use belong to one art.' The analogy is between truth/resemblance and truly praiseworthy/apparently praiseworthy.
3
Apply to Demonstration and Dialectic
Demonstration (burhān) is the primary logical art because it deals with truth. Dialectic (jadal) is secondary because it deals with what resembles truth. Yet both are studied under logic because of this resemblance.
4
Consider the Implications
This argument establishes the unity of the logical sciences. It prevents fragmentation and shows that the methods of proof and debate share a common foundation.
This is a key argument in the Islamic logical tradition.

Key Terms
برهان (burhān): Demonstration: the highest form of syllogism yielding certainty, using true premises. [Note: No significant shift.]
جدلي (jadālī): Dialectical: a syllogism using real praiseworthy premises, resembling demonstration. [Note: No significant shift.]
خطابي (khaṭābī): Rhetorical: a syllogism using praiseworthy premises according to initial opinion. [Note: No significant shift.]
محمودات (maḥmūdāt): Praiseworthy things/premises; dialectic uses real praiseworthy premises, rhetoric uses apparent ones. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, why do demonstration and dialectic belong to one logical art?
A) Both use exactly the same premises.
B) Dialectic's premises (praiseworthy) resemble demonstration's premises (true), just as truth and its resemblance are studied together.
C) The author does not give a reason.
D) Because dialectic is a type of demonstration.

Key Takeaway
Demonstration and dialectic belong to one logical art because dialectic's premises resemble the truth used in demonstration, analogous to how truth and its resemblance are studied together.

The Classification of Tathbit: Damir, Tafakkur, I'tibar, Burhan
Defines tathbit and its subtypes: damir, tafakkur, i'tibar, burhan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the four subtypes of tathbit (damir, tafakkur, i'tibar, burhan) by their definitions and identify which art each is primarily associated with.

Core Idea
Tathbit is the genus of persuasion; its subtypes differ by form and application.

In this passage, the author establishes the foundational classification of persuasive reasoning. Understanding the precise meanings of tathbit, damir, tafakkur, i'tibar, and burhan is crucial because each term corresponds to a distinct logical form used in different Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus: tathbit
Find the definition of tathbit in the passage: 'a statement intended to persuade assent to the conclusion itself'. Notice that it covers all the subtypes.
Tathbit is the broadest category.
2
Distinguish damir and tafakkur
The passage says they are one in subject but differ in aspect. Damir is considered from the perspective of having a missing premise, while tafakkur is from the perspective of seeking the middle term.
Both refer to the same logical structure but with different emphases.
3
Understand i'tibar and burhan
I'tibar is defined as 'consideration by way of example' (tamthil). Burhan is a type of i'tibar that 'quickly achieves the purpose'.
Burhan here is a faster form of example-based reasoning, not necessarily a full syllogism.
4
Map subtypes to arts
The passage states: dialectic uses qiyas and istiqra'; rhetoric uses damir and tamthil. Note that damir is an implicit syllogism (a kind of qiyas), so rhetoric uses a compressed form of syllogism, while dialectic uses full syllogisms.
This mapping helps in selecting the appropriate reasoning type for different contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في العمود وهو التثبيت وأقسامه فنقول يقال تثبيت وتفكر وضمير واعتبار وبرهان وبينها فرق، فالتثبيت هو قول يراد به إقناع التصديق بالمطلوب نفسه وهو يعم جميع ذلك لكن الضمير هو ما كان منه قياسًا، والاعتبار ما كان تمثيلا. واسم البرهان في هذا الكتاب يقع على اعتبار يتم به المقصود سريعا. والتفكير هو الضمير بعينه في الموضوع ولكن من حيث إن يعتبر بالحد الأوسط وهو أن يؤخذ فيه وسط يقتضيه الفكر هو تفكير، ومن حيث فيه نقصان مقدمة وهو ضمير؛ فيكون التفكير والضمير واحدًا بالموضوع. وكما أن الجدل مقولة على قياس واستقراء كذلك الخطابة مقولها على ضمير وتمثيل . وكل ذلك إما أصلي وإما مظنون. وقد يكون في البيانات البرهانية ضمائر قد حذفت كبرياتها، وتكون تلك الضمائر البرهانية في قوة القياسات فإن كبرياتها إنما تحذف لوضوحها، وعلى سبيل الاختصار. وكذلك في الجدل الذي ليس على سبيل المغالطات. وأما الخطابة فإنما تحذف الكبريات فيها لأنه لو صرّح بها لزال الإقناع لأن تلك الأحكام إذا كانت حاضرت بالكلية علم كذبها وخصوصا في المستويات. وقد تحذف أيضًا لئلا يكون البيان منطقيًا فإن الخطيب إذا نسب إلى مخاطبته ((منطقية)) أو ((كلامية)) يوهم أن اقتداره لصناعة أخرى دائما يغلب لفضل قوته في المنطق لا لفضل إصابته ، فالأولى أن يخاطب خطابًا عاميًا. وكما أن حال الخطابة في استعمال الضمير بعكس حال الجدل والعلوم فكذلك انتفاعها باستعمال الاعتبار ؛ والقياس هو بعكس حال الجدل والعلوم؛ إذ القياس أشد إلزاما في الجدل وأشد تحقيقا في العلوم من الاعتبار والاستقراء لكن الاعتبار في الخطابة أقرب إلى إقناع الجمهور من الضمير لأن الضمير وما يجري مجرى القياس يحتمل كثرة المراجعة في سؤال ما كانت المقدمة. ومثل هذا السؤال لا يكون في أمور ظاهر مسلمة. والفرق بين المثال والاستقراء فقد مرَّ ؛ ويشترك المثال والضمير في أن كل واحد منهما يفيد إقناعا أي يجعل شيئًا لم يضع به مقنعا . فإن كل مقنع إما مقنع في نفسه، وإما مقنع في غيره لكنه ما لم يكن مقنعا في نفسه لم يكن مقنعا في غيره. والمقنع في نفسه هو المحمود؛ والمحمود قد يكون بحسب البعض، وقد يكون بحسب الكل من الأشخاص، والآراء تختلف بحسب الأهواء. ومثل هذه المحمودات وإن صلحت لأن يستعمل في كثير من القياسات الخطابية فإنها لا تصلح لأن تجعل عمدة في الصناعة؛ فإن أحوالها غير متناهية والمحمودات محتاج إليها في الجدل على شرط المنطق يعني أن يكون المؤلف منها قياسًا بشرائط وهي أن تكون المقدمات حقيقية الحمل وصحيحة التأليف، وعلى نظم قياس إما بالفعل وإما بالقوة. وأما الخطابة فإنه يكفي فيها أن تكون المقدمات محمودة في الظاهر بأن يكون الناس يرونها لا على سبيل سوي بل على سبيل اعتقادهم في أنفسهم إما بواجب، وإما باعتبار. فمن ذلك محمودات حقيقية وهي المحمودات في بادي الرأي فالخطابي يستعمل المحمود الحقيقي والمحمود بحسب الظن.
Chapter: On the pillar, which is confirmation and its divisions. We say: terms are used: confirmation, reflection, implicit syllogism, consideration, demonstration—there is a difference between them. Confirmation is a statement intended to persuade assent to the desired conclusion itself, and it covers all that. However, the implicit syllogism (damir) is what among it is a syllogism, and consideration (i'tibar) is what is an example. The name 'demonstration' (burhan) in this book applies to a consideration by which the purpose is quickly achieved. Reflection (tafakkur) is the very same as the implicit syllogism in subject, but from the aspect of considering the middle term—that a middle term required by thought is taken, it is reflection; and from the aspect of having a missing premise, it is an implicit syllogism. Thus reflection and implicit syllogism are one in subject. And just as dialectic is predicated of syllogism and induction, so rhetoric is predicated of implicit syllogism and example. All that is either original or presumed. And in demonstrative expositions there may be implicit syllogisms whose major premises are omitted, and those demonstrative implicit syllogisms are in the power of syllogisms, because their major premises are omitted only due to their clarity and for brevity. Similarly in dialectic that is not sophistical. As for rhetoric, major premises are omitted because if they were stated explicitly, persuasion would vanish, because if those judgments were present in their entirety, their falsity would be known, especially in the common [matters]. They may also be omitted so that the exposition not be logical; for if the orator is accused of being 'logical' or 'theological' by his audience, it gives the impression that his power is due to his strength in logic, not due to the correctness of his opinion. So it is better to address them in a popular speech. And just as the state of rhetoric in using the implicit syllogism is opposite to the state of dialectic and the sciences, so also its benefit in using consideration; and the syllogism is opposite to the state of dialectic and the sciences. For the syllogism is more binding in dialectic and more verifying in the sciences than consideration and induction. But consideration in rhetoric is closer to persuading the public than the implicit syllogism, because the implicit syllogism and what proceeds in the manner of a syllogism are susceptible to frequent reexamination in asking what the premise was. Such questioning does not occur in matters that are outwardly conceded. The difference between example and induction has already passed. And example and implicit syllogism share that each of them yields persuasion, i.e., makes something that is not persuasive in itself become persuasive. For every persuasive thing is either persuasive in itself or persuasive through something else, but as long as it is not persuasive in itself, it is not persuasive through something else. What is persuasive in itself is the commendable. The commendable may be according to some, or according to all persons; opinions differ according to inclinations. Such commendable premises, even if they are suitable for use in many rhetorical syllogisms, are not suitable to be made a foundation in the art, because their conditions are infinite. Commendable premises are needed in dialectic under the condition of logic, meaning that a syllogism composed of them should have conditions: that the premises be truly predicated and correctly composed, and in the form of a syllogism either actually or potentially. As for rhetoric, it suffices that the premises be commendable in appearance, in that people see them—not in a sound way, but according to their own belief, either as obligatory or as considered. Among these are genuine commendables, which are commendables at first glance. So the orator uses both the genuine commendable and the commendable according to opinion.
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory section on the concept of 'al-`amud' (the pillar), which is also called 'al-tathbit' (confirmation). The author first lists several terms: tathbit, tafakkur, damir, i'tibar, and burhan, and states they differ. Tathbit is the broadest: a statement intended to persuade assent to the conclusion itself. Under it, damir is what is a syllogism (qiyas), and i'tibar is what is an example (tamthil). Burhan is a type of i'tibar that quickly achieves the purpose. Tafakkur is identified as the same as damir but considered from the perspective of the middle term; when a middle term is sought by thought, it is tafakkur; when a premise is missing, it is damir. Thus they are one in subject. The author then links these to the arts: dialectic uses qiyas and istiqra' (induction), while rhetoric uses damir and tamthil. All can be original or conjectural. The passage then discusses the omission of major premises in different arts. In demonstration, major premises are omitted for clarity and brevity. In dialectic (non-sophistical), similarly. In rhetoric, major premises are omitted because stating them would destroy persuasion, as their falsity might be obvious. They are also omitted to avoid the appearance of being logical or theological, which might make the audience suspect the orator is relying on logic rather than correctness. The author then compares the use of damir and qiyas in rhetoric versus dialectic and sciences: qiyas is more binding and verifying in dialectic and sciences, but in rhetoric, i'tibar (example) is more persuasive to the public because damir invites scrutiny. The difference between example and induction is said to have been mentioned earlier. Example and damir share the property of making something persuasive. Every persuasive thing is either persuasive in itself or through another; what is persuasive in itself is called 'al-mahmud' (the commendable). The commendable can be relative to some or all people. Such commendable premises are useful in rhetorical arguments but cannot be foundational in the art due to their variability. For dialectic, commendable premises must meet logical conditions: true predication, correct composition, and syllogistic form actually or potentially. For rhetoric, it suffices that premises appear commendable to people, either as obligatory or as a matter of consideration. Thus the orator uses both genuine commendables (those at first glance) and those according to opinion.
