The Art-Specific Use of Reasoning Types: Why Rhetoric Omits Premises
Explains omission of major premises in rhetoric vs other arts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why major premises are omitted in rhetorical arguments according to the author, and contrast this with their use in demonstration and dialectic.

Core Idea
Rhetoric omits major premises because stating them would destroy persuasion, while demonstration omits them for brevity and clarity.

The author explains a crucial practical difference: in different arts, the same logical form (enthymeme) is used for different reasons. Understanding why premises are omitted helps you become a more effective communicator in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three arts
The passage discusses demonstration (burhan), dialectic (jadal), and rhetoric (khataba). For each, note how they use reasoning types.
Each art has its own characteristic method.
2
Find reasons for omitting major premises
In demonstration, major premises are omitted due to clarity and brevity. In dialectic (non-sophistical), similarly. In rhetoric, they are omitted because stating them would reveal their falsity and ruin persuasion, and also to avoid the appearance of being logical/theological.
The opposite state: rhetoric uses damir more, while dialectic uses full qiyas.
3
Compare effectiveness of i'tibar and damir in rhetoric
The passage says i'tibar (example) is closer to persuading the public than damir, because damir invites scrutiny by the audience asking about the missing premise.
Examples are less susceptible to questioning.
4
Apply to real rhetorical situations
If you are a khatib, you would choose examples over syllogisms to avoid logical scrutiny and maintain emotional appeal.
This strategic omission is a key skill in Islamic oratory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في العمود وهو التثبيت وأقسامه فنقول يقال تثبيت وتفكر وضمير واعتبار وبرهان وبينها فرق، فالتثبيت هو قول يراد به إقناع التصديق بالمطلوب نفسه وهو يعم جميع ذلك لكن الضمير هو ما كان منه قياسًا، والاعتبار ما كان تمثيلا. واسم البرهان في هذا الكتاب يقع على اعتبار يتم به المقصود سريعا. والتفكير هو الضمير بعينه في الموضوع ولكن من حيث إن يعتبر بالحد الأوسط وهو أن يؤخذ فيه وسط يقتضيه الفكر هو تفكير، ومن حيث فيه نقصان مقدمة وهو ضمير؛ فيكون التفكير والضمير واحدًا بالموضوع. وكما أن الجدل مقولة على قياس واستقراء كذلك الخطابة مقولها على ضمير وتمثيل . وكل ذلك إما أصلي وإما مظنون. وقد يكون في البيانات البرهانية ضمائر قد حذفت كبرياتها، وتكون تلك الضمائر البرهانية في قوة القياسات فإن كبرياتها إنما تحذف لوضوحها، وعلى سبيل الاختصار. وكذلك في الجدل الذي ليس على سبيل المغالطات. وأما الخطابة فإنما تحذف الكبريات فيها لأنه لو صرّح بها لزال الإقناع لأن تلك الأحكام إذا كانت حاضرت بالكلية علم كذبها وخصوصا في المستويات. وقد تحذف أيضًا لئلا يكون البيان منطقيًا فإن الخطيب إذا نسب إلى مخاطبته ((منطقية)) أو ((كلامية)) يوهم أن اقتداره لصناعة أخرى دائما يغلب لفضل قوته في المنطق لا لفضل إصابته ، فالأولى أن يخاطب خطابًا عاميًا. وكما أن حال الخطابة في استعمال الضمير بعكس حال الجدل والعلوم فكذلك انتفاعها باستعمال الاعتبار ؛ والقياس هو بعكس حال الجدل والعلوم؛ إذ القياس أشد إلزاما في الجدل وأشد تحقيقا في العلوم من الاعتبار والاستقراء لكن الاعتبار في الخطابة أقرب إلى إقناع الجمهور من الضمير لأن الضمير وما يجري مجرى القياس يحتمل كثرة المراجعة في سؤال ما كانت المقدمة. ومثل هذا السؤال لا يكون في أمور ظاهر مسلمة. والفرق بين المثال والاستقراء فقد مرَّ ؛ ويشترك المثال والضمير في أن كل واحد منهما يفيد إقناعا أي يجعل شيئًا لم يضع به مقنعا . فإن كل مقنع إما مقنع في نفسه، وإما مقنع في غيره لكنه ما لم يكن مقنعا في نفسه لم يكن مقنعا في غيره. والمقنع في نفسه هو المحمود؛ والمحمود قد يكون بحسب البعض، وقد يكون بحسب الكل من الأشخاص، والآراء تختلف بحسب الأهواء. ومثل هذه المحمودات وإن صلحت لأن يستعمل في كثير من القياسات الخطابية فإنها لا تصلح لأن تجعل عمدة في الصناعة؛ فإن أحوالها غير متناهية والمحمودات محتاج إليها في الجدل على شرط المنطق يعني أن يكون المؤلف منها قياسًا بشرائط وهي أن تكون المقدمات حقيقية الحمل وصحيحة التأليف، وعلى نظم قياس إما بالفعل وإما بالقوة. وأما الخطابة فإنه يكفي فيها أن تكون المقدمات محمودة في الظاهر بأن يكون الناس يرونها لا على سبيل سوي بل على سبيل اعتقادهم في أنفسهم إما بواجب، وإما باعتبار. فمن ذلك محمودات حقيقية وهي المحمودات في بادي الرأي فالخطابي يستعمل المحمود الحقيقي والمحمود بحسب الظن.
Chapter: On the pillar, which is confirmation and its divisions. We say: terms are used: confirmation, reflection, implicit syllogism, consideration, demonstration—there is a difference between them. Confirmation is a statement intended to persuade assent to the desired conclusion itself, and it covers all that. However, the implicit syllogism (damir) is what among it is a syllogism, and consideration (i'tibar) is what is an example. The name 'demonstration' (burhan) in this book applies to a consideration by which the purpose is quickly achieved. Reflection (tafakkur) is the very same as the implicit syllogism in subject, but from the aspect of considering the middle term—that a middle term required by thought is taken, it is reflection; and from the aspect of having a missing premise, it is an implicit syllogism. Thus reflection and implicit syllogism are one in subject. And just as dialectic is predicated of syllogism and induction, so rhetoric is predicated of implicit syllogism and example. All that is either original or presumed. And in demonstrative expositions there may be implicit syllogisms whose major premises are omitted, and those demonstrative implicit syllogisms are in the power of syllogisms, because their major premises are omitted only due to their clarity and for brevity. Similarly in dialectic that is not sophistical. As for rhetoric, major premises are omitted because if they were stated explicitly, persuasion would vanish, because if those judgments were present in their entirety, their falsity would be known, especially in the common [matters]. They may also be omitted so that the exposition not be logical; for if the orator is accused of being 'logical' or 'theological' by his audience, it gives the impression that his power is due to his strength in logic, not due to the correctness of his opinion. So it is better to address them in a popular speech. And just as the state of rhetoric in using the implicit syllogism is opposite to the state of dialectic and the sciences, so also its benefit in using consideration; and the syllogism is opposite to the state of dialectic and the sciences. For the syllogism is more binding in dialectic and more verifying in the sciences than consideration and induction. But consideration in rhetoric is closer to persuading the public than the implicit syllogism, because the implicit syllogism and what proceeds in the manner of a syllogism are susceptible to frequent reexamination in asking what the premise was. Such questioning does not occur in matters that are outwardly conceded. The difference between example and induction has already passed. And example and implicit syllogism share that each of them yields persuasion, i.e., makes something that is not persuasive in itself become persuasive. For every persuasive thing is either persuasive in itself or persuasive through something else, but as long as it is not persuasive in itself, it is not persuasive through something else. What is persuasive in itself is the commendable. The commendable may be according to some, or according to all persons; opinions differ according to inclinations. Such commendable premises, even if they are suitable for use in many rhetorical syllogisms, are not suitable to be made a foundation in the art, because their conditions are infinite. Commendable premises are needed in dialectic under the condition of logic, meaning that a syllogism composed of them should have conditions: that the premises be truly predicated and correctly composed, and in the form of a syllogism either actually or potentially. As for rhetoric, it suffices that the premises be commendable in appearance, in that people see them—not in a sound way, but according to their own belief, either as obligatory or as considered. Among these are genuine commendables, which are commendables at first glance. So the orator uses both the genuine commendable and the commendable according to opinion.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Three Modalities of Implicit Syllogisms
Classification of implicit syllogisms by premise modality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify implicit syllogisms according to the modality of their omitted premise: necessary, probable, or equal.

Core Idea
Implicit syllogisms range from certain (necessary) to merely persuasive (probable and equal).

In rhetorical reasoning, we often omit a premise that is understood by the audience. But not all omitted premises are equally certain. The author distinguishes between those that are necessarily true, those that are true in most cases (probable), and those that are equally likely to be true or false (equal). This classification determines the force of the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modalities
The author lists three types of premises for implicit syllogisms: necessary (daruriyyat), probable (akthariyyat), and equal (mutasawiyat).
2
Examine the necessary example
For necessary premises, he gives: 'Zayd is a scholar, pure of soul, and the scholar pure of soul is happy in the afterlife.' The major premise is omitted but understood as a universal truth.
The omitted major is: 'Every scholar pure of soul is happy in the afterlife.'
3
Examine the probable example
For probable premises, he says: 'Zayd is light in harm, so he is loved.' The major premise 'Whoever is light in harm is loved' is true in most cases, not all.
4
Examine the equal example
For equal premises, the sign points to both a thing and its opposite. Example: 'So-and-so is standing at the head of the slain Zayd with a sword, so he is not the killer?' This is true in most cases but can be false.
These require a suggestion of probability based on opinion, not reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يلزم أن تكون التفكيرات والضمائر عن ممكنات بالتساوي بل قد تكون عن ضروريات وعن أكثريات، والضمائر الموجودة في كل واحد منهما قد تكون من الصادقات أي من المحمودات الحقيقية، وقد تكون من الدلائل: فمثال ما يكون من المحمولات في الضروريات قولنا زيد عالم زكي النفس، والعالم الزكي النفس سعيد في الآخرة؛ وهذه المقدمة المحمودة قد تحذف وتستعمل قوتها. وإنما تصرّح بها مهملة؛ لئلا يكون المقول على الكل من حيث هو مقول على الكل مصرَّحًا به ؛ وهذا من جملة ما يكون تأليفه على نهج الشكل الأول ومثال ما هو من الدليل بالقسمة الخاصة قولنا هذه المرأة ولدت فهي المقتصة؛ فجعل الولادة دليلا على الاقتصاص وهو دليل صدق؛ فيلزم أن يكون معه أو أخص منه، ولذلك يكون على قوة الشكل الأول. وأما العلامة فهي حكم إما أن يكون المحمول يلزمه وهو لا يلزم الموضوع أو هو يلزم الموضوع، والمحمول لا يلزمه. فإنه إذا لزمه المحمول ويلزم هو الموضوع كان دليلا فانعقد الشكل الأول. فالعلامة الأولى منهما تتبين بالشكل الثالث كقولنا الفقيه عفيف لأن زيدًا الفقيه عفيف؛ والصدق في هذا الكلام أن يقال إن زيدًا فقيه وزيدًا عفيف فكل فقيه عفيف. فيكون زيد علامة لكون الفقيه عفيفاً لكن الفقيه يلزم زيدًا أو زيد لا يلزم الفقيه حتى يكون كل فقیه زيدا. والعلامة الثانية تكون من الشكل الثاني في مثل قولهم هذه منتفخة البطن فهي إذن حبلى؛ والصدق في هذا الكلام أن يقال هذه منتفخة البطن، والحبلى منتفخة البطن فيكون انتفاح البطن علامة الحبل لكن الانتفاخ قد يوجد بدون الحبل. وأما أمثلة هذه في الأكثريات فالكبرى في القياس منها محمودة بالحقيقة لكنها لا تكون صادقة في الكل بل في الأكثر من الأشخاص أو الأكثر من الاعتبارات مثل قولهم زيد كاف الأذى فهو محبوب؛ ويكون الدليل الأكثري مثل قولهم زيد محموم فهو إذن سريع النبض؛ وهذا يسمى دليل الأولى والأشبه عند قوم. وأما العلامة من الشكل الثاني فأن يقال زيد سريع النبض مثلاً فهو محموم. وأما العلامة فيها من الشكل الثالث فمثل أن يقال مثلا الشجعان لا يبخلون لأن علياً لا يبخل ؛ فهذه ثمانية وجوه من الضمائر عن الضروريات والأكثريات. وأما الكائنة عن المتساويات فهي التي يكون فيها المعنى علامة للشيء ولنقيضه جميعًا لكنه يكون علامة لأحدهما بنفسه، وللنقيض بواسطة، أو يكون علامة للأمرين بواسطتين. ولا بد من تلويح أكثرية فيه لا بحسب الحقيقة بل بحسب الظنِّ وإلا لا يكون هناك مثل النفس ومثال هذا قول القائل فلان قائم على رأس زيد القتيل الطري منتصب بسيفه فليس هو يقابله؛ وهذا مما يصدق في الأكثر، ويكذب في الأقل. وقد يراد في العلامة شيء كما يقال فلان قائم على رأس زيد القتيل الطري المختون دمه وكذا وكذا؛ وحينئذ يكون الضمير غير الضمير الذي مرَّ ذكره فتكون الضمائر تسعة، وعلى هذا فاعتبر فإن القياس الخطابي يستعمل في العلوم الخلقية والطبيعية والسياسية وغير ذلك.
And it is not necessary that the thoughts and implicit syllogisms be about possibilities equally; rather, they may be about necessities and about probabilities. The implicit syllogisms present in each of them may be from true premises, i.e., from real accepted premises, and may be from indications. An example of what is from the predicated in necessities is our saying: 'Zayd is a scholar, pure of soul, and the scholar pure of soul is happy in the afterlife.' This accepted premise may be omitted and its force used. It is explicitly stated as a universal; so that the statement about all insofar as it is about all is not explicitly stated. This is among what has its composition on the pattern of the first figure. And an example of what is from the indication by specific division is our saying: 'This woman gave birth, so she is the one who is subject to retaliation (muqtassa).' So he made giving birth an indication of retaliation, and it is an indication of truth. So it must be that it is with it or more specific than it, and therefore it has the force of the first figure. As for the sign (al-'alama), it is a judgment such that either the predicate accompanies it and it does not accompany the subject, or it accompanies the subject and the predicate does not accompany it. For if the predicate accompanies it and it accompanies the subject, it becomes an indication and the first figure is formed. The first of these two signs is made clear by the third figure, as in our saying: 'The jurist is chaste because Zayd the jurist is chaste.' The truth in this statement is to say: 'Zayd is a jurist and Zayd is chaste, so every jurist is chaste.' So Zayd becomes a sign that the jurist is chaste, but the jurist accompanies Zayd, or Zayd does not accompany the jurist so that every jurist is Zayd. The second sign is from the second figure in such as their saying: 'This woman has a swollen belly, so she is pregnant.' The truth in this statement is to say: 'This woman has a swollen belly, and the pregnant woman has a swollen belly,' so swollen belly is a sign of pregnancy, but swelling may exist without pregnancy. As for examples of these in probabilities, the major premise in the syllogism is truly accepted but is not true in all cases, rather in most persons or most considerations, like their saying: 'Zayd is light in harm, so he is loved.' And the probable indication is like their saying: 'Zayd is feverish, so he has a rapid pulse.' This is called 'indication of the first and more appropriate' by some people. As for the sign from the second figure, it is that it is said: 'Zayd has a rapid pulse, for example, so he is feverish.' As for the sign from the third figure, it is like saying, for example: 'Brave men are not stingy because 'Ali is not stingy.' These are eight kinds of implicit syllogisms from necessities and probabilities. As for those arising from equal probabilities, they are those in which the meaning is a sign for the thing and its opposite together, but it is a sign for one of them by itself and for the opposite through a middle, or it is a sign for both through two middles. There must be a suggestion of probability in it, not according to reality but according to opinion; otherwise there would be no such thing as the soul. And an example of this is a speaker's saying: 'So-and-so is standing at the head of the slain Zayd, freshly erected with his sword, so he is not his killer?' This is true in most cases and false in the minority. And something may be intended in the sign, as it is said: 'So-and-so is standing at the head of the slain Zayd, his blood freshly flowing, etc.' Then the implicit syllogism is different from the one previously mentioned, making the implicit syllogisms nine. On this basis, reflect, for the rhetorical syllogism is used in the ethical, natural, political sciences, and others.
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This is a syllogistic passage that categorizes implicit syllogisms (al-damair) according to the modality of their premises: necessary (daruriyyat), probable (akthariyyat), and equal (mutasawiyat). The author begins by stating that thoughts (tafkirat) and implicit syllogisms (damair) are not limited to equal possibilities but include necessary and probable premises. Each type may use true accepted premises (sadiqat) or indications (dala'il). He provides examples: for necessary premises, 'Zayd is a scholar, pure of soul, and the scholar pure of soul is happy in the afterlife' – the major premise is omitted but its force used. For indication by specific division (qismat khas), he gives the example of a woman giving birth indicating retaliation (qisas). He then defines the 'sign' (al-'alama) as a relation where the predicate accompanies the sign and the sign accompanies the subject, forming the first figure. Alternatively, the sign may not accompany the subject, leading to other figures. He explains two types: one made clear by the third figure (e.g., 'The jurist is chaste because Zayd the jurist is chaste' – Zayd is a sign for the connection between jurist and chastity), and another by the second figure (e.g., 'This woman has a swollen belly, so she is pregnant' – swollen belly is a sign of pregnancy, but not exclusive). Then he discusses probable examples: major premises that are true in most cases (akthari), like 'Zayd is light in harm, so he is loved.' He further distinguishes signs in the second figure (rapid pulse indicates fever) and third figure (brave men are not stingy because 'Ali is not stingy). He counts eight types of damair from necessities and probabilities. Next, he addresses implicit syllogisms from equal probabilities (mutasawiyat), where a sign indicates both a thing and its opposite, either directly or through a middle. He gives the example: 'So-and-so is standing at the head of the slain Zayd, freshly erected with his sword, so he is not his killer?' – this is true in most cases but sometimes false. He mentions that with an additional detail (like mention of blood), the implicit syllogism changes, making the total nine. He concludes that the rhetorical syllogism is used in ethical, natural, and political sciences.

Implicit syllogisms vary in certainty based on whether their omitted premise is necessary, probable, or equal in possibility.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Function of Signs in Syllogistic Figures
Definition and exemplification of signs in three syllogistic figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three syllogistic figures (shakl) as applied to signs (alama) and reconstruct the implicit syllogism for each.

Core Idea
A sign (alama) becomes a middle term that links subject and predicate, forming a syllogism in one of three figures.

The author defines a sign as a judgment that relates predicate to subject. Depending on the direction of the relation, the sign forms a syllogism in the first, second, or third figure. Recognizing the figure helps determine the validity and strength of the inference, especially when the sign is probabilistic.

Guided Path
1
Define the sign
The author says: the sign is a judgment such that either the predicate accompanies the sign and the sign does not accompany the subject, or the sign accompanies the subject and the predicate does not accompany it.
This creates a relation that can be turned into a syllogism.
2
First figure sign
If the predicate accompanies the sign and the sign accompanies the subject, then the first figure is formed. Example: 'The jurist is chaste because Zayd the jurist is chaste.' The middle term (sign) is Zayd, connecting jurist and chastity.
The reconstructed syllogism: Zayd is a jurist, Zayd is chaste, so every jurist is chaste.
3
Second figure sign
When the sign accompanies the subject but the predicate does not accompany the sign, it forms the second figure. Example: 'This woman has a swollen belly, so she is pregnant.' The swollen belly is the sign (middle term) that appears in both premises, but the predicate (pregnant) is not always accompanied by it.
The reconstructed syllogism: This woman has a swollen belly; pregnant women have swollen bellies; so this woman is pregnant (but with a caveat).
4
Third figure sign
The author mentions that the first of the two signs is made clear by the third figure. In the example, Zayd is used as a sign that all jurists are chaste. This involves an inference from a particular to a universal, typical of the third figure.
The reconstructed syllogism: Zayd is a jurist; Zayd is chaste; so some jurists are chaste (or every jurist is chaste, a leap).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يلزم أن تكون التفكيرات والضمائر عن ممكنات بالتساوي بل قد تكون عن ضروريات وعن أكثريات، والضمائر الموجودة في كل واحد منهما قد تكون من الصادقات أي من المحمودات الحقيقية، وقد تكون من الدلائل: فمثال ما يكون من المحمولات في الضروريات قولنا زيد عالم زكي النفس، والعالم الزكي النفس سعيد في الآخرة؛ وهذه المقدمة المحمودة قد تحذف وتستعمل قوتها. وإنما تصرّح بها مهملة؛ لئلا يكون المقول على الكل من حيث هو مقول على الكل مصرَّحًا به ؛ وهذا من جملة ما يكون تأليفه على نهج الشكل الأول ومثال ما هو من الدليل بالقسمة الخاصة قولنا هذه المرأة ولدت فهي المقتصة؛ فجعل الولادة دليلا على الاقتصاص وهو دليل صدق؛ فيلزم أن يكون معه أو أخص منه، ولذلك يكون على قوة الشكل الأول. وأما العلامة فهي حكم إما أن يكون المحمول يلزمه وهو لا يلزم الموضوع أو هو يلزم الموضوع، والمحمول لا يلزمه. فإنه إذا لزمه المحمول ويلزم هو الموضوع كان دليلا فانعقد الشكل الأول. فالعلامة الأولى منهما تتبين بالشكل الثالث كقولنا الفقيه عفيف لأن زيدًا الفقيه عفيف؛ والصدق في هذا الكلام أن يقال إن زيدًا فقيه وزيدًا عفيف فكل فقيه عفيف. فيكون زيد علامة لكون الفقيه عفيفاً لكن الفقيه يلزم زيدًا أو زيد لا يلزم الفقيه حتى يكون كل فقیه زيدا. والعلامة الثانية تكون من الشكل الثاني في مثل قولهم هذه منتفخة البطن فهي إذن حبلى؛ والصدق في هذا الكلام أن يقال هذه منتفخة البطن، والحبلى منتفخة البطن فيكون انتفاح البطن علامة الحبل لكن الانتفاخ قد يوجد بدون الحبل. وأما أمثلة هذه في الأكثريات فالكبرى في القياس منها محمودة بالحقيقة لكنها لا تكون صادقة في الكل بل في الأكثر من الأشخاص أو الأكثر من الاعتبارات مثل قولهم زيد كاف الأذى فهو محبوب؛ ويكون الدليل الأكثري مثل قولهم زيد محموم فهو إذن سريع النبض؛ وهذا يسمى دليل الأولى والأشبه عند قوم. وأما العلامة من الشكل الثاني فأن يقال زيد سريع النبض مثلاً فهو محموم. وأما العلامة فيها من الشكل الثالث فمثل أن يقال مثلا الشجعان لا يبخلون لأن علياً لا يبخل ؛ فهذه ثمانية وجوه من الضمائر عن الضروريات والأكثريات. وأما الكائنة عن المتساويات فهي التي يكون فيها المعنى علامة للشيء ولنقيضه جميعًا لكنه يكون علامة لأحدهما بنفسه، وللنقيض بواسطة، أو يكون علامة للأمرين بواسطتين. ولا بد من تلويح أكثرية فيه لا بحسب الحقيقة بل بحسب الظنِّ وإلا لا يكون هناك مثل النفس ومثال هذا قول القائل فلان قائم على رأس زيد القتيل الطري منتصب بسيفه فليس هو يقابله؛ وهذا مما يصدق في الأكثر، ويكذب في الأقل. وقد يراد في العلامة شيء كما يقال فلان قائم على رأس زيد القتيل الطري المختون دمه وكذا وكذا؛ وحينئذ يكون الضمير غير الضمير الذي مرَّ ذكره فتكون الضمائر تسعة، وعلى هذا فاعتبر فإن القياس الخطابي يستعمل في العلوم الخلقية والطبيعية والسياسية وغير ذلك.
And it is not necessary that the thoughts and implicit syllogisms be about possibilities equally; rather, they may be about necessities and about probabilities. The implicit syllogisms present in each of them may be from true premises, i.e., from real accepted premises, and may be from indications. An example of what is from the predicated in necessities is our saying: 'Zayd is a scholar, pure of soul, and the scholar pure of soul is happy in the afterlife.' This accepted premise may be omitted and its force used. It is explicitly stated as a universal; so that the statement about all insofar as it is about all is not explicitly stated. This is among what has its composition on the pattern of the first figure. And an example of what is from the indication by specific division is our saying: 'This woman gave birth, so she is the one who is subject to retaliation (muqtassa).' So he made giving birth an indication of retaliation, and it is an indication of truth. So it must be that it is with it or more specific than it, and therefore it has the force of the first figure. As for the sign (al-'alama), it is a judgment such that either the predicate accompanies it and it does not accompany the subject, or it accompanies the subject and the predicate does not accompany it. For if the predicate accompanies it and it accompanies the subject, it becomes an indication and the first figure is formed. The first of these two signs is made clear by the third figure, as in our saying: 'The jurist is chaste because Zayd the jurist is chaste.' The truth in this statement is to say: 'Zayd is a jurist and Zayd is chaste, so every jurist is chaste.' So Zayd becomes a sign that the jurist is chaste, but the jurist accompanies Zayd, or Zayd does not accompany the jurist so that every jurist is Zayd. The second sign is from the second figure in such as their saying: 'This woman has a swollen belly, so she is pregnant.' The truth in this statement is to say: 'This woman has a swollen belly, and the pregnant woman has a swollen belly,' so swollen belly is a sign of pregnancy, but swelling may exist without pregnancy. As for examples of these in probabilities, the major premise in the syllogism is truly accepted but is not true in all cases, rather in most persons or most considerations, like their saying: 'Zayd is light in harm, so he is loved.' And the probable indication is like their saying: 'Zayd is feverish, so he has a rapid pulse.' This is called 'indication of the first and more appropriate' by some people. As for the sign from the second figure, it is that it is said: 'Zayd has a rapid pulse, for example, so he is feverish.' As for the sign from the third figure, it is like saying, for example: 'Brave men are not stingy because 'Ali is not stingy.' These are eight kinds of implicit syllogisms from necessities and probabilities. As for those arising from equal probabilities, they are those in which the meaning is a sign for the thing and its opposite together, but it is a sign for one of them by itself and for the opposite through a middle, or it is a sign for both through two middles. There must be a suggestion of probability in it, not according to reality but according to opinion; otherwise there would be no such thing as the soul. And an example of this is a speaker's saying: 'So-and-so is standing at the head of the slain Zayd, freshly erected with his sword, so he is not his killer?' This is true in most cases and false in the minority. And something may be intended in the sign, as it is said: 'So-and-so is standing at the head of the slain Zayd, his blood freshly flowing, etc.' Then the implicit syllogism is different from the one previously mentioned, making the implicit syllogisms nine. On this basis, reflect, for the rhetorical syllogism is used in the ethical, natural, political sciences, and others.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Rhetorical Syllogisms from Probable and Equal Premises
Examples of probable and equal implicit syllogisms in rhetoric

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify rhetorical syllogisms based on probable (akthari) and equal (mutasawi) premises, and explain how they differ from necessary syllogisms.

Core Idea
Rhetorical syllogisms rely on signs that are true in most cases or ambiguous, aiming at persuasion rather than certainty.

Not all syllogisms aim for demonstrative certainty. In rhetoric and dialectic, we often use premises that are probable (true in most cases) or equal (equally likely true or false). The author provides examples of each and explains how they are used in ethical, natural, and political sciences.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish probable from equal premises
Probable premises (akthariyyat) are true in most instances, like 'whoever is light in harm is loved.' Equal premises (mutasawiyat) are signs that could apply to both a thing and its opposite, requiring a suggestion of probability to tip the scale.
2
Examine probable examples
The author gives 'Zayd is light in harm, so he is loved' (major premise true in most cases) and 'Zayd is feverish, so he has a rapid pulse' (a sign from the first figure, probabilistic).
These are called 'indication of the first and more appropriate' by some.
3
Examine equal examples
For equal premises, the sign indicates both the thing and its opposite. Example: 'So-and-so is standing at the head of the slain Zayd with a sword, so he is not the killer?' This suggests innocence, but could be false if the killer is standing there.
The sign is ambiguous; a suggestion of probability is needed.
4
Understand the ninth type
When an additional detail is added (e.g., mention of blood), the sign becomes more specific, creating a ninth type of implicit syllogism.
The author counts eight types from necessities and probabilities, and one from equals, making nine.

As for the sign from the second figure, it is that it is said: 'Zayd has a rapid pulse, for example, so he is feverish.' As for the sign from the third figure, it is like saying, for example: 'Brave men are not stingy because 'Ali is not stingy.' These are eight kinds of implicit syllogisms from necessities and probabilities. As for those arising from equal probabilities, they are those in which the meaning is a sign for the thing and its opposite together, but it is a sign for one of them by itself and for the opposite through a middle, or it is a sign for both through two middles. There must be a suggestion of probability in it, not according to reality but according to opinion; otherwise there would be no such thing as the soul. And an example of this is a speaker's saying: 'So-and-so is standing at the head of the slain Zayd, freshly erected with his sword, so he is not his killer?' This is true in most cases and false in the minority. And something may be intended in the sign, as it is said: 'So-and-so is standing at the head of the slain Zayd, his blood freshly flowing, etc.' Then the implicit syllogism is different from the one previously mentioned, making the implicit syllogisms nine. On this basis, reflect, for the rhetorical syllogism is used in the ethical, natural, political sciences, and others.
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This is a syllogistic passage that categorizes implicit syllogisms (al-damair) according to the modality of their premises: necessary (daruriyyat), probable (akthariyyat), and equal (mutasawiyat). The author begins by stating that thoughts (tafkirat) and implicit syllogisms (damair) are not limited to equal possibilities but include necessary and probable premises. Each type may use true accepted premises (sadiqat) or indications (dala'il). He provides examples: for necessary premises, 'Zayd is a scholar, pure of soul, and the scholar pure of soul is happy in the afterlife' – the major premise is omitted but its force used. For indication by specific division (qismat khas), he gives the example of a woman giving birth indicating retaliation (qisas). He then defines the 'sign' (al-'alama) as a relation where the predicate accompanies the sign and the sign accompanies the subject, forming the first figure. Alternatively, the sign may not accompany the subject, leading to other figures. He explains two types: one made clear by the third figure (e.g., 'The jurist is chaste because Zayd the jurist is chaste' – Zayd is a sign for the connection between jurist and chastity), and another by the second figure (e.g., 'This woman has a swollen belly, so she is pregnant' – swollen belly is a sign of pregnancy, but not exclusive). Then he discusses probable examples: major premises that are true in most cases (akthari), like 'Zayd is light in harm, so he is loved.' He further distinguishes signs in the second figure (rapid pulse indicates fever) and third figure (brave men are not stingy because 'Ali is not stingy). He counts eight types of damair from necessities and probabilities. Next, he addresses implicit syllogisms from equal probabilities (mutasawiyat), where a sign indicates both a thing and its opposite, either directly or through a middle. He gives the example: 'So-and-so is standing at the head of the slain Zayd, freshly erected with his sword, so he is not his killer?' – this is true in most cases but sometimes false. He mentions that with an additional detail (like mention of blood), the implicit syllogism changes, making the total nine. He concludes that the rhetorical syllogism is used in ethical, natural, and political sciences.

Key Terms
الأكثريات
المتساويات
دليل الأولى والأشبه
القياس الخطابي

Key Takeaway
Rhetorical syllogisms use probabilistic and ambiguous signs to achieve persuasion in ethical, natural, and political reasoning.

The Three Types of Signs in Syllogisms
Author classifies signs into three types and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given sign (علامة) as necessary, probable, or equal, based on its relationship to the indicated thing.

Core Idea
Signs in syllogism vary from certain to probable to ambiguous; each has a distinct logical function.

This passage from a logical text classifies signs (علامات) used in syllogisms into three categories: necessary signs that indicate with certainty, probable signs that hold in most cases, and equal signs that can indicate both a thing and its opposite. Understanding this classification is key to evaluating the strength of arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sign in the syllogism
Look for the term that serves as evidence for the conclusion. For example, in 'Zayd is a faqih and Zayd is chaste, so every faqih is chaste,' the sign is Zayd.
The sign is often the middle term.
2
Determine the relationship between sign and indicated
Is the connection necessary (always holds), probable (holds most often), or equal (the sign can indicate both the thing and its opposite)?
The author gives examples of each.
3
Classify the sign as ضروري, أكثري, or متساوي
Use the author's criteria: necessary signs yield certainty, probable signs yield likelihood, equal signs yield ambiguity.
The passage describes each category.
4
Check the author's example
Verify your classification against the examples: swollen belly as probable, standing over corpse as equal, etc.
This reinforces the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام أن يقال إن زيدًا فقيه وزيدًا عفيف فكل فقيه عفيف. فيكون زيد علامة لكون الفقيه عفيفاً لكن الفقيه يلزم زيدًا أو زيد لا يلزم الفقيه حتى يكون كل فقيه زيدا. والعلامة الثانية تكون من الشكل الثاني في مثل قولهم هذه منتفخة البطن فهي إذن حبلى؛ والصدق في هذا الكلام أن يقال هذه منتفخة البطن، والحبلى منتفخة البطن فيكون انتفاح البطن علامة الحبل لكن الانتفاخ قد يوجد بدون الحبل. وأما أمثلة هذه في الأكثريات فالكبرى في القياس منها محمودة بالحقيقة لكنها لا تكون صادقة في الكل بل في الأكثر من الأشخاص أو الأكثر من الاعتبارات مثل قولهم زيد كاف الأذى فهو محبوب؛ ويكون الدليل الأكثري مثل قولهم زيد محموم فهو إذن سريع النبض؛ وهذا يسمى دليل الأولى والأشبه عند قوم. وأما العلامة من الشكل الثاني فأن يقال زيد سريع النبض مثلاً فهو محموم. وأما العلامة فيها من الشكل الثالث فمثل أن يقال مثلا الشجعان لا يبخلون لأن علياً لا يبخل ؛ فهذه ثمانية وجوه من الضمائر عن الضروريات والأكثريات. وأما الكائنة عن المتساويات فهي التي يكون فيها المعنى علامة للشيء ولنقيضه جميعًا لكنه يكون علامة لأحدهما بنفسه، وللنقيض بواسطة، أو يكون علامة للأمرين بواسطتين. ولا بد من تلويح أكثرية فيه لا بحسب الحقيقة بل بحسب الظنِّ وإلا لا يكون هناك مثل النفس ومثال هذا قول القائل فلان قائم على رأس زيد القتيل الطري منتصب بسيفه فليس هو يقابله؛ وهذا مما يصدق في الأكثر، ويكذب في الأقل. وقد يراد في العلامة شيء كما يقال فلان قائم على رأس زيد القتيل الطري المختون دمه وكذا وكذا؛ وحينئذ يكون الضمير غير الضمير الذي مرَّ ذكره فتكون الضمائر تسعة، وعلى هذا فاعتبر فإن القياس الخطابي يستعمل في العلوم الخلقية والطبيعية والسياسية وغير ذلك.
The statement is that it is said: Zayd is a faqih and Zayd is chaste, so every faqih is chaste. So Zayd is a sign that the faqih is chaste, but the faqih is implied by Zayd, or Zayd does not imply the faqih such that every faqih is Zayd. The second sign is from the second figure, like their saying: This one has a swollen belly, so she is pregnant. The truth in this statement is that this one has a swollen belly, and the pregnant one has a swollen belly, so the swelling is a sign of pregnancy, but swelling may exist without pregnancy. As for examples of this in probables, the major premise in the syllogism from them is praiseworthy in reality but it is not true in all, but in most individuals or most considerations, like their saying: Zayd is restrained in harm, so he is beloved. The probable indication is like their saying: Zayd has a fever, so he has a fast pulse; this is called the indication of priority and similarity by some. As for the sign from the second figure, it is said: Zayd has a fast pulse for example, so he has a fever. As for the sign from the third figure, it is like saying: The brave do not withhold, because Ali does not withhold. So these are eight types of implied premises from necessities and probables. As for the ones from equals, they are those in which the meaning is a sign for the thing and its opposite together, but it is a sign for one of them directly, and for the opposite through mediation, or it is a sign for both through two mediations. It must involve a probability not in reality but in estimation, otherwise there would be no such thing as the soul. An example is someone saying: So-and-so is standing over the corpse of Zayd, freshly killed, standing with his sword, so he is not the one who confronted him. This is true in most cases and false in a few. And something may be intended in the sign as it is said: So-and-so is standing over the corpse of Zayd, freshly killed, his blood stained, etc. Then the implied premise is different from the implied premise that passed, so the implied premises become nine. On this, reflect, for the rhetorical syllogism is used in ethical, natural, political sciences, and others.
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage classifies signs (علامات) used in syllogisms and counts the resulting implied premises (ضمائر). The author begins with an example of a sign in the first figure, showing that Zayd serves as a sign for the faqih being chaste, but the relationship is not reversible. Then examples from the second and third figures are given: the swollen belly as a sign of pregnancy (second figure), and bravery as a sign of not being miserly (third figure). The author distinguishes between necessary signs (ضروريات) and probable signs (أكثريات), noting that probable signs are true in most cases but not all, like fever indicating fast pulse. Eight types of implied premises arise from these. Then a third category is introduced: signs from equals (متساويات), where the sign can indicate both the thing and its opposite, directly or indirectly, and involves probability in estimation. An example is given about a person standing over a corpse, which in most cases indicates guilt but not always. The author shows that this adds another implied premise, making the total nine. The passage concludes by noting that rhetorical syllogism applies in various sciences. Throughout, the author emphasizes that these classifications are based on the nature of the sign's relationship to what it indicates.

Counting Implied Premises (ضمائر) in Sign-Based Syllogisms
Author counts implied premises from signs, starting with eight and adding a ninth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to count the implied premises (ضمائر) in a sign-based syllogism, distinguishing between eight (from necessary and probable signs) and nine (when including equal signs).

Core Idea
The number of implied premises expands from eight to nine when including signs that indicate both sides.

The author not only classifies signs but also counts the implied premises (ضمائر) that result from using these signs in syllogisms. He states that from necessary and probable signs, we have eight types of implied premises, and with the addition of equal signs, the number becomes nine. This counting helps us understand the variety of unstated assumptions in rhetorical arguments.

Guided Path
1
List the eight types from the passage
Identify the eight faces (وجوه) of implied premises mentioned for necessary and probable signs. The passage implies they come from the three syllogistic figures with variations.
The author does not list them explicitly, but references the three figures.
2
Identify the ninth type from equal signs
Recognize that the ninth implied premise arises from the unique nature of equal signs, which can indicate both a thing and its opposite.
This is an addition to the original eight.
3
Relate each type to a figure
Notice that the examples are drawn from the first, second, and third figures of syllogism, each yielding different forms of sign arguments.
The author gives examples for each figure.
4
Count the total
Verify that the total number of implied premises starts at eight and becomes nine when the category of equal signs is included.
This is explicitly stated in the passage.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Probable Syllogism (al-Lāḥiq)
Author explains the probable syllogism and clarifies the deviant syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the probable syllogism (al-lāḥiq) and distinguish it from valid categorical syllogisms based on its structure.

Core Idea
The probable syllogism uses two affirmatives in the second figure, yielding only probability, not certainty.

This lesson introduces a rhetorical syllogism called 'bringing the consequent' (al-lāḥiq). The author explains that it is a probable syllogism because it consists of two affirmative premises in the second figure. He further clarifies Aristotle's term 'mā'il' (deviant), showing that the deviation is in usage, not logical form. Understanding this helps distinguish rhetorical arguments from demonstrative proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of al-lāḥiq
Find the two premises: both are affirmative, and the middle term is the predicate in both (second figure). The conclusion is also affirmative.
Example: 'Every pious person is blessed; every generous person is pious' leads to 'Every generous person is blessed' – but the middle term is not necessarily distributed.
2
Recognize why it is probable
In the second figure, two affirmatives do not yield a necessary conclusion. The syllogism is only probable because the middle term may not be universal.
Compare with first figure, which guarantees certainty if premises are true.
3
Interpret Aristotle's 'deviant' (mā'il)
The author clarifies that 'deviant' does not mean a distorted figure, but deviation from the normal syllogistic direction – premises are expressed as questions or commands in rhetoric.
This shows the flexibility of logical forms in actual discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونوع آخر أن يأتي باللاحق فإن هذا بالحقيقة قياس مظنون؛ لأنه من الموجبتين في الشكل الثاني. ومن هنا نعلم أن المعلّم الأول لما ذكر في كلامه المائل المنحرف لم يعن به المائل من جهة وضع الحدود، والمحرف عن الشكل المنتج في نفسه بل عنى بالمائل ما حرف عن الجهة القياسية؛ وذلك لأن كثيرًا من المقدّمات تستعمل في الخطابة لا على أنها مقدّمات بل على أنها مسائل أو تعجبات أو أوامر. وبالجملة فالتفكرات إما أن تكون من الواجبات وهي الآراء المحمودة، وإما أن تكون من البرهانات لا من حيث يصحح بها المطلوب نفسه بل بأن ينتقل منها إلى حكم كلي ثمَّ يضع منه ضمير. وإما أن تكون من الدلائل؛ وذلك على مثال الشكل الأول وهو اضطراري جدًّا، وإما من الرسوم والعلامات؛ إما من الكلية على سبيل الشكل الثاني، وإما من الجزئية على سبيل الشكل الثالث على ما عرف، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Another type is that one brings the consequent; for this is in reality a probable syllogism, because it is from two affirmatives in the second figure. From here we know that when the First Teacher mentioned in his speech the 'deviant and distorted,' he did not mean by 'deviant' the deviation in terms of placing the limits, nor the distorted from the productive figure in itself; rather, he meant by 'deviant' what is distorted from the syllogistic direction. This is because many premises are used in rhetoric not as premises but as questions, exclamations, or commands. In summary, thoughts are either from obligations, which are praiseworthy opinions; or from demonstrations, not in that they validate the desired conclusion itself but in that one moves from them to a universal judgment and then places a pronoun from it; or from indications on the model of the first figure, which is very necessary; or from signs and marks; either from the universal by way of the second figure, or from the particular by way of the third figure, as known. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage introduces another type of rhetorical argument: bringing the consequent (al-lāḥiq), which is actually a probable syllogism because it consists of two affirmative premises in the second figure. The author then clarifies Aristotle's term 'mā'il' (deviant): he did not mean deviation in terms of definition or distorted from the productive figure, but rather deviation from the syllogistic direction, since many rhetorical premises are not stated as premises but as questions, exclamations, or commands. Finally, the author summarizes the classification of thoughts (tafakkurāt): they can be from obligations (wājibāt, i.e., praiseworthy opinions), from demonstrations (burhānāt) that lead to a universal judgment, from indications (dala'il) modeled on the first figure, or from signs (rusūm wa 'alāmāt) either universally (second figure) or particularly (third figure).

Key Terms
اللاحق
المائل
الشكل الثاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It uses two negative premises

C) It uses a conditional premise
D) It is a syllogism in the first figure

Key Takeaway
The probable syllogism (al-lāḥiq) uses two affirmatives in the second figure, yielding only probability, not certainty.

Classification of Thoughts into Four Types
Author classifies thoughts into four types with syllogistic forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify arguments into obligations, demonstrations, indications, and signs based on their syllogistic form and epistemic role.

Core Idea
Thoughts are classified by their syllogistic structure: indications are first figure, signs are second or third figure, demonstrations yield universal judgments, and obligations are praiseworthy opinions.

After discussing the probable syllogism, the author provides a comprehensive classification of all thoughts (tafakkurāt). This helps identify the logical type of any argument and its level of certainty. The classification ties directly to the figures of the categorical syllogism, showing how different argument forms serve different rhetorical and demonstrative purposes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
List obligations (praiseworthy opinions), demonstrations (leading to universal judgment), indications (first figure), and signs (second or third figure).
2
Understand the structure of each
Indications follow the first figure (necessary conclusion if premises true). Signs are universal via second figure (probable) or particular via third figure (partial).
Demonstrations involve moving to a universal judgment and then using that as a premise.
3
Apply to real arguments
Take a theological argument (e.g., from creation to Creator) and identify whether it is an indication (first figure) or a sign (second figure).
Example: 'Every contingent thing has a cause; the world is contingent; therefore the world has a cause' is an indication (first figure).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونوع آخر أن يأتي باللاحق فإن هذا بالحقيقة قياس مظنون؛ لأنه من الموجبتين في الشكل الثاني. ومن هنا نعلم أن المعلّم الأول لما ذكر في كلامه المائل المنحرف لم يعن به المائل من جهة وضع الحدود، والمحرف عن الشكل المنتج في نفسه بل عنى بالمائل ما حرف عن الجهة القياسية؛ وذلك لأن كثيرًا من المقدّمات تستعمل في الخطابة لا على أنها مقدّمات بل على أنها مسائل أو تعجبات أو أوامر. وبالجملة فالتفكرات إما أن تكون من الواجبات وهي الآراء المحمودة، وإما أن تكون من البرهانات لا من حيث يصحح بها المطلوب نفسه بل بأن ينتقل منها إلى حكم كلي ثمَّ يضع منه ضمير. وإما أن تكون من الدلائل؛ وذلك على مثال الشكل الأول وهو اضطراري جدًّا، وإما من الرسوم والعلامات؛ إما من الكلية على سبيل الشكل الثاني، وإما من الجزئية على سبيل الشكل الثالث على ما عرف، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Another type is that one brings the consequent; for this is in reality a probable syllogism, because it is from two affirmatives in the second figure. From here we know that when the First Teacher mentioned in his speech the 'deviant and distorted,' he did not mean by 'deviant' the deviation in terms of placing the limits, nor the distorted from the productive figure in itself; rather, he meant by 'deviant' what is distorted from the syllogistic direction. This is because many premises are used in rhetoric not as premises but as questions, exclamations, or commands. In summary, thoughts are either from obligations, which are praiseworthy opinions; or from demonstrations, not in that they validate the desired conclusion itself but in that one moves from them to a universal judgment and then places a pronoun from it; or from indications on the model of the first figure, which is very necessary; or from signs and marks; either from the universal by way of the second figure, or from the particular by way of the third figure, as known. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage introduces another type of rhetorical argument: bringing the consequent (al-lāḥiq), which is actually a probable syllogism because it consists of two affirmative premises in the second figure. The author then clarifies Aristotle's term 'mā'il' (deviant): he did not mean deviation in terms of definition or distorted from the productive figure, but rather deviation from the syllogistic direction, since many rhetorical premises are not stated as premises but as questions, exclamations, or commands. Finally, the author summarizes the classification of thoughts (tafakkurāt): they can be from obligations (wājibāt, i.e., praiseworthy opinions), from demonstrations (burhānāt) that lead to a universal judgment, from indications (dala'il) modeled on the first figure, or from signs (rusūm wa 'alāmāt) either universally (second figure) or particularly (third figure).

البرهانات
الدلائل
الرسوم

Key Takeaway
Arguments are classified into four types based on syllogistic form: indications are first figure, signs are second or third figure.

Four Types of Rhetorical Reasoning and Their Figures
Author classifies probable reasoning into four types with figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify rhetorical reasoning into four types and identify the syllogistic figure associated with each type.

Core Idea
Rhetorical reasoning adapts syllogistic figures for persuasion, not certainty.

This passage from a classical logic text concludes a discussion on probable argumentation used in rhetoric. The author provides a clear taxonomy of 'reflections' (تفكرات) that rhetoricians employ, each linked to a specific syllogistic figure. Understanding this taxonomy helps you see how the same logical structures used in demonstration can be repurposed for persuasive effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of reflections
Find in the Arabic the enumeration: الواجبات (obligatory opinions), البرهانات (demonstrations), الدلائل (indications), الرسوم والعلامات (marks/signs). Note that البرهانات here is used loosely, not for certain demonstration.
البرهان here means moving from a universal judgment to a conditional syllogism.
2
Match each type to its figure
Indications (دلائل) follow the first figure, which is described as 'اضطراري' (compelling). Marks/signs (رسوم وعلامات) use the second figure when universal, and the third figure when particular.
The first figure is considered necessary because it is the most natural and self-evident.
3
Understand the role of the second figure
The author gives an example of a probable syllogism in the second figure: both premises are affirmative. This is typical of rhetorical reasoning where the middle term is predicate in both premises, leading to a probable conclusion.
Second figure with two affirmatives is valid but yields only a probable conclusion.
4
Apply to a real example
Consider a rhetorical argument: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; wine is an intoxicant; therefore wine is forbidden.' This is a first-figure indication. Now modify it: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every forbidden thing is harmful; so wine is harmful' – this would be second figure with two universal affirmatives (Barbara) but with a different middle term.
The figure changes based on the position of the middle term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعظم صنعه، وهذا نوع من الاحتجاج المظنون لأن الحاكم إذا كان كون الأمر ولا كونه مشكلا لديه لا يتضح له فعُومل ما ذكرنا فيشتغل عن استثبات الحال وانتقل إلى اعتبار ما يخاطب به، أو يتراءى لديه فلم يلبث أن يصدق. فهكذا يجب أن يفهم هذا الموضع، ونوع آخر أن يأتي باللاحق فإن هذا بالحقيقة قياس مظنون؛ لأنه من الموجبتين في الشكل الثاني. ومن هنا نعلم أن المعلّم الأول لما ذكر في كلامه المائل المنحرف لم يعن به المائل من جهة وضع الحدود، والمحرف عن الشكل المنتج في نفسه بل عنى بالمائل ما حرف عن الجهة القياسية؛ وذلك لأن كثيرًا من المقدّمات تستعمل في الخطابة لا على أنها مقدّمات بل على أنها مسائل أو تعجبات أو أوامر. وبالجملة فالتفكرات إما أن تكون من الواجبات وهي الآراء المحمودة، وإما أن تكون من البرهانات لا من حيث يصحح بها المطلوب نفسه بل بأن ينتقل منها إلى حكم كلي ثمَّ يضع منه ضمير. وإما أن تكون من الدلائل؛ وذلك على مثال الشكل الأول وهو اضطراري جدًّا، وإما من الرسوم والعلامات؛ إما من الكلية على سبيل الشكل الثاني، وإما من الجزئية على سبيل الشكل الثالث على ما عرف، والله أعلم بالصواب.
And its construction is great, and this is a type of probable argumentation because when the judge finds the existence or non-existence of the matter problematic for him and unclear, he is treated with what we mentioned so that he becomes occupied with verifying the situation and turns to considering what he is addressed with, or it appears to him, and soon he believes. Thus this place must be understood. Another type is to bring the consequent, for this is in reality a probable syllogism because it is from two affirmative premises in the second figure. From here we know that when the First Teacher mentioned in his speech the 'inclined' and 'deviated', he did not mean the inclined from the aspect of placing the limits, nor the deviated from the figure that is productive in itself, but rather he meant by 'inclined' what is deviated from the syllogistic aspect. That is because many premises are used in rhetoric not as premises but as questions, expressions of wonder, or commands. In summary, the reflections are either from the obligatory (i.e., the praised opinions), or from demonstrations not in the sense of validating the desired conclusion itself but in the sense of moving from them to a universal judgment and then positing from it a conditional [syllogism]. Or they are from indications, which are on the model of the first figure, which is extremely necessary. Or they are from marks and signs, either from the universal on the way of the second figure, or from the particular on the way of the third figure, as has been known. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage concludes a discussion on probable reasoning (الاحتجاج المظنون) used in rhetoric. The author first describes a psychological mechanism: when a judge is uncertain, the use of certain rhetorical devices distracts him from verification and leads him to belief. Then he clarifies a technical point: the 'inclined' and 'deviated' speech attributed to Aristotle (the First Teacher) does not refer to distorted syllogistic forms but to premises used in non-syllogistic ways, such as questions or commands. The passage then provides a classification of 'reflections' (تفكرات): (1) الواجبات, i.e., accepted opinions; (2) البرهانات, which here mean reasoning that moves from a universal judgment to a conditional syllogism, not strict demonstration; (3) الدلائل, which follow the first figure (necessary); (4) الرسوم والعلامات (marks and signs) which follow the second figure when universal, and the third figure when particular. This taxonomy shows how rhetorical reasoning adapts the standard syllogistic figures for persuasive purposes, using probable premises rather than certain ones.

Key Terms
احتجاج مظنون
برهان (as used here)
شكل ثان
رسم وعلامة

Key Takeaway
Rhetorical reasoning employs three syllogistic figures: first for indications, second for universal signs, third for particular signs.

Aristotle's 'Inclined' Speech: Non-Syllogistic Premises in Rhetoric
Author clarifies Aristotle's 'inclined' speech as non-syllogistic use of premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain what Aristotle meant by 'inclined' and 'deviated' speech and distinguish it from invalid syllogistic forms.

Core Idea
Premises in rhetoric are often used as questions or commands, not as logical propositions.

In this passage, the author addresses a common misinterpretation of Aristotle's terminology. When Aristotle described certain rhetorical speeches as 'inclined' or 'deviated', he was not talking about logically invalid syllogisms. Instead, he was referring to the fact that premises in rhetoric are often not stated as straightforward declarations but as questions, exclamations, or commands. This insight is crucial for understanding how rhetoric operates differently from strict logic.

Guided Path
1
Find the phrase 'المائل المنحرف' in the Arabic
This phrase occurs in the middle of the passage. It refers to Aristotle's description of some rhetorical speeches. The author immediately clarifies its meaning.
The author explicitly rejects an interpretation related to формальные defects.
2
Understand what the author denies
The author says the 'inclined' does NOT mean deviation in the placement of limits (حدود) and 'deviated' does NOT mean deviation from a productive figure. So it's not about invalid moods or figures.
This shows the author is distinguishing between logical form and rhetorical function.
3
Identify the author's positive interpretation
The author states: 'بل عنى بالمائل ما حرف عن الجهة القياسية' – rather, he meant by 'inclined' what is turned away from the syllogistic aspect, i.e., premises used as questions, expressions of wonder, or commands.
This is a pragmatic shift: the same words can function differently in rhetorical context.
4
Connect to the psychological mechanism
Earlier, the author described how rhetorical devices distract a listener from verification. Using non-premise speech acts (like commands) can influence belief without going through rational deduction.
This explains why rhetoric is effective even without strict logical form.
